# QA Results for quiz-5-fixed.md

## Q01

### Grounding

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available. <!-- correct -->

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she was at fault in creating the situation by choosing to operate a shelter in a building that was subject to condemnation, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the legislature has already addressed the specific situation by enacting the building code and condemnation procedures, which preempts any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils, not coercion by another person. Under the common law (Fact 6), Foot must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) she was not at fault in creating the emergency. Twelve lives threatened by winter exposure clearly outweigh the regulatory harm of occupying a condemned building. The threat is imminent (winter cold is present and immediate). No shelter beds are available (no legal alternative). And Foot did not create the emergency -- she was operating a lawful shelter until the city condemned the building. Option (b) misidentifies the defense: duress requires coercion by another person directing a threat at the defendant, not a natural emergency -- the false proposition is that a natural condition (freezing weather) can constitute the "threat" element of duress. Option (c) applies the MPC proportionality standard ("greater than"), but Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, which requires "clearly outweigh" -- the false proposition is that the MPC standard governs. Option (d) misstates the fault element -- Foot did not create the emergency (the condemnation and winter weather created it); operating a shelter is not the kind of fault that bars necessity -- the false proposition is that operating a shelter in a building later condemned constitutes being "at fault in creating the emergency." Option (e) raises legislative preemption, which is a real doctrine (see *Oakland Cannabis*), but building codes are general safety regulations, not a legislative determination that occupying a condemned building is always worse than letting people freeze -- the false proposition is that a general building code constitutes the kind of specific legislative balancing that preempts necessity.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) CORRECT
(b) False claim: Duress applies to natural threats — wrong because duress requires coercion by another person, not natural conditions
(c) False claim: MPC proportionality standard governs — wrong because Fact 6 specifies common law for necessity
(d) False claim: Operating a shelter in a building later condemned = fault in creating the emergency — wrong because the emergency is the risk to life from cold, which Foot did not create
(e) False claim: Building codes constitute specific legislative preemption of necessity — wrong because general safety codes are not the kind of particularized legislative balancing that forecloses the defense
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement (CL "clearly outweigh" vs. MPC "greater than").

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — necessity, Ch 21, Refinement `necessity-proportionality-standard`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available. <!-- correct -->

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she was at fault in creating the situation by choosing to operate a shelter in a building that was subject to condemnation, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the legislature has already addressed the specific situation by enacting the building code and condemnation procedures, which preempts any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

Option (c) applies the MPC proportionality standard ("greater than"), but Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, which requires "clearly outweigh" -- the false proposition is that the MPC standard governs. Option (d) misstates the fault element -- Foot did not create the emergency (the condemnation and winter weather created it); operating a shelter is not the kind of fault that bars necessity -- the false proposition is that operating a shelter in a building later condemned constitutes being "at fault in creating the emergency." Option (e) raises legislative preemption, which is a real doctrine (see *Oakland Cannabis*), but building codes are general safety regulations, not a legislative determination that occupying a condemned building is always worse than letting people freeze -- the false proposition is that a general building code constitutes the kind of specific legislative balancing that preempts necessity.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available. <!-- correct -->

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she was at fault in creating the situation by choosing to operate a shelter in a building that was subject to condemnation, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the legislature has already addressed the specific situation by enacting the building code and condemnation procedures, which preempts any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils, not coercion by another person. Under the common law (Fact 6), Foot must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) she was not at fault in creating the emergency. Twelve lives threatened by winter exposure clearly outweigh the regulatory harm of occupying a condemned building. The threat is imminent (winter cold is present and immediate). No shelter beds are available (no legal alternative). And Foot did not create the emergency -- she was operating a lawful shelter until the city condemned the building. Option (b) misidentifies the defense: duress requires coercion by another person directing a threat at the defendant, not a natural emergency -- the false proposition is that a natural condition (freezing weather) can constitute the "threat" element of duress. Option (c) applies the MPC proportionality standard ("greater than"), but Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, which requires "clearly outweigh" -- the false proposition is that the MPC standard governs. Option (d) misstates the fault element -- Foot did not create the emergency (the condemnation and winter weather created it); operating a shelter is not the kind of fault that bars necessity -- the false proposition is that operating a shelter in a building later condemned constitutes being "at fault in creating the emergency." Option (e) raises legislative preemption, which is a real doctrine (see *Oakland Cannabis*), but building codes are general safety regulations, not a legislative determination that occupying a condemned building is always worse than letting people freeze -- the false proposition is that a general building code constitutes the kind of specific legislative balancing that preempts necessity.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available. <!-- correct -->

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she was at fault in creating the situation by choosing to operate a shelter in a building that was subject to condemnation, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the legislature has already addressed the specific situation by enacting the building code and condemnation procedures, which preempts any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils, not coercion by another person. Under the common law (Fact 6), Foot must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) she was not at fault in creating the emergency. Twelve lives threatened by winter exposure clearly outweigh the regulatory harm of occupying a condemned building. The threat is imminent (winter cold is present and immediate). No shelter beds are available (no legal alternative). And Foot did not create the emergency -- she was operating a lawful shelter until the city condemned the building. Option (b) misidentifies the defense: duress requires coercion by another person directing a threat at the defendant, not a natural emergency -- the false proposition is that a natural condition (freezing weather) can constitute the "threat" element of duress. Option (c) applies the MPC proportionality standard ("greater than"), but Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, which requires "clearly outweigh" -- the false proposition is that the MPC standard governs. Option (d) misstates the fault element -- Foot did not create the emergency (the condemnation and winter weather created it); operating a shelter is not the kind of fault that bars necessity -- the false proposition is that operating a shelter in a building later condemned constitutes being "at fault in creating the emergency." Option (e) raises legislative preemption, which is a real doctrine (see *Oakland Cannabis*), but building codes are general safety regulations, not a legislative determination that occupying a condemned building is always worse than letting people freeze -- the false proposition is that a general building code constitutes the kind of specific legislative balancing that preempts necessity.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) CORRECT
(b) False claim: Duress applies to natural threats — wrong because duress requires coercion by another person, not natural conditions
(c) False claim: MPC proportionality standard governs — wrong because Fact 6 specifies common law for necessity
(d) False claim: Operating a shelter in a building later condemned = fault in creating the emergency — wrong because the emergency is the risk to life from cold, which Foot did not create
(e) False claim: Building codes constitute specific legislative preemption of necessity — wrong because general safety codes are not the kind of particularized legislative balancing that forecloses the defense
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement (CL "clearly outweigh" vs. MPC "greater than").

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the necessity defense as the strongest available option. Under the common law standards mentioned in Fact 6, Foot meets all requirements: the threat of death from freezing is imminent, there are no legal alternatives because other shelters are full, and Foot did not create the weather-related emergency. The preservation of twelve lives "clearly outweighs" the technical regulatory violation of a condemnation order, providing a classic choice-of-evils justification.

(b) Argument-for: While duress is typically limited to human threats, some legal theorists argue that the same rationale—the "person of ordinary firmness" being unable to resist pressure—should apply to natural disasters or extreme environmental threats. If the weather is viewed as an "external force" that removes the defendant's volition to obey the building code, duress provides a compelling excuse defense. Foot was "compelled" by the threat of death to her residents, which any reasonable person would find irresistible.

(c) Argument-for: This option argues that the MPC’s "greater than" standard for necessity provides the "strongest" defense because it lowers the bar for proportionality compared to the common law's "clearly outweigh" standard. Even if Fact 6 mentions the common law, an advocate would argue for the application of MPC-style logic to ensure that human life is prioritized over regulatory compliance. The "greater than" threshold makes the defense significantly easier to establish in a courtroom setting.

(d) Argument-for: This option relies on the "fault" exception to necessity. At common law, a defendant who is responsible for bringing about the situation requiring the choice of evils is barred from the defense. One could argue that Foot's decision to continue operating a shelter in a building she knew was subject to condemnation created the crisis; had she moved earlier or found a different facility, the residents would not be in this legal predicament.

(e) Argument-for: This option invokes the doctrine that necessity is unavailable when the legislature has already performed the balancing of interests. The existence of building codes and specific condemnation procedures represents a legislative determination that "unsafe buildings" are a public harm that must be avoided. By enacting these laws without an exception for "emergency shelter," the legislature has arguably preempted the court from performing its own ad hoc balancing of harms.

Head-to-head: (a) is the most accurate because it correctly identifies both the defense (necessity) and the specific jurisdiction's legal standard (common law "clearly outweighs"). (b) is legally incorrect because duress categorically requires a human threat at common law. (c) is incorrect because it cites the MPC standard when the prompt specifies common law applies. (d) fails because Foot's "fault" in running a shelter is not the type of reckless or intentional creation of the *emergency* (the weather and bed shortage) required to bar the defense. (e) fails because general safety regulations like building codes are not the specific legislative balancing required to pre-empt a life-saving necessity defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter [under a duress defense]" — wrong because duress always requires a threat from another human being, not a natural condition.
- (c): "under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be 'greater than' the harm caused" — wrong because Fact 6 specifies that the common law applies, making the MPC standard legally irrelevant to this analysis.
- (d): "Foot... was at fault in creating the situation by choosing to operate a shelter in a building that was subject to condemnation" — wrong because operating a lawful business that becomes non-compliant due to a city order does not constitute the legal "fault" (usually criminal or negligent creation of the crisis) required to bar necessity.
- (e): "the legislature has already addressed the specific situation... which preempts any judicial balancing of harms" — wrong because a general building code is not a specific legislative determination that occupying a building is always worse than freezing to death.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available. <!-- correct -->

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she was at fault in creating the situation by choosing to operate a shelter in a building that was subject to condemnation, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the legislature has already addressed the specific situation by enacting the building code and condemnation procedures, which preempts any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils, not coercion by another person. Under the common law (Fact 6), Foot must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) she was not at fault in creating the emergency. Twelve lives threatened by winter exposure clearly outweigh the regulatory harm of occupying a condemned building. The threat is imminent (winter cold is present and immediate). No shelter beds are available (no legal alternative). And Foot did not create the emergency -- she was operating a lawful shelter until the city condemned the building. Option (b) misidentifies the defense: duress requires coercion by another person directing a threat at the defendant, not a natural emergency -- the false proposition is that a natural condition (freezing weather) can constitute the "threat" element of duress. Option (c) applies the MPC proportionality standard ("greater than"), but Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, which requires "clearly outweigh" -- the false proposition is that the MPC standard governs. Option (d) misstates the fault element -- Foot did not create the emergency (the condemnation and winter weather created it); operating a shelter is not the kind of fault that bars necessity -- the false proposition is that operating a shelter in a building later condemned constitutes being "at fault in creating the emergency." Option (e) raises legislative preemption, which is a real doctrine (see *Oakland Cannabis*), but building codes are general safety regulations, not a legislative determination that occupying a condemned building is always worse than letting people freeze -- the false proposition is that a general building code constitutes the kind of specific legislative balancing that preempts necessity.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) CORRECT
(b) False claim: Duress applies to natural threats — wrong because duress requires coercion by another person, not natural conditions
(c) False claim: MPC proportionality standard governs — wrong because Fact 6 specifies common law for necessity
(d) False claim: Operating a shelter in a building later condemned = fault in creating the emergency — wrong because the emergency is the risk to life from cold, which Foot did not create
(e) False claim: Building codes constitute specific legislative preemption of necessity — wrong because general safety codes are not the kind of particularized legislative balancing that forecloses the defense
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement (CL "clearly outweigh" vs. MPC "greater than").

<!-- argument-pass: SHOULD FIX
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies necessity as the applicable defense since the threat stems from natural forces (freezing weather) rather than human coercion. It accurately lists and applies the common law elements, including the strict "clearly outweighs" proportionality standard, the imminence of the threat, and the lack of legal alternatives. This aligns perfectly with the fact pattern and the jurisdiction's common law rules.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that Foot acted under extreme pressure that overcame her will. Under the MPC's objective standard for duress, a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the threat of twelve people freezing to death. Thus, duress provides a strong framework for excusing her conduct.
(c) Argument-for: The MPC's necessity standard is often the default analytical tool in criminal law classes. Under the MPC, the harm avoided must only be "greater than" the harm caused. Given that saving twelve lives is indisputably greater than a regulatory condemnation violation, necessity provides a strong defense under this modern standard.
(d) Argument-for: The common law bars the necessity defense if the defendant is at fault in creating the emergency. A student might argue that by choosing to operate a shelter in a building subject to condemnation, Foot created the unsafe conditions and the subsequent conflict between the condemnation order and the residents' need for shelter, thus rendering her at fault and foreclosing the defense.
(e) Argument-for: Necessity is a gap-filler that cannot override explicit legislative policy. A student could argue that building codes and condemnation procedures represent a deliberate legislative balancing of harms regarding building safety. By condemning the building, the city legally predetermined that the structure is too dangerous to occupy, preempting ad hoc judicial balancing.

Head-to-head:
Option (a) correctly identifies the defense and accurately applies the common law elements to the facts. Option (b) fails because duress requires coercion by a human actor, whereas the threat here is a natural condition (the weather). Option (c) is incorrect because it applies the MPC "greater than" standard, whereas Fact 6 specifies the common law applies. Option (d) relies on a misapplication of the "at fault" element, claiming Foot created the emergency; however, her conduct mitigated rather than created the actual emergency (freezing weather). Option (e) incorrectly elevates a general building code into a specific legislative preemption of the necessity defense. While (b) and (c) contain clear, objective legal errors, (d) and (e) currently rely on debatable applications of law to fact rather than explicitly locked false legal claims, which could make them ambiguous to top students.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her" — wrong because duress requires an unlawful threat of force by another human being, not natural circumstances like freezing weather.
- (c): "because under the MPC" — wrong because Fact 6 explicitly establishes that this jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC.
- (d): "she was at fault in creating the situation by choosing to operate a shelter" — wrong because operating a shelter does not constitute culpable creation of the weather emergency itself, but lacks absolute locking language.
- (e): "which preempts any judicial balancing of harms" — wrong because general building codes do not constitute the specific, particularized legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense, though the phrasing could be locked tighter.

Recommended fix: Add absolute words to (d) and (e) to strictly lock their falsity. Change (d) to: "...because she is automatically deemed at fault in creating the situation solely by choosing to operate..." Change (e) to: "...which categorically preempts any judicial balancing of harms in every jurisdiction."
-->

---

## Q02

### Grounding

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.

(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification -- the act itself is deemed right -- and any third party who assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->

(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict -- once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.

(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.

(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification -- the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor, but necessity is a justification. Option (c) relies on a false rule of strict accomplice liability; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach to. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element; it actually flows from the principal act being right. Option (e) reaches the right result but wrongly applies the MPC framework; Fact 6 specifies common law, and justification-sharing is a common law principle.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Slide 3 ("Justification: You made the right choice. Excuse: You had no real choice.").

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — justification-vs-excuse, Ch 18, Refinement `dl-justification-vs-excuse`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.

(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification -- the act itself is deemed right -- and any third party who assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->

(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict -- once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.

(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.

(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification -- the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor, but necessity is a justification. Option (c) relies on a false rule of strict accomplice liability; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach to. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element; it actually flows from the principal act being right. Option (e) reaches the right result but wrongly applies the MPC framework; Fact 6 specifies common law, and justification-sharing is a common law principle.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Slide 3 ("Justification: You made the right choice. Excuse: You had no real choice.").

<!-- audit: SHOULD FIX
check 1: pass
check 2: pass
check 3: SHOULD FIX. The explanation for (e) says it "wrongly applies the MPC framework" because the facts specify common law. However, (e) also contains a substantive misstatement of MPC law: it claims the MPC "extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices." It does not (see Ch 18 `dl-mpc-independent-conviction`); under the MPC, excuses remain personal and an accomplice can be independently convicted even if the principal is excused. The explanation implies (e)'s statement of MPC law is correct but inapplicable; it should clarify that (e) also substantively misstates the MPC.
check 4: pass (assuming the master fact pattern provides Facts 1 and 6).
check 5: pass
check 6: pass
check 7: SHOULD FIX. The question tests the interaction of necessity with derivative liability. The specific rule here perfectly aligns with the `dl-justification-vs-excuse` tag, which is located in Chapter 18 (Accomplice Liability). The tags currently only list Chapter 21. Chapter 18 must be added.
Recommended fix: Add Chapter 18 to the tags. Update the explanation for option (e) to note that it also substantively misstates MPC accomplice liability rules, since the MPC does not extend "all" affirmative defenses (like excuses) to accomplices.
-->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.

(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification -- the act itself is deemed right -- and any third party who assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->

(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict -- once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.

(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.

(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification -- the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor, but necessity is a justification. Option (c) relies on a false rule of strict accomplice liability; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach to. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element; it actually flows from the principal act being right. Option (e) reaches the right result but wrongly applies the MPC framework; Fact 6 specifies common law, and justification-sharing is a common law principle.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Slide 3 ("Justification: You made the right choice. Excuse: You had no real choice.").

<!-- edge-case-audit: CLEAN
1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: pass
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: pass
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
Recommended fix: n/a
-->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.

(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification -- the act itself is deemed right -- and any third party who assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->

(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict -- once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.

(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.

(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification -- the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor, but necessity is a justification. Option (c) relies on a false rule of strict accomplice liability; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach to. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element; it actually flows from the principal act being right. Option (e) reaches the right result but wrongly applies the MPC framework; Fact 6 specifies common law, and justification-sharing is a common law principle.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Slide 3 ("Justification: You made the right choice. Excuse: You had no real choice.").

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under certain theoretical frameworks, necessity is treated similarly to duress as an "excuse" based on the psychological pressure of a crisis, rather than an objective "justification." If necessity is an excuse, it is personal to the actor whose will was overborne or who faced the pressure, meaning it would not automatically shield accomplices. Volunteers would therefore need to establish their own independent defenses, such as showing they personally faced an immediate threat of harm that would constitute duress.

(b) Argument-for: Necessity is the paradigmatic justification defense in criminal law, where the defendant's conduct is seen as the objectively "right" or "lesser evil" choice. Because a justification renders the act itself lawful under the circumstances, there is no "crime" for an accomplice to assist in. Therefore, if Foot's conduct is justified, any party who assisted in that justified conduct is protected by the same justification, as the law cannot punish the assistance of a legally correct act.

(c) Argument-for: A proponent of this option might argue that accomplice liability focuses on the individual's choice to contribute to a prohibited outcome. In this view, if the volunteers provided the "aid" and had the "intent" to assist in the unauthorized operation of a shelter, their liability is triggered by their own actions. If the common law prioritized individual accountability over derivative defense rules, the volunteers could be held liable for their own knowing participation in the standoff, regardless of the principal's personal defense.

(d) Argument-for: Necessity requires a showing that there was "no reasonable legal alternative." A volunteer who chose to join the standoff might have had many legal alternatives (such as staying home or protesting legally) that the principal (Foot) did not have while managing the crisis. This argument suggests that for a justification to apply to a third party, that party must personally satisfy the objective requirements of the defense, ensuring that only those truly forced by circumstance are shielded from liability.

(e) Argument-for: In modern statutory jurisdictions, the Model Penal Code (MPC) provides a more uniform framework for derivative liability than the often-conflicting common law. A student might argue that the volunteers' protection stems from the MPC’s specific drafting regarding accomplice liability and the availability of defenses. This approach prioritizes the statutory structure of the code over the theoretical distinctions between "justification" and "excuse" found in common law history.

Head-to-head: (b) is the strongest answer because it correctly identifies necessity as a justification and accurately describes the common law rule of derivative justification-sharing. Option (a) is legally incorrect because necessity is classified as a justification, not an excuse. Option (c) contains a false claim regarding "strict" accomplice liability, which is not a common law doctrine. Option (d) fails because if an act is justified (the "right" choice), it is right for everyone involved; the "no legal alternative" element is an attribute of the choice itself, not a personal trait of every assistant. Option (e) is incorrect because it relies on the MPC when the question specifically references common law (Fact 6), and the MPC does not actually extend "all" affirmative defenses to accomplices regardless of the justification/excuse distinction.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils" — wrong because necessity is historically and legally classified as a justification that applies to the act.
- (c): "the common law treats accomplice liability as strict" — wrong because accomplice liability requires specific mens rea and is derivative, not strict.
- (d): "necessity's 'no reasonable legal alternative' element must be proven individually by each participant" — wrong because if the principal's act is justified, the act itself is not a crime, shielding all participants.
- (e): "the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design" — wrong because it contradicts the MPC's actual structure (which maintains the justification/excuse distinction) and the fact-pattern's reliance on common law.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.

(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification -- the act itself is deemed right -- and any third party who assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->

(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict -- once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.

(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.

(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification -- the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor, but necessity is a justification. Option (c) relies on a false rule of strict accomplice liability; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach to. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element; it actually flows from the principal act being right. Option (e) reaches the right result but wrongly applies the MPC framework; Fact 6 specifies common law, and justification-sharing is a common law principle.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Slide 3 ("Justification: You made the right choice. Excuse: You had no real choice.").

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that necessity is driven by the overwhelming pressure of circumstances, making it conceptually similar to duress. If necessity is classified as an excuse, it focuses on the actor's lack of blameworthiness rather than the objective rightness of the act. Under common law, excuses are personal to the actor. Therefore, the volunteers cannot piggyback on Foot's excuse and must independently prove they were coerced or faced a choice of evils.
(b) Argument-for: This correctly relies on the fundamental distinction between justification and excuse. Necessity is a justification defense, meaning society deems the actor's choice of evils to be the objectively right conduct under the circumstances. Because a justified act is considered lawful, there is no underlying criminal act to which derivative liability can attach. Thus, any volunteer who assists a justified principal is inherently assisting in a lawful act and shares the justification.
(c) Argument-for: A student might contend that common law accomplice liability focuses strictly on the accomplice's own actus reus (assistance) and mens rea (intent to assist). They might argue that the principal's successful affirmative defense merely exempts the principal from punishment without altering the inherently illegal nature of the act itself. Under this view, the volunteers' knowing assistance in the standoff makes them fully liable regardless of Foot's necessity claim.
(d) Argument-for: Even if necessity applies generally, a student could argue that affirmative defenses require an individualized factual inquiry. Necessity strictly requires that there be "no reasonable legal alternative." A student might reason that if a volunteer had other legal ways to help but chose to participate in the standoff instead, that volunteer's specific conduct was not "necessary." Thus, the legal elements of necessity must be independently satisfied by each party seeking its protection.
(e) Argument-for: A student might assume that modern criminal law, specifically the Model Penal Code, abolishes the strict common law distinction between justification and excuse for accomplice liability. They could argue that the MPC systematically extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices to avoid disparate outcomes, making the conceptual justification/excuse dichotomy irrelevant to why the volunteers escape liability.

Head-to-head:
Option (b) is clearly the best answer because it correctly identifies necessity as a justification and accurately states the common law rule that justification defenses flow to accomplices. Option (a) is definitively wrong because it explicitly misclassifies necessity as an excuse. Option (c) relies on a fabricated rule of "strict" accomplice liability that categorically bars defense transfers, ignoring the justification/excuse distinction. Option (d) incorrectly applies the requirement of independent proof to derivative liability; accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the justification elements because the principal act itself is not a crime. Option (e) contains a false legal claim because the MPC explicitly does not extend "all" affirmative defenses to accomplices (excuses remain personal), and it conflicts with the prompt's common law orientation.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is a personal excuse" — wrong because necessity is universally recognized as a justification defense.
- (c): "no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice" — wrong because justification defenses categorically do transfer to accomplices under the common law.
- (d): "must be proven individually by each participant" — wrong because derivative justification flows automatically from the principal's act being deemed lawful, requiring no independent proof of necessity elements by the accomplice.
- (e): "the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices" — wrong because the MPC does not extend personal excuses (like duress or insanity) to accomplices.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q03

### Grounding

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Facts 3 and 6). Holmes claims self-defense. Under this jurisdiction's rules, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient to establish self-defense under this jurisdiction's standard.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation -- knowing only that a small, unarmed woman identified herself as a process server delivering legal papers -- would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes the kind of prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force, and this jurisdiction imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character" who is categorically barred from raising self-defense under the common law.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary to repel an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed. Holmes's "situation" includes hearing Foot's statement earlier that day. But a reasonable person who hears a secondhand warning about possible forced removal, and then encounters a small, unarmed woman who identifies herself as a process server with legal papers, would not conclude that imminent unlawful force was about to be used. Lisa made no threatening gestures, carried only a clipboard and envelope, and verbally identified her purpose. Option (a) applies a purely subjective standard -- the false proposition is that "an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient" under this jurisdiction. Fact 6 specifies an objective reasonableness standard, consistent with *Goetz*'s holding that "reasonably believes" requires an objective element. Option (c) states that prior threat information "automatically makes" a subsequent belief "objectively reasonable" -- the false proposition is the word "automatically." Prior information is incorporated into the reasonableness inquiry, but the incorporation must still produce a reasonable conclusion when measured against the totality of circumstances. Option (d) states that the duty to retreat applies "before any use of force" -- the false proposition is that retreat is required for non-deadly force. The duty to retreat applies only to deadly force, not to all uses of force. Holmes used non-deadly force (a shove), so the retreat requirement is not triggered. Option (e) fabricates a categorical bar -- the false proposition is that a prior assault conviction bars a defendant from raising self-defense. No such rule exists; prior convictions do not eliminate the right to self-defense.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: Honest belief alone is sufficient — wrong because this jurisdiction requires objective reasonableness (Fact 6)
(b) CORRECT
(c) False claim: Prior threat information automatically makes any subsequent belief reasonable — wrong because incorporation still requires the overall conclusion to be objectively reasonable
(d) False claim: Duty to retreat applies to all uses of force — wrong because it applies only to deadly force
(e) False claim: Prior assault conviction categorically bars self-defense — wrong because no such rule exists
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; *People v. Goetz*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Self-defense (Reasonableness and Retreat), Ch 22, Refinement `objective-reasonableness-standard`, `situational-incorporation`, and `duty-to-retreat-general`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Facts 3 and 6). Holmes claims self-defense. Under this jurisdiction's rules, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient to establish self-defense under this jurisdiction's standard.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation -- knowing only that a small, unarmed woman identified herself as a process server delivering legal papers -- would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force.

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes the kind of prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force, and this jurisdiction imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character" who is categorically barred from raising self-defense under the common law.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary to repel an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed. Holmes's "situation" includes hearing Foot's statement earlier that day. But a reasonable person who hears a secondhand warning about possible forced removal, and then encounters a small, unarmed woman who identifies herself as a process server with legal papers, would not conclude that imminent unlawful force was about to be used. Lisa made no threatening gestures, carried only a clipboard and envelope, and verbally identified her purpose. Option (a) applies a purely subjective standard -- the false proposition is that "an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient" under this jurisdiction. Fact 6 specifies an objective reasonableness standard, consistent with *Goetz*'s holding that "reasonably believes" requires an objective element. Option (c) states that prior threat information "automatically makes" a subsequent belief "objectively reasonable" -- the false proposition is the word "automatically." Prior information is incorporated into the reasonableness inquiry, but the incorporation must still produce a reasonable conclusion when measured against the totality of circumstances. Option (d) states that the duty to retreat applies "before any use of force" -- the false proposition is that retreat is required for non-deadly force. The duty to retreat applies only to deadly force, not to all uses of force. Holmes used non-deadly force (a shove), so the retreat requirement is not triggered. Option (e) fabricates a categorical bar -- the false proposition is that a prior assault conviction bars a defendant from raising self-defense. No such rule exists; prior convictions do not eliminate the right to self-defense.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under some jurisdictions influenced by a purely subjective interpretation of the Model Penal Code’s "belief" requirement, the defendant’s actual internal perception is the primary focus of the justification. If Holmes genuinely perceived Lisa as an imminent threat of unlawful force due to Foot’s warning, his "honest belief" would satisfy the core requirement for self-defense. This approach prioritizes the defendant’s moral culpability, arguing that one who acts in a sincere belief of necessity lacks the requisite "evil mind" for a criminal assault conviction.

(b) Argument-for: Self-defense under the *Goetz* standard requires that a defendant's belief be objectively reasonable. Here, the "situation" includes the defendant's knowledge, but a reasonable person would also account for the victim's physical appearance and verbal statements. Lisa was a small, unarmed woman who explicitly identified herself as a process server; a shove is an unreasonable response to a person delivering papers. Therefore, even if Holmes felt fear, that fear was not one a reasonable person in his position would share, defeating the defense.

(c) Argument-for: The objective reasonableness standard requires the jury to place themselves in the defendant's shoes, including all "prior knowledge" the defendant possessed. Because Foot explicitly warned Holmes that people were coming to "drag us out," any person approaching the property shortly thereafter is framed by that specific threat. This prior information serves as the foundational lens through which all subsequent actions are viewed, rendering Holmes’s reactive use of force a reasonable response to the "situation" as he was told it would unfold.

(d) Argument-for: This argument relies on a strict interpretation of the "necessity" element inherent in all self-defense claims. If a jurisdiction mandates that force is only a "last resort," the duty to avoid conflict could be construed to apply to all levels of force, not just deadly force. If Holmes had the ability to simply close the door or step back—avoiding the confrontation entirely—his failure to do so renders the use of force "unnecessary," thereby forfeiting the defense under a "strict necessity" or "universal retreat" rule.

(e) Argument-for: One might argue from a "clean hands" or "initial aggressor" perspective that the law should not afford affirmative justifications to individuals with a demonstrated history of violating the peace. If the common law recognizes that character informs the interpretation of intent, a categorical rule could be posited that those with prior violent convictions cannot benefit from a defense that presumes a law-abiding person was forced into a state of nature. This ensures that the defense of self-defense remains a narrow privilege for those not otherwise predisposed to violence.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the strongest because it correctly identifies the hybrid objective-subjective standard used in most modern jurisdictions (*Goetz*). It acknowledges Holmes's subjective "situation" (the warning) but correctly concludes that the objective indicators (Lisa’s identity and appearance) override it. Option (a) fails because it ignores the objective element entirely. Option (c) uses "automatically," which misstates the law—prior threats are factors to consider, not dispositive "get out of jail free" cards. Option (d) fails because the duty to retreat is famously limited to deadly force in retreat jurisdictions. Option (e) is legally a non-starter, as there is no common law or statutory "categorical bar" based on prior criminal record.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient" — wrong because the jurisdiction follows an objective reasonableness standard, requiring the belief to be both honest AND reasonable.
- (c): "automatically makes any subsequent belief... objectively reasonable" — wrong because prior knowledge is only one factor in the totality of the circumstances and does not create a per se rule of reasonableness.
- (d): "imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force" — wrong because the duty to retreat applies specifically to the use of deadly force, not non-deadly force like a shove.
- (e): "categorically barred from raising self-defense" — wrong because prior convictions may be used for impeachment or sentencing, but they never legally preclude a defendant from asserting a justification defense for a new act.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Facts 3 and 6). Holmes claims self-defense. Under this jurisdiction's rules, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient to establish self-defense under this jurisdiction's standard.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation -- knowing only that a small, unarmed woman identified herself as a process server delivering legal papers -- would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes the kind of prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force, and this jurisdiction imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character" who is categorically barred from raising self-defense under the common law.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary to repel an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed. Holmes's "situation" includes hearing Foot's statement earlier that day. But a reasonable person who hears a secondhand warning about possible forced removal, and then encounters a small, unarmed woman who identifies herself as a process server with legal papers, would not conclude that imminent unlawful force was about to be used. Lisa made no threatening gestures, carried only a clipboard and envelope, and verbally identified her purpose. Option (a) applies a purely subjective standard -- the false proposition is that "an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient" under this jurisdiction. Fact 6 specifies an objective reasonableness standard, consistent with *Goetz*'s holding that "reasonably believes" requires an objective element. Option (c) states that prior threat information "automatically makes" a subsequent belief "objectively reasonable" -- the false proposition is the word "automatically." Prior information is incorporated into the reasonableness inquiry, but the incorporation must still produce a reasonable conclusion when measured against the totality of circumstances. Option (d) states that the duty to retreat applies "before any use of force" -- the false proposition is that retreat is required for non-deadly force. The duty to retreat applies only to deadly force, not to all uses of force. Holmes used non-deadly force (a shove), so the retreat requirement is not triggered. Option (e) fabricates a categorical bar -- the false proposition is that a prior assault conviction bars a defendant from raising self-defense. No horizontal such rule exists; prior convictions do not eliminate the right to self-defense.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: Honest belief alone is sufficient — wrong because this jurisdiction requires objective reasonableness (Fact 6)
(b) CORRECT
(c) False claim: Prior threat information automatically makes any subsequent belief reasonable — wrong because incorporation still requires the overall conclusion to be objectively reasonable
(d) False claim: Duty to retreat applies to all uses of force — wrong because it applies only to deadly force
(e) False claim: Prior assault conviction categorically bars self-defense — wrong because no such rule exists
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; *People v. Goetz*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under a purely subjective standard, such as the Model Penal Code § 3.04 formulation ("believes that such force is immediately necessary"), the defendant's genuine state of mind governs the defense. A student might argue that if Holmes honestly feared an imminent forced removal, this honest subjective belief is indeed "sufficient" to validate his self-defense claim, excusing his mistaken use of force against the process server.
(b) Argument-for: In jurisdictions following the standard objective requirement for self-defense (as modeled in *Goetz*), the defendant's belief must be that of a "reasonable person in the defendant's situation." Even accounting for the prior warning of being "dragged out," a reasonable person would not view a small, unarmed woman identifying herself as a process server as an imminent threat of unlawful force. Thus, Holmes's use of non-deadly force was objectively unreasonable, and the defense fails.
(c) Argument-for: Under *People v. Goetz*, prior experiences and contextual knowledge possessed by the defendant must be incorporated into the "reasonable person" standard. A student could interpret this doctrine to mean that when a defendant receives a specific threat (e.g., people coming to drag them out), the law effectively mandates that taking defensive action against unfamiliar intruders aligns with objective reasonableness, automatically validating the defendant's fearful reaction to the perceived threat.
(d) Argument-for: In jurisdictions that reject stand-your-ground laws, a duty to retreat may be imposed if it can be done with complete safety. A student might argue that this duty applies universally to minimize all violent altercations. Because Holmes could have simply stepped back and closed the door instead of shoving Lisa, he failed to fulfill this strict prerequisite, invalidating his self-defense claim entirely.
(e) Argument-for: Self-defense is traditionally reserved for innocent parties protecting themselves from unprovoked attacks. A student might mistakenly recall that "unclean hands" or being a known initial aggressor can defeat self-defense claims, and improperly extrapolate that a prior assault conviction renders someone a "person of violent character" who forfeits the privilege to invoke self-defense as a matter of law.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly synthesizes the objective reasonableness standard with the specific facts, noting that a reasonable person would not foresee imminent unlawful force from an unarmed process server. The distractors all fail because they contain strictly falsifiable, absolute legal claims. Option (a) wrongly claims an honest subjective belief alone is "sufficient" under this jurisdiction's rules. Option (c) wrongly claims prior threats "automatically" make subsequent beliefs objectively reasonable, defying the totality-of-the-circumstances test. Option (d) incorrectly extends the duty to retreat to "any use of force," whereas the doctrine applies exclusively to deadly force. Option (e) fabricates a rule that a prior conviction "categorically" bars self-defense. All distractors are cleanly locked with definitive falsehoods.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient" — wrong because an honest belief is necessary but not sufficient; the belief must also be objectively reasonable.
- (c): "automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable" — wrong because prior threat information is merely a factor to be incorporated into the reasonableness analysis, not an automatic guarantee of objective reasonableness.
- (d): "this jurisdiction imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force" — wrong because the duty to retreat applies strictly to the use of deadly force, not non-deadly force like a shove.
- (e): "who is categorically barred from raising self-defense under the common law" — wrong because common law does not categorically bar individuals with prior convictions from asserting self-defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q04

### Grounding

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. <!-- correct -->

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: A documented pattern of carrying out past threats satisfies CL imminence regardless of any future deadline — wrong because CL requires a present and immediate threat; pattern-of-past-violence does not substitute for temporal proximity, and "until Friday" defeats imminence
(b) False claim: CL does not extend duress to threats against family members — wrong because CL extends to threats against close relations
(c) CORRECT
(d) False claim: Duress is categorically unavailable for theft — wrong because the CL bar applies only to homicide
(e) False claim: Credibility and specificity substitute for imminence — wrong because CL treats imminence as a mandatory separate element
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duress, Ch 21, Refinements `duress-imminence-proximity` and `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists.
