**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that Nietzsche’s history of violence makes the threat "operating" on Holmes's mind immediately. Under a functional view of the common law, the requirement for imminence is intended to ensure the threat is credible and leaves no time for legal alternatives. If Nietzsche's pattern of violence proves he will certainly follow through, the "deadline" of Friday is a mere formality, and the danger is effectively present from the moment of the threat. Thus, the documented pattern of carrying out threats satisfies the spirit of the imminence requirement.

(b) Argument-for: While modern interpretations of the common law often include family members, the oldest formulations of the defense were strictly limited to the person of the defendant (the "threat of death or serious bodily injury to himself"). A student might argue that the common law's default rules, if strictly applied as they existed in their most narrow historical form, do not permit vicarious duress. Therefore, because the threat was directed at Holmes's mother rather than Holmes himself, the defense fails.

(c) Argument-for: This is the standard application of the common law "present and immediate" rule. Common law duress is notoriously narrow, requiring a "gun to the head" scenario. Because Nietzsche gave Holmes a deadline of Friday—several days away—Holmes had time to seek legal alternatives, such as contacting the police. The threat was of a future harm, not a present and immediate one, which categorically disqualifies the duress defense under the common law.

(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that because duress is an excuse based on the overwhelming of the will to avoid physical harm, it is logically restricted to crimes committed against persons to prevent greater personal harm. While it is widely accepted that duress is excluded from murder, some jurisdictions or historical interpretations may have excluded property crimes because the "choice of evils" (even if duress is technically an excuse) does not favor the commission of theft when the personal threat is not immediate.

(e) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the "credible and specific" nature of the threat as the primary concern of the common law. If the threat is so specific that Holmes knows exactly what will happen and so credible that he knows it is certain to occur, the temporal proximity (imminence) becomes a secondary factor. By this logic, Nietzsche's history of violence satisfies the core concern of the common law—ensuring the defendant acted under a genuine and overwhelming fear—regardless of the temporal gap.

Head-to-head: (c) is the strongest answer because it accurately applies the rigid "present and immediate" requirement of the common law. Option (a) and (e) attempt to bypass the temporal requirement using credibility or past behavior, but the common law treats imminence as a distinct, mandatory element that is not satisfied by "inevitability." Option (b) is incorrect because the common law has long recognized "close relations" (especially a parent) as valid targets of a threat. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law limitation on duress is famously narrow, applying almost exclusively to murder, not to theft or property crimes.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline" — wrong because common law imminence is a temporal requirement (present and immediate) that cannot be substituted by a pattern of behavior.
- (b): "the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" — wrong because the common law specifically includes "close relations" (including mothers) in the scope of protected targets.
- (d): "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law" — wrong because the common law exclusion for duress applies only to homicide, not to property crimes like theft.
- (e): "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" — wrong because the common law treats imminence as a mandatory, separate element from credibility/specificity.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. <!-- correct -->

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: A documented pattern of carrying out past threats satisfies CL imminence regardless of any future deadline — wrong because CL requires a present and immediate threat; pattern-of-past-violence does not substitute for temporal proximity, and "until Friday" defeats imminence
(b) False claim: CL does not extend duress to threats against family members — wrong because CL extends to threats against close relations
(c) CORRECT
(d) False claim: Duress is categorically unavailable for theft — wrong because the CL bar applies only to homicide
(e) False claim: Credibility and specificity substitute for imminence — wrong because CL treats imminence as a mandatory separate element
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that option (a) is correct by drawing on specific modern exceptions or functionally relaxed standards (like battered-spouse syndrome cases) where strict temporal proximity is sometimes softened. Under this view, a documented history of violence makes a threat practically inescapable and therefore "imminent" in a functional sense. They could reason that the common law has evolved to accommodate such patterns of abuse, rendering the "Friday" deadline legally irrelevant as long as the threatener's violent history is established.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that option (b) correctly identifies an early or strict common-law limitation. Because duress is fundamentally about involuntary action driven by an immediate instinct for self-preservation, one might incorrectly conclude that threats to third parties, even family members, cannot legally overbear a defendant's will to excuse a crime. This relies on an overly rigid, archaic view of the defense's target scope.
(c) Argument-for: Option (c) relies on the orthodox common-law definition of duress, which strictly requires the threat to be present, imminent, and impending. Because Nietzsche's threat gave Holmes until Friday—multiple days away—the danger was not immediate. Consequently, Holmes had time to seek legal alternatives, meaning his duress defense fails under the traditional strict imminence standard.
(d) Argument-for: A student might select option (d) by conflating the common-law bar on using duress for murder with a broader prohibition on its use for specific property crimes. They might rationalize that crimes involving calculated theft, unlike defensive force or necessity-driven acts against nature, categorically cannot be justified by external threats under the common law. This incorrectly assumes the common law applies categorical offense-type exclusions beyond homicide.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) appeals to a student who confuses the strict common-law imminence requirement with more flexible modern approaches, such as the Model Penal Code. Under this reasoning, the ultimate test is whether the threat was credible and specific enough to overbear the will of a reasonable person. If credibility and specificity are established, the strict temporal requirement of imminence is arguably functionally satisfied or legally waived.

Head-to-head: Option (c) is the only legally accurate application of the common-law duress standard, correctly recognizing that a threat materializing days in the future fails the strict imminence requirement. The distractors all employ absolute, falsifiable language to explicitly misstate common-law doctrine. Options (a) and (e) confidently state false legal rules about imminence, claiming it is automatically satisfied by past patterns or credibility alone. Option (b) makes a clear, false claim about the target scope (excluding family members), and option (d) fabricates a categorical ban on duress for theft. The distractors are appropriately locked and unambiguously false.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline" — wrong because common law strict imminence requires a present and immediate threat; past patterns do not override future deadlines.
- (b): "the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" — wrong because the common law recognizes threats against close relations, such as a mother.
- (d): "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law" — wrong because the categorical exclusion at common law applies to homicide, not theft.
- (e): "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" — wrong because the common law treats imminence as a mandatory, independent temporal requirement.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q05

### Grounding

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Dingwall reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, that Nietzsche had previously carried out threats against other people Holmes knew, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable or unwilling to protect his mother. Would the duress analysis change? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because under the Dingwall approach, a pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the threatening party is not physically present at the time of the crime, and the combination of months of threats, Nietzsche's history of carrying out threats, and Holmes's reasonable belief that police cannot help would support submitting the duress claim to a jury. <!-- correct -->

(b) No, because the Dingwall reasoning relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained by the threatening party at the time of the crime, and Holmes was free to move about and had access to means of self-help.

(c) Yes, because the Dingwall approach eliminates the imminence requirement entirely and replaces it with a credibility inquiry, so any threat of future harm -- no matter how remote in time -- satisfies the duress defense if the threatening party has a track record of violence.

(d) No, because *Dingwall* expressly limited its relaxed imminence standard to defendants who are subject to ongoing physical confinement or restraint by the threatening party at the time of the offense, and Holmes -- who was free to move about, retained access to his phone, and was not physically restrained -- falls outside that limitation.

(e) Yes, but only if Holmes can present expert testimony on the psychological effects of sustained threats, because the Dingwall approach requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition to submitting the duress claim to the jury.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Under the Dingwall approach (Seventh Circuit), imminence does not require physical proximity of the threatening party. A pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger. The modified facts here closely parallel the Dingwall facts. Option (b) states that Dingwall "relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained" -- the false proposition is this confinement limitation. The entire point of the Dingwall approach is that imminence can be satisfied by a pattern of threats even when the threatening party is not physically present or confining the defendant. Option (c) states that Dingwall "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" and that "any threat of future harm -- no matter how remote" suffices -- the false proposition is that Dingwall eliminates imminence. Dingwall relaxes but does not eliminate the imminence requirement; it still requires a pattern of continuous, sustained threats creating a reasonable perception of imminent danger. Option (d) falsely states that *Dingwall* limited its relaxed imminence standard to defendants under ongoing physical confinement or restraint. *Dingwall*'s central holding is the opposite: a pattern of continuous, sustained threats can satisfy imminence even when the threatening party is not physically present and even when the defendant retains physical freedom of movement. The whole point of the case is that physical proximity is not required. Option (e) states that expert testimony is "a mandatory procedural precondition" -- the false proposition is this procedural requirement. Dingwall does not impose mandatory expert testimony as a precondition; expert testimony may be helpful but is not required.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) CORRECT
(b) False claim: Dingwall applies only when defendant is physically confined — wrong because Dingwall's point is that physical presence/confinement is not required
(c) False claim: Dingwall eliminates imminence entirely — wrong because it relaxes but does not eliminate the requirement
(d) False claim: Dingwall's relaxed imminence standard is limited to defendants under ongoing physical confinement — wrong because the case's central holding rejects any physical-proximity requirement; the relaxed standard applies to patterns of sustained threats regardless of confinement status
(e) False claim: Expert testimony is a mandatory procedural precondition — wrong because Dingwall imposes no such requirement
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students apply the strict physical proximity test even in circuits that have relaxed it, or limit Dingwall to IPV cases

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Dingwall Reasoning, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-imminence-proximity`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

Assume further that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, that Nietzsche had previously carried out threats against other people Holmes knew, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable or unwilling to protect his mother. (e) Yes, but only if Holmes can present expert testimony on the psychological effects of sustained threats, because the Dingwall approach requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition to submitting the duress claim to the jury.

### Edge Case Audit

```markdown
<!-- edge-case-audit: SHOULD FIX

Both assert that *Dingwall* is expressly limited to defendants who are physically confined or restrained. A test-savvy student will recognize that a multiple-choice question cannot have two identical correct answers, allowing them to immediately eliminate both (b) and (d) without knowing the law. 
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: pass
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
Recommended fix: Revise option (d) to align with the trap mentioned in your own metadata tags. This fixes the redundancy and tests exactly what you aimed to test.
```

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Dingwall reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, that Nietzsche had previously carried out threats against other people Holmes knew, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable or unwilling to protect his mother. Would the duress analysis change? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because under the Dingwall approach, a pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the threatening party is not physically present at the time of the crime, and the combination of months of threats, Nietzsche's history of carrying out threats, and Holmes's reasonable belief that police cannot help would support submitting the duress claim to a jury. <!-- correct -->

(b) No, because the Dingwall reasoning relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained by the threatening party at the time of the crime, and Holmes was free to move about and had access to means of self-help.

(c) Yes, because the Dingwall approach eliminates the imminence requirement entirely and replaces it with a credibility inquiry, so any threat of future harm -- no matter how remote in time -- satisfies the duress defense if the threatening party has a track record of violence.

(d) No, because *Dingwall* expressly limited its relaxed imminence standard to defendants who are subject to ongoing physical confinement or restraint by the threatening party at the time of the offense, and Holmes -- who was free to move about, retained access to his phone, and was not physically restrained -- falls outside that limitation.

(e) Yes, but only if Holmes can present expert testimony on the psychological effects of sustained threats, because the Dingwall approach requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition to submitting the duress claim to the jury.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Under the Dingwall approach (Seventh Circuit), imminence does not require physical proximity of the threatening party. A pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger. The modified facts here closely parallel the Dingwall facts. Option (b) states that Dingwall "relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained" -- the false proposition is this confinement limitation. The entire point of the Dingwall approach is that imminence can be satisfied by a pattern of threats even when the threatening party is not physically present or confining the defendant. Option (c) states that Dingwall "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" and that "any threat of future harm -- no matter how remote" suffices -- the false proposition is that Dingwall eliminates imminence. Dingwall relaxes but does not eliminate the imminence requirement; it still requires a pattern of continuous, sustained threats creating a reasonable perception of imminent danger. Option (d) falsely states that *Dingwall* limited its relaxed imminence standard to defendants under ongoing physical confinement or restraint. *Dingwall*'s central holding is the opposite: a pattern of continuous, sustained threats can satisfy imminence even when the threatening party is not physically present and even when the defendant retains physical freedom of movement. The whole point of the case is that physical proximity is not required. Option (e) states that expert testimony is "a mandatory procedural precondition" -- the false proposition is this procedural requirement. Dingwall does not impose mandatory expert testimony as a precondition; expert testimony may be helpful but is not required.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students apply the strict physical proximity test even in circuits that have relaxed it, or limit Dingwall to IPV cases

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the Dingwall (7th Cir.) standard, the "imminence" requirement for duress is not interpreted through a strict "temporal proximity" or "gun-to-the-head" lens. Instead, the court recognizes that a "pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats" can make a threat effectively imminent even if the threatener is not physically present at the moment of the crime. Here, Nietzsche's months-long campaign of threats, his history of violence, and Holmes's reasonable belief that authorities could not intervene satisfy this more flexible standard, making the duress defense a jury question.

(b) Argument-for: A defendant seeking a duress instruction must generally show they had "no reasonable alternative" to committing the crime. Even under a relaxed imminence standard, courts are wary of extending duress to individuals who are physically free. If Holmes was "free to move about" and had access to his phone, a court might hold that the Dingwall relaxation is inapplicable because the defendant was not in a state of "inescapable" control, which is the functional equivalent of physical confinement that the Dingwall court sought to address.

(c) Argument-for: One could argue that Dingwall functionally collapses the "imminence" element into a general inquiry about the "credibility" and "reasonableness" of the defendant's fear. If the core of the duress defense is that the defendant's will was overborne, then a credible track record of violence (like Nietzsche's) should satisfy the defense regardless of when the threat was scheduled to be carried out. In this view, Dingwall is the first step toward a system where any sufficiently terrifying future threat allows for a duress claim.

(d) Argument-for: This argument rests on a narrow reading of Dingwall's holding. One could interpret the case as being tethered to the specific dynamics of coercive control where a defendant is "psychologically confined" or effectively restrained. If the court intended to preserve the traditional boundaries of duress for the general public, it might "expressly limit" the relaxed standard to those who lack the physical autonomy Holmes possessed (freedom of movement and communication), ensuring the defense does not swallow the general rule of criminal responsibility.

(e) Argument-for: Courts often require specialized evidence to explain why a person would feel an "imminent" threat from a party who is not physically present. Much like the Battered Person Syndrome defense, which often requires expert testimony to bridge the gap between "future harm" and "imminent harm" for a jury, one could argue that Dingwall implicitly or explicitly requires such testimony as a "mandatory procedural precondition" to ensure the jury isn't simply guessing about the psychological impact of sustained threats.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is the strongest because it accurately describes the Seventh Circuit’s holding in United States v. Dingwall, which specifically permits a duress defense based on a pattern of sustained threats even without physical proximity. Option (c) fails because Dingwall explicitly refines and expands the definition of imminence rather than "eliminating" it entirely. Options (b) and (d) fail because the central innovation of Dingwall was precisely that physical confinement or "gun-to-the-head" proximity is not a prerequisite for the defense. Option (e) fails because while expert testimony is common and helpful in these cases, the law does not establish it as a "mandatory procedural precondition" for a jury instruction.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained" — wrong because Dingwall specifically applies to cases where the defendant has physical freedom but is under a pattern of unpredictable threats.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" — wrong because the Dingwall court characterizes its holding as a "refinement" or "broader understanding" of imminence, not its abolition.
- (d): "expressly limited its relaxed imminence standard to defendants who are subject to ongoing physical confinement" — wrong because Dingwall contains no such express limitation; its logic applies to the nature of the threat, not the physical walls surrounding the defendant.
- (e): "requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition" — wrong because Dingwall creates no such categorical procedural rule; the standard is about the sufficiency of the evidence of the threat itself.

Recommended fix: none
-->

Assume further that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, that Nietzsche had previously carried out threats against other people Holmes knew, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable or unwilling to protect his mother. **Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly applies the *Dingwall* standard. In *Dingwall*, the Seventh Circuit held that a continuous pattern of unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent harm, even if the threatener is not physically present or strictly confining the defendant at the exact moment of the offense. Applying this reasoning to Holmes's months of threats, Nietzsche's track record, and the police's inability to help correctly mirrors the structural conditions that justify submitting the defense to the jury.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that *Dingwall* was fundamentally concerned with defendants who were essentially trapped by their abusers. By extending the doctrine to Holmes—who is completely "free to move about and had access to means of self-help"—the relaxed imminence standard is stretched beyond its logical bounds. Therefore, the defense fails because Holmes lacked the physical confinement or restraint that practically defined the victim's plight in *Dingwall*.
(c) Argument-for: A student might read *Dingwall* as a functional abrogation of the strict temporal "imminence" requirement in favor of a "continuous threat" credibility inquiry. If the threatener has a known track record of violence, the actual timing of the future threat is irrelevant; the victim knows the threatener is capable of executing it eventually. Thus, *Dingwall* effectively eliminates strict imminence.
(d) Argument-for: Similar to (b), a student might rely on the specific factual context of *Dingwall* to argue that the court drew a hard line to avoid opening the floodgates for duress claims. They could argue the court explicitly cabined its relaxed standard to defendants under "ongoing physical confinement or restraint," preventing a free-moving person with cell phone access, like Holmes, from utilizing the defense. 
(e) Argument-for: Because *Dingwall* relied heavily on the psychological dynamics of battered woman syndrome and the profound effects of sustained abuse, a student could argue that expert psychological testimony is a necessary element to establish why a non-proximate threat was reasonably perceived as "imminent." Without this expert testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition, the jury lacks the necessary context to properly evaluate the claim.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is correct because it perfectly matches the *Dingwall* holding that imminence does not strictly require physical proximity, and a sustained pattern of threats can suffice. Distractors (b), (c), (d), and (e) all contain explicit false legal claims. (b) falsely claims *Dingwall* "relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined." (c) falsely claims *Dingwall* "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely." (d) falsely claims *Dingwall* "expressly limited" its standard to "ongoing physical confinement." (e) falsely claims expert testimony is a "mandatory procedural precondition." While all distractors contain falsifiable errors, options (b) and (d) are practically identical in their underlying legal claim (asserting a physical confinement limitation). 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained" — wrong because *Dingwall* explicitly held that physical confinement or physical presence is not required for a pattern of threats to create an imminent danger.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" and "any threat... no matter how remote in time -- satisfies the duress defense" — wrong because *Dingwall* only relaxes the strict physical-proximity aspect of imminence; it does not eliminate imminence entirely.
- (d): "expressly limited its relaxed imminence standard to defendants who are subject to ongoing physical confinement or restraint" — wrong for the same reason as (b); the case's central premise explicitly rejects this limitation.
- (e): "requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition" — wrong because while expert testimony on the psychological effects of sustained threats is helpful and often admissible, *Dingwall* does not establish it as a mandatory procedural precondition.

Recommended fix: Options (b) and (d) test the exact same false limitation (physical confinement). Change (d) to test a different falsifiable limitation to eliminate this redundancy. For example: "No, because *Dingwall* expressly limited its relaxed imminence standard exclusively to cases involving intimate partner violence (IPV), and Holmes and Nietzsche's relationship falls outside that categorical limitation."
-->

---

## Q06

### Grounding

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald Wald. Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov was in an acute psychotic episode caused by schizophrenia and could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's MPC insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's insanity defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person.

(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong.

(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect under MPC Section 4.01(2).

(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives, which means he could not appreciate it was wrong. <!-- correct -->

(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(1), a defendant is not responsible if, as a result of mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality (wrongfulness) of his conduct. Markov has a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia -- a major psychotic disorder, not a personality disorder). The causal link is clear: Dr. Cole will testify the psychotic episode was driven by the schizophrenia. Markov understood he was striking a person (he knew the nature and quality of the act). But he believed he was saving lives from an assassin, meaning he could not appreciate that his act was wrong. This is the classic wrongfulness prong. Option (a) misapplies the nature-and-quality prong -- the false proposition is that Markov "did not know the nature and quality of his act." Markov knew he was hitting a person; the nature-and-quality prong applies to defendants who do not understand what they are physically doing (e.g., believing they are squeezing a grapefruit when strangling a child). Option (b) treats Markov's statement as evidence of sanity -- the false proposition is that understanding the nature of one's actions means one "knew what he was doing was wrong." The statement "I was stopping an assassination" is evidence of the delusion, not consciousness of guilt; it supports, rather than undermines, the claim that he could not appreciate wrongfulness. Option (c) states that paranoid schizophrenia is excluded under MPC Section 4.01(2) as a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" -- the false proposition is this classification. Paranoid schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying mental disease; the Section 4.01(2) exclusion targets personality disorders manifested solely by repeated antisocial conduct, not psychotic disorders. Option (e) invokes the irresistible impulse test and claims Markov's capacity to control his behavior was "entirely destroyed" -- the false proposition is that irresistible impulse is the applicable framework. Markov's problem was not that he knew it was wrong but could not stop; his problem was that he did not appreciate it was wrong at all. The volitional prong is not the best fit when the cognitive prong directly applies.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: Markov did not know the nature and quality of his act — wrong because he knew he was striking a person
(b) False claim: Understanding the nature of one's actions = knowing the act was wrong — wrong because the statement evidences the delusion, not sanity
(c) False claim: Paranoid schizophrenia is excluded under 4.01(2) — wrong because schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying disease; the exclusion targets personality disorders
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: Irresistible impulse is the applicable framework — wrong because Markov's problem was cognitive (not appreciating wrongfulness), not volitional
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC Insanity Defense, Ch 23, Refinement `mpc-appreciate-criminality`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

### Edge Case Audit

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald Wald. Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov was in an acute psychotic episode caused by schizophrenia and could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's MPC insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's insanity defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person.

(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong.

(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect under MPC Section 4.01(2).

(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives, which means he could not appreciate it was wrong. <!-- correct -->

(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(1), a defendant is not responsible if, as a result of mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality (wrongfulness) of his conduct. Markov has a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia -- a major psychotic disorder, not a personality disorder). The causal link is clear: Dr. Cole will testify the psychotic episode was driven by the schizophrenia. Markov understood he was striking a person (he knew the nature and quality of the act). But he believed he was saving lives from an assassin, meaning he could not appreciate that his act was wrong. This is the classic wrongfulness prong. Option (a) misapplies the nature-and-quality prong -- the false proposition is that Markov "did not know the nature and quality of his act." Markov knew he was hitting a person; the nature-and-quality prong applies to defendants who do not understand what they are physically doing (e.g., believing they are squeezing a grapefruit when strangling a child). Option (b) treats Markov's statement as evidence of sanity -- the false proposition is that understanding the nature of one's actions means one "knew what he was doing was wrong." The statement "I was stopping an assassination" is evidence of the delusion, not consciousness of guilt; it supports, rather than undermines, the claim that he could not appreciate wrongfulness. Option (c) states that paranoid schizophrenia is excluded under MPC Section 4.01(2) as a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" -- the false proposition is this classification. Paranoid schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying mental disease; the Section 4.01(2) exclusion targets personality disorders manifested solely by repeated antisocial conduct, not psychotic disorders. Option (e) invokes the irresistible impulse test and claims Markov's capacity to control his behavior was "entirely destroyed" -- the false proposition is that irresistible impulse is the applicable framework. Markov's problem was not that he knew it was wrong but could not stop; his problem was that he did not appreciate it was wrong at all. The volitional prong is not the best fit when the cognitive prong directly applies.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald Wald. Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov was in an acute psychotic episode caused by schizophrenia and could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's MPC insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's insanity defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person.

(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong.

(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect under MPC Section 4.01(2).

(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives, which means he could not appreciate it was wrong.

(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(1), a defendant is not responsible if, as a result of mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality (wrongfulness) of his conduct. Markov has a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia -- a major psychotic disorder, not a personality disorder). The causal link is clear: Dr. Cole will testify the psychotic episode was driven by the schizophrenia. Markov understood he was striking a person (he knew the nature and quality of the act). But he believed he was saving lives from an assassin, meaning he could not appreciate that his act was wrong. This is the classic wrongfulness prong. Option (a) misapplies the nature-and-quality prong -- the false proposition is that Markov "did not know the nature and quality of his act." Markov knew he was hitting a person; the nature-and-quality prong applies to defendants who do not understand what they are physically doing (e.g., believing they are squeezing a grapefruit when strangling a child). Option (b) treats Markov's statement as evidence of sanity -- the false proposition is that understanding the nature of one's actions means one "knew what he was doing was wrong." The statement "I was stopping an assassination" is evidence of the delusion, not consciousness of guilt; it supports, rather than undermines, the claim that he could not appreciate wrongfulness. Option (c) states that paranoid schizophrenia is excluded under MPC Section 4.01(2) as a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" -- the false proposition is this classification. Paranoid schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying mental disease; the Section 4.01(2) exclusion targets personality disorders manifested solely by repeated antisocial conduct, not psychotic disorders. Option (e) invokes the irresistible impulse test and claims Markov's capacity to control his behavior was "entirely destroyed" -- the false proposition is that irresistible impulse is the applicable framework. Markov's problem was not that he knew it was wrong but could not stop; his problem was that he did not appreciate it was wrong at all. The volitional prong is not the best fit when the cognitive prong directly applies.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: Markov did not know the nature and quality of his act — wrong because he knew he was striking a person
(b) False claim: Understanding the nature of one's actions = knowing the act was wrong — wrong because the statement evidences the delusion, not sanity
(c) False claim: Paranoid schizophrenia is excluded under 4.01(2) — wrong because schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying disease; the exclusion targets personality disorders
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: Irresistible impulse is the applicable framework — wrong because Markov's problem was cognitive (not appreciating wrongfulness), not volitional

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the M'Naghten standard, which informs the cognitive prong of the MPC, a defendant must know the nature and quality of their act. Markov believed he was "stopping an assassination," which suggests that the "nature" of his act in his own mind was an act of law enforcement or heroism, not an "assault." If a defendant's delusion fundamentally recharacterizes the physical act from a crime into a virtuous deed, a advocate can argue he lacks knowledge of the act's nature.

(b) Argument-for: Evidence of a defendant's state of mind often comes from their own words. By stating "I was stopping an assassination," Markov demonstrated that he knew exactly what he was doing: using force to stop a person. This shows he was not in a state of automatism or "squeezing a grapefruit." Furthermore, by offering a justification, he shows an awareness that his act (striking someone) would otherwise be socially unacceptable or "wrong," suggesting he understood the concept of wrongfulness but simply believed he had a defense.

(c) Argument-for: MPC Section 4.01(2) contains the "caveat paragraph" intended to exclude sociopaths and those with antisocial personality disorders from the insanity defense. If a defendant's schizophrenia primarily manifests through violent outbursts and antisocial acts against others, a prosecutor might argue that the condition is "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" in the context of the criminal justice system. This would technically exclude him from the defense under a strict, conduct-based reading of the exclusion.

(d) Argument-for: This is the strongest application of the MPC's "substantial capacity" test. The MPC uses the word "appreciate" to signify a deeper emotional and cognitive understanding than mere "knowledge." Even if Markov knew he was hitting a man, his delusion that the man was an assassin meant he could not appreciate that the act was morally or legally wrong. Schizophrenia is the paradigmatic "mental disease" that satisfies the threshold of the rule.

(e) Argument-for: The MPC insanity defense includes a volitional prong—the "control" or "conformity" test. If Markov was in an "acute psychotic episode," it is medically plausible that he was acting under a command hallucination or an uncontrollable delusional impulse. If his capacity to conform his conduct to the law was "entirely destroyed," he meets and exceeds the MPC requirement of lacking "substantial capacity" to conform his conduct, fitting the spirit of the irresistible impulse test.

Head-to-head:
(d) is the most accurate because it correctly identifies the "wrongfulness" prong of the MPC (4.01(1)) and uses the correct "substantial capacity" language. (a) is weaker because Markov *did* know the nature and quality of his act (he knew he was striking a human body); his error was about the *status* of that human and the *justification* for the strike, which is a "wrongfulness" issue. (b) is legally flawed because a delusional justification is evidence of a *lack* of appreciation of wrongfulness, not evidence of sanity. (c) is factually and legally incorrect because schizophrenia is a psychotic disorder with internal symptoms (hallucinations/delusions), not a personality disorder "manifested only by" conduct. (e) is weaker than (d) because "irresistible impulse" is a common-law term superseded by the MPC's "substantial capacity to conform" language, and the facts point more directly to a cognitive failure (delusion) than a volitional one.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "he did not know the nature and quality of his act" — wrong because he understood he was physically striking a human being, which constitutes the physical nature/quality of the act.
- (b): "demonstrates that he ... knew what he was doing was wrong" — wrong because a delusional belief that one is stopping an assassination is evidence that the actor believes the act is right/justified, not wrong.
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia... is a condition 'manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct'" — wrong because schizophrenia is a clinical psychotic disorder with symptoms (hallucinations/delusions) independent of criminal conduct, unlike the personality disorders targeted by MPC 4.01(2).
- (e): "satisfying the irresistible impulse test" — wrong because the MPC uses the "substantial capacity to conform" test, and the facts describe a cognitive delusion rather than an inability to physically restrain a known-wrongful impulse.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald Wald. Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov was in an acute psychotic episode caused by schizophrenia and could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's MPC insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's insanity defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person.

(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong.

(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect under MPC Section 4.01(2).

(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives, which means he could not appreciate it was wrong. <!-- correct -->

(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(1), a defendant is not responsible if, as a result of mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality (wrongfulness) of his conduct. Markov has a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia -- a major psychotic disorder, not a personality disorder). The causal link is clear: Dr. Cole will testify the psychotic episode was driven by the schizophrenia. Markov understood he was striking a person (he knew the nature and quality of the act). But he believed he was saving lives from an assassin, meaning he could not appreciate that his act was wrong. This is the classic wrongfulness prong. Option (a) misapplies the nature-and-quality prong -- the false proposition is that Markov "did not know the nature and quality of his act." Markov knew he was hitting a person; the nature-and-quality prong applies to defendants who do not understand what they are physically doing (e.g., believing they are squeezing a grapefruit when strangling a child). Option (b) treats Markov's statement as evidence of sanity -- the false proposition is that understanding the nature of one's actions means one "knew what he was doing was wrong." The statement "I was stopping an assassination" is evidence of the delusion, not consciousness of guilt; it supports, rather than undermines, the claim that he could not appreciate wrongfulness. Option (c) states that paranoid schizophrenia is excluded under MPC Section 4.01(2) as a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" -- the false proposition is this classification. Paranoid schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying mental disease; the Section 4.01(2) exclusion targets personality disorders manifested solely by repeated antisocial conduct, not psychotic disorders. Option (e) invokes the irresistible impulse test and claims Markov's capacity to control his behavior was "entirely destroyed" -- the false proposition is that irresistible impulse is the applicable framework. Markov's problem was not that he knew it was wrong but could not stop; his problem was that he did not appreciate it was wrong at all. The volitional prong is not the best fit when the cognitive prong directly applies.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: Markov did not know the nature and quality of his act — wrong because he knew he was striking a person
(b) False claim: Understanding the nature of one's actions = knowing the act was wrong — wrong because the statement evidences the delusion, not sanity
(c) False claim: Paranoid schizophrenia is excluded under 4.01(2) — wrong because schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying disease; the exclusion targets personality disorders
(d) CORRECT

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that the MPC cognitive prong inherently encompasses the M'Naghten "nature and quality" concept. Because Markov believed he was striking an assassin rather than an innocent person, he did not truly understand the "quality" of his act. The quality of an act arguably changes depending on the identity and intent of the target. Thus, his delusion negated his knowledge of the nature and quality of the act itself, making (a) a viable interpretation of his cognitive impairment.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that Markov's statement shows deliberate, goal-oriented behavior ("stopping an assassination"), which establishes that he understood the physical reality of committing an assault. Because he knew he was acting violently against another person, he understood the legal wrongfulness of assault. Therefore, this understanding nullifies his insanity defense, as the cognitive prong requires a lack of understanding of the act's wrongfulness, and his statement proves he knew he was intentionally using force.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that the MPC § 4.01(2) exclusion for conditions "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" applies broadly to any violent symptomatology. If the student incorrectly classifies Markov's violent attack as purely antisocial conduct driven by command hallucinations, they could conclude schizophrenia falls under the psychopathy exclusion. This would categorically bar the insanity defense for Markov, making (c) appear legally supported under a strict reading of the exclusion.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that (d) perfectly maps onto the MPC standard. The MPC requires a lack of "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the wrongfulness (or criminality) of conduct. Since Markov suffered from schizophrenia, a qualifying mental disease, and believed he was saving lives, he could not appreciate the moral or legal wrongfulness of his assault. This precisely aligns with the cognitive prong of MPC § 4.01, supported by the specific facts of the prompt.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that a delusion compelling a defendant to stop an assassination functions as an overwhelming internal force. If schizophrenia completely overrode his ability to choose otherwise, it destroyed his volitional capacity. Therefore, even under the MPC, the inability to stop oneself could be seen as satisfying the volitional prong, and a student might equate this with the "irresistible impulse" test, making (e) seem like a correct description of his defense.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is clearly the correct answer because it accurately identifies the applicable MPC cognitive standard (lack of substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness) and maps it correctly to the facts (Markov believed his actions were justified to save lives). Option (a) incorrectly claims Markov did not know the nature and quality of his act, confusing a mistake as to justification with a mistake as to the physical nature of the conduct. Option (b) makes the false legal leap that understanding the physical nature of an act automatically equates to knowing it is wrong. Option (c) relies on the explicitly false legal claim that schizophrenia falls under the MPC § 4.01(2) psychopathy exclusion. Option (e) explicitly invokes the wrong legal test ("irresistible impulse test") for an MPC jurisdiction and claims his capacity was "entirely destroyed," which misstates the MPC's "substantial capacity" standard. All distractors contain explicitly falsifiable legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "he did not know the nature and quality of his act" — wrong because Markov knew he was physically striking a person; his delusion affected his appreciation of the act's wrongfulness, not its physical nature.
- (b): "demonstrates that he understood the nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong" — wrong because under insanity doctrine, understanding the physical nature of an act does not categorically equate to knowing the act is wrong (e.g., when acting under a delusion of moral justification).
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia... is a condition 'manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct' and is excluded" — wrong because schizophrenia is the paradigmatic qualifying mental disease; the MPC 4.01(2) exclusion specifically targets psychopathy/sociopathy, not psychotic disorders.
- (e): "satisfying the irresistible impulse test" — wrong because the jurisdiction applies the MPC framework (which uses the "conform his conduct" standard, not the absolute "irresistible impulse" test), and the facts only support a cognitive impairment.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q07

### Grounding

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold. <!-- correct -->

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: IDRA uses "lacked substantial capacity" — wrong because IDRA uses "unable," a stricter standard
(b) False claim: IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong — wrong because IDRA eliminated the volitional prong, not the wrongfulness prong
(c) False claim: IDRA places burden on prosecution — wrong because IDRA shifted burden to defendant
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: "Severe" excludes paranoid schizophrenia — wrong because schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic severe mental disease
-->

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — federal IDRA vs MPC insanity standards, Ch 23, Refinement `federal-idra-requirements` and `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold. <!-- correct -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold.

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: IDRA uses "lacked substantial capacity" — wrong because IDRA uses "unable," a stricter standard
(b) False claim: IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong — wrong because IDRA eliminated the volitional prong, not the wrongfulness prong
(c) False claim: IDRA places burden on prosecution — wrong because IDRA shifted burden to defendant
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: "Severe" excludes paranoid schizophrenia — wrong because schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic severe mental disease
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the IDRA and MPC tests are functionally identical in cases involving profound psychosis. While the IDRA uses the word "unable" and the MPC uses "substantial capacity," both focus on the defendant's cognitive appreciation of wrongfulness. In Markov's case, if his delusions are powerful enough to satisfy the MPC standard, they are likely powerful enough to meet the IDRA standard as well, making the linguistic difference purely academic. Thus, for Markov specifically, the change would make "no difference" in the ultimate outcome of the analysis.

(b) Argument-for: This option appeals to a student who understands that the IDRA was a restrictive reaction to the Hinckley acquittal. One way to restrict the insanity defense is to move toward a strict M'Naghten "nature and quality" test while stripping away the "moral wrongfulness" component. If IDRA were interpreted as a strict return to the cognitive-understanding-of-physical-acts standard, Markov would fail because he knew he was pulling a trigger and killing a human being. A student might believe the "reform" in the Act's title refers to this specific narrowing.

(c) Argument-for: This option targets students who confuse the federal IDRA with the pre-1984 federal standard. Under the old *Davis* rule, once a defendant raised a glimmer of insanity, the burden shifted to the prosecution to disprove it beyond a reasonable doubt. A student might argue that "the federal standard" generally favors the constitutional principle of placing all burdens on the government. If this standard applied, Markov's defense would be significantly "easier" to maintain in the face of prosecutorial rebuttal.

(d) Argument-for: This is the strongest case because it systematically identifies all four major shifts in the law triggered by 18 U.S.C. § 17. It correctly identifies the "severe" requirement, the elimination of the volitional prong (the "irresistible impulse" type test), the move from "substantial capacity" to a total "unable" standard, and the heightened burden of proof (clear and convincing). It then applies these to the facts, noting that even with these hurdles, a diagnosis like paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychosis is the classic candidate for a successful insanity defense.

(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that the addition of the word "severe" in the IDRA was the most critical change, as it was specifically intended to prevent "designer" defenses or personality disorders from qualifying. The student might contend that "severe" is a term of art that excludes most conditions except those where the defendant is truly non-functional. Under such a strict interpretation, Markov's ability to plan and execute a complex task might suggest he was not "incapacitated" enough to meet the "severe" threshold, thus making the defense harder "solely" on that basis.

Head-to-head: (d) is the superior answer because it provides an exhaustive and accurate list of the four technical changes in the federal statute. (a) contains a false legal claim regarding the phrasing of the IDRA cognitive test. (b) contains a false legal claim regarding the elimination of the wrongfulness prong; IDRA explicitly includes the inability to appreciate the "wrongfulness" of acts. (c) contains a false legal claim regarding the burden of proof, which IDRA famously shifted to the defendant. (e) contains a false claim that the "severe" requirement categorically excludes paranoid schizophrenia; in fact, the legislative history and subsequent case law make clear that such psychotic disorders are precisely what the "severe" requirement was meant to include, while excluding personality disorders like sociopathy.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness'" — wrong because IDRA uses the stricter "unable to appreciate" standard.
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because IDRA explicitly retains the "wrongfulness" prong in 18 U.S.C. § 17(a).
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17(b)) explicitly places the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "IDRA adds the word 'severe'... which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia" — wrong because paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychosis is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease for which the IDRA was designed.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold. <!-- correct -->

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.
