**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: IDRA and MPC both contain a cognitive prong centered on "appreciating wrongfulness." A student might argue that the slight linguistic distinction between "lacked substantial capacity" (MPC) and "unable" (IDRA) is a distinction without a practical difference in many cognitive-insanity cases, especially in jurisdictions that read "unable" functionally as a substantial lack of capacity. Therefore, practically speaking, they use identical cognitive tests, and Markov satisfies both.
(b) Argument-for: Under the original M'Naghten rule, some jurisdictions limited insanity strictly to the "nature and quality" of the act. A student might mistakenly believe that IDRA, being a strict post-Hinckley reform, reverted entirely to the strictest form of M'Naghten, eliminating the wrongfulness prong to prevent defendants who knew what they were doing from using the defense. Thus, under this view, IDRA defeats Markov entirely.
(c) Argument-for: Prior to IDRA, the federal rule placed the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt once raised. A student might argue that this historical fact still governs constitutional due process under *In re Winship* for elements of culpability, and thus IDRA actually kept or reinforced this burden for cognitive insanity, making the defense easier than some state versions of the MPC.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) correctly identifies all the major statutory changes enacted by the IDRA: adding "severe," eliminating the volitional ("irresistible impulse") prong, changing "substantial capacity" to "unable," and shifting the burden of proof to the defendant under a clear and convincing evidence standard. Despite these heightened requirements, paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is precisely the type of severe mental illness IDRA was designed to cover, meaning Markov's defense remains viable.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) correctly recognizes that IDRA added the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement. A student might argue that this addition was specifically intended to filter out all but the most incapacitating disorders, leaving out common episodic disorders like schizophrenia unless they cause total permanent incapacitation. Under this strict reading, IDRA's primary mechanism of making the defense harder is this definitional exclusion.

Head-to-head: Option (d) accurately recites the specific statutory mechanics of the federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) and applies them correctly to the facts. The other options fail because they rely on definitively false statements about the IDRA statute's plain text or application. Option (a) incorrectly claims the IDRA uses "substantial capacity," which it explicitly does not. Option (b) incorrectly claims IDRA eliminated the wrongfulness prong, whereas it actually eliminated the volitional prong. Option (c) completely reverses the post-Hinckley burden of proof, claiming the prosecution must disprove it. Option (e) falsely uses the absolute phrase "solely because" and wrongly claims paranoid schizophrenia is excluded from being a "severe" mental disease. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity...'" — wrong because IDRA explicitly uses the standard "unable to appreciate," rejecting the MPC's "substantial capacity."
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because IDRA expressly retained the cognitive wrongfulness prong (whether the defendant was unable to appreciate the nature and quality or the wrongfulness of his acts).
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because the IDRA shifted the burden of proof to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "solely because IDRA adds the word 'severe'... which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia" — wrong because IDRA made several other changes (burden, volitional prong), and paranoid schizophrenia is canonically recognized as a severe mental disease under the statute.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q08

### Grounding

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense at all because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose.

(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense for mentally ill defendants.

(c) Markov could use his mental illness to negate the mens rea element of assault, but only if his expert testified that the schizophrenia prevented him from forming the specific intent required -- for example, that the psychotic episode prevented him from acting purposely or knowingly with respect to causing physical injury. The wrongfulness of the act would be irrelevant. <!-- correct -->

(d) Markov would be acquitted under the mens rea model because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified -- since Markov believed he was defending others from an assassin, his delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated.

(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue that he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (adopted by Idaho, Kansas, Montana, Utah), the traditional insanity defense is abolished. Mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the specific statutory mens rea element. Markov could argue that his psychotic episode prevented him from forming the required intent for assault. But the crucial gap is that Markov deliberately struck Wald -- he intended the physical contact and intended to cause harm. His claim is not that he lacked intent to strike; his claim is that he did not know the strike was wrong. Under the mens rea model, wrongfulness is irrelevant. Option (a) states that abolition states treat mental illness as "entirely irrelevant" and that expert testimony is inadmissible "for any purpose" -- the false proposition is this total exclusion. Abolition states permit mental illness evidence to negate mens rea; they restrict its use, not eliminate it entirely. Option (b) states that *Kahler v. Kansas* "requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" -- the false proposition reverses the actual holding. *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness defense. Option (d) states that the mens rea model "permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified" -- the false proposition is that the model provides a freestanding justification defense based on delusional belief. The mens rea model does no such thing; it permits mental illness evidence *only* to negate a statutory element of the offense. The assault statute here requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." Markov purposely struck Wald and knowingly caused bodily injury -- his delusional belief about *why* he struck (defense of others) does not negate any element of the statute. The mens rea model restricts mental illness evidence to the narrow question of whether the defendant formed the required mental state, not whether the defendant believed the conduct was justified. Option (e) states that abolition states "permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness" -- the false proposition is that wrongfulness evidence is admissible. Abolition states restrict mental illness evidence to the mens rea inquiry only; moral wrongfulness is precisely what these jurisdictions exclude.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: Abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant — wrong because they permit it to negate mens rea
(b) False claim: Kahler requires a wrongfulness defense — wrong because Kahler held the opposite
(c) CORRECT
(d) False claim: Mens rea model permits a freestanding justification defense based on delusional belief — wrong because the model only permits evidence to negate statutory mens rea elements, not a separate justification defense; assault requires purposely causing bodily injury, which Markov did
(e) False claim: Abolition states permit evidence on moral wrongfulness — wrong because wrongfulness is precisely what these states exclude
-->

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense at all because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose.

(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense for mentally ill defendants.

(c) Markov could use his mental illness to negate the mens rea element of assault, but only if his expert testified that the schizophrenia prevented him from forming the specific intent required -- for example, that the psychotic episode prevented him from acting purposely or knowingly with respect to causing physical injury. The wrongfulness of the act would be irrelevant. <!-- correct -->

(d) Markov would be acquitted under the mens rea model because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified -- since Markov believed he was defending others from an assassin, his delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated.

(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue that he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (adopted by Idaho, Kansas, Montana, Utah), the traditional insanity defense is abolished. Mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the specific statutory mens rea element. Markov could argue that his psychotic episode prevented him from forming the required intent for assault. But the crucial gap is that Markov deliberately struck Wald -- he intended the physical contact and intended to cause harm. His claim is not that he lacked intent to strike; his claim is that he did not know the strike was wrong. Under the mens rea model, wrongfulness is irrelevant. Option (a) states that abolition states treat mental illness as "entirely irrelevant" and that expert testimony is inadmissible "for any purpose" -- the false proposition is this total exclusion. Abolition states permit mental illness evidence to negate mens rea; they restrict its use, not eliminate it entirely. Option (b) states that *Kahler v. Kansas* "requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" -- the false proposition reverses the actual holding. *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness defense. Option (d) states that the mens rea model "permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified" -- the false proposition is that the model provides a freestanding justification defense based on delusional belief. The mens rea model does no such thing; it permits mental illness evidence *only* to negate a statutory element of the offense. The assault statute here requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." Markov purposely struck Wald and knowingly caused bodily injury -- his delusional belief about *why* he struck (defense of others) does not negate any element of the statute. The mens rea model restricts mental illness evidence to the narrow question of whether the defendant formed the required mental state, not whether the defendant believed the conduct was justified. Option (e) states that abolition states "permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness" -- the false proposition is that wrongfulness evidence is admissible. Abolition states restrict mental illness evidence to the mens rea inquiry only; moral wrongfulness is precisely what these jurisdictions exclude.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: Abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant — wrong because they permit it to negate mens rea
(b) False claim: Kahler requires a wrongfulness defense — wrong because Kahler held the opposite
(c) CORRECT
(d) False claim: Mens rea model permits a freestanding justification defense based on delusional belief — wrong because the model only permits evidence to negate statutory mens rea elements, not a separate justification defense; assault requires purposely causing bodily injury, which Markov did

Check 1: pass (the substantive doctrine is correct, but relies on flawed phrasing flagged in Check 2)
Check 2: A highly prepared student could argue against (c) because it erroneously equates "purposely or knowingly" with "specific intent." "Knowingly causing bodily injury" is traditionally a general intent crime, and students are drilled on the specific vs. general intent dichotomy (particularly regarding when mental state defenses apply). Using the phrase "specific intent required" to describe a "knowingly" mens rea creates a strong, unintended distractor that a smart student will avoid, thinking it's a trap.
Check 3: pass (the explanation correctly centers on negation of the required mens rea)
Check 4: pass (assuming "Fact 2" in the full exam provides the context of the assassin delusion mentioned in option d)
Check 5: pass
Check 6: pass
Check 7: pass
Recommended fix: In Option (c), change "the specific intent required" to "the required mental state" or "the required mens rea." Similarly, in the Explanation, change "specific statutory mens rea" to "required statutory mens rea" to avoid triggering common law specific/general intent confusion.

### Edge Case Audit

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense at all because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose.

(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense for mentally ill defendants.

(c) Markov could use his mental illness to negate the mens rea element of assault, but only if his expert testified that the schizophrenia prevented him from forming the specific intent required -- for example, that the psychotic episode prevented him from acting purposely or knowingly with respect to causing physical injury. The wrongfulness of the act would be irrelevant. <!-- correct -->

(d) Markov would be acquitted under the mens rea model because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified -- since Markov believed he was defending others from an assassin, his delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated.

(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue that he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (adopted by Idaho, Kansas, Montana, Utah), the traditional insanity defense is abolished. Mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the specific statutory mens rea element. Markov could argue that his psychotic episode prevented him from forming the required intent for assault. Abolition states permit mental illness evidence to negate mens rea; they restrict its use, not eliminate it entirely. Option (b) states that *Kahler v. The mens rea model restricts mental illness evidence to the narrow question of whether the defendant formed the required mental state, not whether the defendant believed the conduct was justified. **Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: In jurisdictions that have abolished the insanity defense, the primary goal of the legislature was to move away from the "revolving door" of psychiatric defenses. A student could argue that in practice, abolition states treat mental illness as "entirely irrelevant" to the question of guilt, reserving such evidence solely for the sentencing phase. If the jurisdiction views psychiatric evidence as inherently unreliable for determining guilt, it might bar expert testimony altogether to prevent jury confusion, effectively leaving the defendant with "no defense" based on their mental condition during the trial phase.

(b) Argument-for: A defendant could argue that the fundamental principles of justice inherent in the Anglo-American legal tradition require a "wrongfulness" component for criminal liability. While *Kahler v. Kansas* upheld Kansas's mens rea model, a student might argue that the case did not grant states carte blanche to eliminate all moral culpability inquiries. One could argue that the Due Process Clause must provide a "floor"—a M'Naghten-style defense—because a legal system that punishes those who cannot distinguish right from wrong is fundamentally unfair and violates long-standing historical standards of decency.

(c) Argument-for: Under the "mens rea model," the insanity defense is not a separate affirmative defense; rather, mental illness evidence is only admissible if it directly negates an element of the crime (mens rea). For an assault charge requiring "purposely or knowingly" causing injury, Markov's only path is to argue that his psychosis was so severe he did not know he was striking a "person" or did not intend "injury" (e.g., he thought he was hitting a ghost). This model explicitly excludes the "wrongfulness" inquiry, making Markov's belief about the morality of his actions irrelevant.

(d) Argument-for: The "mens rea model" is predicated on the idea that a defendant must possess the requisite "guilty mind." A student could argue that a "knowing" or "purposeful" act in the criminal context implies a lack of legal justification. If Markov's mental illness led him to a delusional belief that he was acting in self-defense or defense of others, he lacks the specific "culpable" state of mind intended by the legislature for "assault." In this view, a delusional belief in justification is not an affirmative defense but a failure of the prosecution to prove a truly "purposeful" criminal injury.

(e) Argument-for: Some might argue that the term "abolition" is a misnomer and that the "mens rea model" is actually a broad invitation to use mental health evidence. If a state permits mental health evidence to negate mens rea, it would be logically inconsistent to exclude that same evidence when it bears on the defendant's overall "responsibility" or "moral wrongfulness." A student could argue that once the door to psychiatric evidence is opened for mens rea, it should be admissible for "all purposes" related to criminal responsibility to ensure a fair trial.

Head-to-head: The central conflict is the scope of psychiatric evidence in "abolition" (mens rea model) states. Option (c) is the strongest because it precisely tracks the holding of *Kahler v. Kansas* and the statutory framework of states like Kansas, which allow mental health evidence only for element-negation. Option (b) is a direct reversal of *Kahler*. Option (a) overstates the exclusion, as the Supreme Court specifically noted that Kansas *did* allow evidence for mens rea negation. Option (d) incorrectly invents a "freestanding justification" defense that does not exist in these statutes; the mens rea model is strictly limited to negating the elements of the offense itself (purpose/knowledge of injury). Option (e) fails because the very definition of the mens rea model is the exclusion of the "moral wrongfulness" inquiry.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant... for any purpose." — wrong because *Kahler* and mens rea models specifically permit evidence to negate statutory mens rea.
- (b): "Supreme Court held in Kahler v. Kansas that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense." — wrong because *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does *not* require a wrongfulness defense.
- (d): "delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated." — wrong because the mens rea model *only* permits evidence to negate statutory elements; it does not provide separate justification defenses.
- (e): "abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes... including moral wrongfulness." — wrong because the hallmark of the mens rea model is the exclusion of the moral wrongfulness inquiry.

Recommended fix: none
-->
```

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense at all because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose.

(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense for mentally ill defendants.

(c) Markov could use his mental illness to negate the mens rea element of assault, but only if his expert testified that the schizophrenia prevented him from forming the specific intent required -- for example, that the psychotic episode prevented him from acting purposely or knowingly with respect to causing physical injury. The wrongfulness of the act would be irrelevant. <!-- correct -->

(d) Markov would be acquitted under the mens rea model because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified -- since Markov believed he was defending others from an assassin, his delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated.

(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue that he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (adopted by Idaho, Kansas, Montana, Utah), the traditional insanity defense is abolished. Mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the specific statutory mens rea element. Markov could argue that his psychotic episode prevented him from forming the required intent for assault. But the crucial gap is that Markov deliberately struck Wald -- he intended the physical contact and intended to cause harm. His claim is not that he lacked intent to strike; his claim is that he did not know the strike was wrong. Under the mens rea model, wrongfulness is irrelevant. Option (a) states that abolition states treat mental illness as "entirely irrelevant" and that expert testimony is inadmissible "for any purpose" -- the false proposition is this total exclusion. Abolition states permit mental illness evidence to negate mens rea; they restrict its use, not eliminate it entirely. Option (b) states that *Kahler v. Kansas* "requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" -- the false proposition reverses the actual holding. *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness defense. The mens rea model does no such thing; it permits mental illness evidence *only* to negate a statutory element of the offense. Markov purposely struck Wald and knowingly caused bodily injury -- his delusional belief about *why* he struck (defense of others) does not negate any element of the statute. The mens rea model restricts mental illness evidence to the narrow question of whether the defendant formed the required mental state, not whether the defendant believed the conduct was justified. <!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that if a jurisdiction categorically "abolishes the insanity defense," it completely severs mental disease or defect from the guilt-phase calculus. Therefore, evidence of mental illness would be entirely irrelevant to establishing guilt and purely a matter for sentencing, making expert testimony wholly inadmissible.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that fundamental fairness mandates a threshold of moral culpability to warrant criminal punishment. Following this premise, they might conclude that *Kahler v. Kansas* established a constitutional floor via the Due Process Clause, requiring states to maintain a traditional M'Naghten wrongfulness test to protect defendants who cannot distinguish right from wrong.
(c) Argument-for: This accurately tracks the Supreme Court's ruling in *Kahler v. Kansas* and the defining features of the mens rea model. When the insanity defense is abolished, evidence of mental illness is admissible solely if it is relevant to negating the statutorily required mens rea for the offense (here, acting purposely or knowingly). Since wrongfulness inquiries are abandoned, only the specific cognitive intent to cause physical injury matters.
(d) Argument-for: A student could reason that subjective justification defenses (like an honest belief in the need for self-defense or defense of others) are intrinsically tied to the defendant's mental state. Consequently, if a defendant's mental illness causes an honest, albeit delusional, belief that their conduct was justified, the mens rea model must automatically afford a complete justification defense that operates independently of the statutory elements.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that abolishing the formal "insanity defense" simply means shifting mental illness from an affirmative defense to a standard evidentiary attack on the prosecution's case. Under general rules of relevance, all evidence bearing on criminal responsibility—including an inability to perceive moral wrongfulness—would remain admissible to argue that the defendant lacks the necessary culpability.

Head-to-head: 
Option (c) is the legally precise correct answer, accurately applying the mens rea model where mental illness is strictly funneled into negating statutory mens rea. All distractors provide robust, falsifiable legal claims that lock into clear errors. Option (a) incorrectly claims a total evidentiary ban; Option (b) directly contradicts the holding in *Kahler*; Option (d) incorrectly claims the mens rea model creates a freestanding justification defense divorced from statutory elements; and Option (e) falsely claims abolition states admit wrongfulness evidence.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant... neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose" — wrong because abolition states expressly allow psychiatric evidence to negate specific statutory mens rea elements.
- (b): "the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" — wrong because *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does *not* mandate a wrongfulness defense.
- (d): "the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified... operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated" — wrong because the mens rea model exclusively limits mental illness evidence to negating statutory elements and does not recognize a freestanding justification based on delusions.
- (e): "abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness" — wrong because these states categorically exclude mental illness evidence for the purpose of demonstrating lack of moral wrongfulness.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q09

### Grounding

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: MPC excludes any defendant with a personality disorder diagnosis — wrong because the exclusion applies only to conditions constituting solely a personality disorder
(b) False claim: MPC provides for automatic merger of co-occurring disorders — wrong because no such merger doctrine exists
(c) False claim: Co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies causation — wrong because the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: But-for causation by the personality disorder defeats the defense — wrong because the statute does not impose a sole-cause requirement
-->

### Ambiguity Audit

## The Question

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

(c) False claim: Co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies causation — wrong because the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity

### Edge Case Audit

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. ### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: The "caveat paragraph" of MPC Section 4.01(2) is designed to exclude the "sociopath" or "psychopath" from the insanity defense to prevent character-based criminality from being excused. A proponent could argue that allowing a defendant with a personality disorder diagnosis to raise the defense, even with a co-occurring illness, creates an unacceptable risk that the jury will excuse behavior driven by antisocial traits. To safeguard the policy goal of the exclusion, the rule must be interpreted as an absolute bar for any defendant carrying such a diagnosis, ensuring that the defense is reserved for those whose impairment is untainted by character-based disorders.

(b) Argument-for: Medical science often views co-morbidities not as discrete boxes but as a single, integrated psychiatric profile. In the interest of judicial economy and medical accuracy, a proponent could argue that the MPC implicitly adopts a "merger" doctrine. Under this view, when a qualifying disease (schizophrenia) and a non-qualifying disorder (APD) co-occur, they "automatically merge" into a single legal category of "mental disease or defect." This prevents the prosecution from engaging in the impossible task of disentangling which specific neuron "caused" the violence, treating the defendant's mental state as a unified whole.

(c) Argument-for: Schizophrenia is a severe psychotic disorder that fundamentally disrupts a defendant's cognitive and volitional faculties. A proponent could argue that because psychotic disorders are the "gold standard" for the insanity defense, their presence should "trump" any secondary diagnosis. Under this logic, once schizophrenia is established, it "automatically satisfies" the causation element because a psychotic break is legally presumed to be the primary driver of behavior. This makes the prosecution's focus on the personality disorder "legally irrelevant" because the higher-order qualifying disease dictates the legal outcome.

(d) Argument-for: This is the correct legal standard. MPC 4.01(2) defines what counts as a "mental disease or defect" by exclusion, stating that the term does not include an abnormality manifested "only" by repeated criminal conduct. This is a threshold inquiry. If the defendant has schizophrenia, they have met the threshold for a qualifying disease. The causation question then asks if *that* disease caused the lack of capacity. The law does not require the qualifying disease to be the exclusive cause; as long as it is a sufficient cause of the incapacity, the presence of a contributing personality disorder does not defeat the defense.

(e) Argument-for: In criminal law, causation is typically established using the but-for test. A proponent could argue that for an insanity defense to be valid, the *qualifying* mental disease must be the necessary cause of the criminal act. If the prosecution can demonstrate that the defendant's antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause—meaning the violence would not have occurred without the antisocial traits—then the defense fails. Because the personality disorder is a "non-qualifying" cause, its status as a but-for factor creates an "insurmountable causation problem" that defeats the defense entirely.

Head-to-head: (d) is the strongest because it correctly interprets the word "only" in the MPC text as a threshold gatekeeper rather than a causation-disqualifier. (a) is legally incorrect because the MPC excludes an "abnormality," not a "defendant." (b) is incorrect because there is no "merger" doctrine in the MPC or case law; disorders remain legally distinct for the causation inquiry. (c) is incorrect because causation is always a question of fact for the jury, and no diagnosis "automatically" satisfies it. (e) is incorrect because the MPC does not require the qualifying disease to be the *sole* cause; the test is whether the qualifying disease resulted in the lack of capacity, regardless of other contributing factors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" — wrong because the exclusion applies to abnormalities manifested *solely* by conduct, not to defendants who also have qualifying diseases.
- (b): "MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because no such merger doctrine exists in the MPC or insanity jurisprudence.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because causation remains a distinct element that the defense must prove as a matter of fact.
- (e): "prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause... to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the presence of a contributory non-qualifying cause does not negate the causal impact of a qualifying disease.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that Option (a) is correct by reading the MPC § 4.01(2) "psychopath exception" as an absolute, tainting bar. If a jurisdiction treats an antisocial personality disorder diagnosis as per se evidence of an excluded abnormality manifested by repeated criminal conduct, the presence of that disorder would trigger a complete statutory bar to prevent the "bad rather than mad" from utilizing the defense. Thus, conceding the defeat of the defense is the safest response, regardless of other diagnoses.
(b) Argument-for: Option (b) could be argued as correct by treating co-occurring disorders as inseparable clinical realities. In mental health law, separating the exact causal contribution of intertwined mental conditions is notoriously difficult. A student might argue that under the MPC's holistic assessment of mental disease or defect, comorbid diagnoses automatically merge into a single clinical picture, making the prosecution's attempt to parse the personality disorder out legally invalid.
(c) Argument-for: Option (c) could be defended by asserting that a severe psychotic disorder like schizophrenia operates as a "trump" condition. Under this theory, once a defendant establishes a qualifying severe psychotic disorder, the causation element is presumptively met because psychosis inherently overwhelms other psychological mechanisms. Therefore, the prosecution's argument about the personality disorder driving the behavior is legally irrelevant once the threshold of a severe psychotic disease is passed.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) correctly states the legal standard under MPC § 4.01(2). The MPC provides that the terms "mental disease or defect" do not include an abnormality manifested *only* by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct. Because the statute targets a condition acting *alone*, a co-occurring qualifying disease like schizophrenia allows the defendant to pass the threshold requirement. The defense must then show that the qualifying disease caused the substantial incapacity, which remains a factual question for the jury.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) could be defended by applying strict but-for causation principles to the insanity defense's exclusions. If the antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct, the student might argue that the behavior was inherently "driven" by the excluded condition, thus tainting the causal chain. Under this view, any essential causal contribution by an excluded disorder defeats the defense entirely because the incapacity was not exclusively caused by the qualifying mental disease.

Head-to-head:
Option (d) is unequivocally the strongest argument because it faithfully applies the MPC § 4.01(2) text, which only excludes an abnormality manifested "only" (or "solely") by repeated criminal conduct. The distractors fail because they rely on fabricated absolutes that contradict the MPC framework. Option (a) falsely claims the MPC categorically excludes any defendant with a personality disorder diagnosis. Option (b) invents an "automatic merger" doctrine for co-occurring disorders. Option (c) falsely asserts that a psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, ignoring the ongoing burden to prove causation. Option (e) falsely claims that but-for causation by a personality disorder defeats the defense entirely, whereas the MPC permits the defense to survive if the qualifying disease caused the incapacity, even if other factors contributed.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness" — wrong because the MPC exclusion targets abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct; a co-occurring qualifying illness lifts the threshold bar.
- (b): "the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because no such automatic merger doctrine exists in the MPC.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defense must still independently prove that the qualifying mental disease caused the substantial incapacity.
- (e): "the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the MPC does not impose a sole-cause requirement; concurrent causation by a personality disorder does not legally preclude the defense.

Recommended fix: none

## Q10

### Grounding

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law -- a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family members). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration. The murder bar is a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase: duress is unavailable as a matter of law for any homicide, regardless of the threat's severity, credibility, or the defendant's reasonable firmness. Option (c) applies the MPC rule in a CL jurisdiction and states that "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" -- the false proposition is both the jurisdictional application (Fact 6 specifies common law) and the claim about a universal modern trend (only a small number of MPC-adjacent states allow duress for murder). Option (d) states that "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or close relations. Option (e) states that "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability" in "every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense" -- the false proposition is the universality claim. The felony murder exception to the duress murder bar exists in only a few states (Michigan, Maryland, California, Ohio), not in every jurisdiction, and the question specifies common law, under which the traditional murder bar does not carve out a felony murder exception.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: The CL murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction — wrong because the murder bar is a categorical substantive rule at the guilt phase that makes duress unavailable for any homicide
(b) CORRECT
(c) False claim: MPC rule applies here and modern trend favors universal extension — wrong because Fact 6 specifies CL, and only a small minority allow duress for murder
(d) False claim: CL requires the threat target the defendant personally — wrong because CL extends to close relations
(e) False claim: Duress always breaks felony murder chain in every jurisdiction — wrong because only a few states recognize this exception
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress-murder-bar, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law -- a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family member's). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration. The murder bar is a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase: duress is unavailable as a matter of law for any homicide, regardless of the threat's severity, credibility, or the defendant's reasonable firmness. Option (c) applies the MPC rule in a CL jurisdiction and states that "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" -- the false proposition is both the jurisdictional application (Fact 6 specifies common law) and the claim about a universal modern trend (only a small number of MPC-adjacent states allow duress for murder). Option (d) states that "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or close relations. Option (e) states that "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability" in "every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense" -- the false proposition is the universality claim. The felony murder exception to the duress murder bar exists in only a few states (Michigan, Maryland, California, Ohio), not in every jurisdiction, and the question specifies common law, under which the traditional murder bar does not carve out a felony murder exception.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: The CL murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction — wrong because the murder bar is a categorical substantive rule at the guilt phase that makes duress unavailable for any homicide
(b) CORRECT
(c) False claim: MPC rule applies here and modern trend favors universal extension — wrong because Fact 6 specifies CL, and only a small minority allow duress for murder
(d) False claim: CL requires the threat target the defendant personally — wrong because CL extends to close relations
(e) False claim: Duress always breaks felony murder chain in every jurisdiction — wrong because only a few states recognize this exception
-->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law -- a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family member). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration. The murder bar is a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase: duress is unavailable as a matter of law for any homicide, regardless of the threat's severity, credibility, or the defendant's reasonable firmness. Option (c) applies the MPC rule in a CL jurisdiction and states that "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" -- the false proposition is both the jurisdictional application (Fact 6 specifies common law) and the claim about a universal modern trend (only a small number of MPC-adjacent states allow duress for murder). Option (d) states that "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or close relations. Option (e) states that "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability" in "every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense" -- the false proposition is the universality claim. The felony murder exception to the duress murder bar exists in only a few states (Michigan, Maryland, California, Ohio), not in every jurisdiction, and the question specifies common law, under which the traditional murder bar does not carve out a felony murder exception.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option appeals to the principle of "moral involuntariness," arguing that if a defendant's will is truly overborne by an immediate threat to a loved one, they lack the necessary culpability for a murder conviction. A student might argue that the historical "murder bar" is an archaic relic that functions more like a sentencing guideline to prevent jury nullification, rather than a substantive element of the crime, especially in modern jurisdictions where "reasonableness" is a jury question. Under this view, Holmes should be allowed to present the evidence of the threat at the guilt phase to contest the element of malice.

(b) Argument-for: This is the classic common law position. Blackstone and Hale famously asserted that a person must choose to die themselves rather than take the life of an innocent third party. Because Fact 6 specifies the common law applies, the bar to using duress as a defense for homicide is absolute. This categorical rule prioritizes the preservation of life and maintains a clear moral line that the law refuses to cross, regardless of the severity of the pressure placed on the defendant's will.

(c) Argument-for: This option follows the logic of the Model Penal Code § 2.09, which does not exclude murder from the duress defense. A student might argue that the "common law" is not a static set of rules but evolves through "modern trends." Given that many jurisdictions are adopting MPC-style reforms, one could argue that the modern common law now favors a unified standard for duress that applies to all crimes, ensuring that defendants are only punished for acts reflecting a choice they were actually capable of making.

(d) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the historical restrictive nature of common law defenses. Early common law strictly limited duress to "fear of life and limb" of the actor themselves. By arguing that the threat must be personal to the defendant, this option presents a narrow, formalistic interpretation of the defense. A student might believe that the common law prioritized the immediate physical integrity of the person being coerced over the safety of third parties, even close relatives like a mother.

(e) Argument-for: This argument utilizes the "merger" or "derivative liability" theory of felony murder. If Holmes was coerced into the predicate assault, his lack of criminal intent for that assault should theoretically prevent that intent from being "transferred" to the resulting death. Some jurisdictions, like Maryland, have held that duress can be a defense to felony murder if it is a defense to the underlying felony. This logic suggests that if the foundation of the felony murder charge (the felony) is excused, the entire liability chain must collapse.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the strongest because it correctly identifies the specific, categorical prohibition on duress for homicide that defines common law jurisprudence. Option (a) fails because it incorrectly characterizes a substantive bar to a defense as a sentencing limitation. Option (c) fails by applying MPC standards to a common law prompt and overstating the "modern trend" as universal. Option (d) fails because the common law generally expanded to include threats against immediate family members. Option (e) fails because it asserts a universal rule ("always" and "every jurisdiction") for a felony-murder exception that is actually a minority rule and conflicts with the traditional common law murder bar.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration... not a substantive bar at the guilt phase" — wrong because the common law murder bar is a substantive rule that makes the defense unavailable at trial for the crime of murder.
- (c): "modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because most U.S. jurisdictions still maintain the common law or statutory bar against duress for murder.
- (d): "common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law standard for duress includes threats to the defendant's immediate family.
- (e): "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability... in every jurisdiction" — wrong because many jurisdictions hold that the murder bar extends to felony murder, and it is certainly not a universal rule in every jurisdiction.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law -- a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family member's). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration. The murder bar is a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase: duress is unavailable as a matter of law for any homicide, regardless of the threat's severity, credibility, or the defendant's reasonable firmness. Option (c) applies the MPC rule in a CL jurisdiction and states that "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" -- the false proposition is both the jurisdictional application (Fact 6 specifies common law) and the claim about a universal modern trend (only a small number of MPC-adjacent states allow duress for murder). Option (d) states that "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or close relations. Option (e) states that "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability" in "every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense" -- the false proposition is the universality claim. The felony murder exception to the duress murder bar exists in only a few states (Michigan, Maryland, California, Ohio), not in every jurisdiction, and the question specifies common law, under which the traditional murder bar does not carve out a felony murder exception.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: The CL murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction — wrong because the murder bar is a categorical substantive rule at the guilt phase that makes duress unavailable for any homicide
(b) CORRECT
(c) False claim: MPC rule applies here and modern trend favors universal extension — wrong because Fact 6 specifies CL, and only a small minority allow duress for murder
