**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that modern criminal law has shifted the function of duress in murder cases from a complete substantive defense to a mitigating factor, similar to imperfect self-defense. In this view, the "murder bar" might be characterized strictly as a sentencing-phase consideration (e.g., barring the death penalty) rather than an absolute guilt-phase bar. If a jurisdiction treats duress solely as a sentencing mitigator for homicide, this option would be theoretically defensible.
(b) Argument-for: This is the classic common law rule. Blackstone established that a person under duress "ought rather to die himself than escape by the murder of an innocent." Thus, under traditional common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a substantive defense to murder, no matter how severe the threat to the defendant or their family. This makes the defense invalid as a matter of law.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that the common law is judge-made and continuously evolves, often incorporating the Model Penal Code. Under MPC Section 2.09, there is no carve-out for murder—duress applies to all crimes. If the student believes the "modern trend" universally absorbs the MPC approach, they might conclude Holmes has a valid defense here.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that early, strict interpretations of the common law required the coercive threat to be directed at the defendant personally to ensure the immediacy and directness of the coercion. Under this narrow historical view, a threat directed at a third party—even a close relative like a mother—would categorically fail the strict standing requirement for a duress claim.
(e) Argument-for: A student could rely on the logic of derivative liability. Felony murder requires a valid conviction for the underlying predicate felony. If the underlying assault is completely excused by duress, the predicate felony vanishes, ostensibly breaking the chain of felony murder liability. A student could plausibly argue that basic principles of criminal law require this logical chain to break in "every jurisdiction," validating the defense.

Head-to-head: (b) is the clear winner as it accurately states the traditional common law rule: duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to intentional murder. Distractor (a) introduces the explicitly false claim that the murder bar is merely a sentencing-phase consideration, contradicting centuries of substantive common law. Distractor (c) incorrectly imports the MPC into a common law question and relies on a fabricated "universal" modern trend. Distractor (d) contains the explicitly false premise that the common law strictly requires the threat to target the defendant personally; the common law has long recognized threats to close family members. Distractor (e) fails due to its absolutist claim that duress "always" breaks the felony murder chain in "every jurisdiction," which ignores states that apply the murder bar to felony murder regardless of whether the predicate is excused.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction... not a substantive bar at the guilt phase" — wrong because the common law murder bar is a categorical substantive bar at the guilt phase.
- (c): "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because only a small minority of jurisdictions allow duress as a defense to murder.
- (d): "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" — wrong because common law doctrine extends the duress defense to threats of death or serious bodily injury made against the defendant's close relatives.
- (e): "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability... in every jurisdiction" — wrong because this is a minority approach; traditional common law jurisdictions maintain the murder bar even against felony murder charges.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q11

### Grounding

**Q11.** Evaluate Kant's necessity defense to criminal mischief and unauthorized possession charges stemming from the Green Street Pharmacy incident, under both common-law ("clearly outweigh") and Model Penal Code ("greater than") proportionality standards.

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because federal scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim involving self-acquisition of a controlled substance.
(b) The defense fails under the common law because Kant cannot show that no person of ordinary firmness would have tolerated the risk of psychosis, and that objective-firmness test is the touchstone of the "clearly outweigh" inquiry.
(c) The defense is likely viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break substantially outweighs the property damage of the broken window and the regulatory harm of unauthorized possession, and the fact that an ER bed would not be available for at least 48 hours—offering no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired—supports the no-reasonable-legal-alternative element. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically bars necessity whenever the defendant contributed in any way to creating the crisis, and Kant allowed his prescription to run out.
(e) The defense is available under the Model Penal Code's "greater than" standard but fails under the common-law "clearly outweigh" standard, because "clearly outweigh" requires the averted harm to be a homicide or an equivalent felony against persons.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly applies both proportionality frameworks: the threatened harm (acute psychotic episode) substantially exceeds the property destruction (broken window) and regulatory harms of unauthorized possession. The no-reasonable-alternative element is supported by the closure of Dr. Singer's office, insurance denial, and the lack of available ER psychiatric beds for at least 48 hours, which offered no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired. (a) falsely overreads *Oakland Cannabis*: that case held legislative preemption forecloses the defense only where the legislature explicitly weighed the competing harms for the specific conduct. (b) imports the duress "person of ordinary firmness" standard; necessity compares harms, not firmness. (d) misstates Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea (defense fails only if fault in creating the crisis is at least as culpable as the offense's mens rea)—not a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a rule: "clearly outweigh" compares the magnitude of harms, not their categorical type.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Necessity/Duress, Ch 21, Refinement `necessity-proportionality-standard`, `legislative-preemption-absolute`, `mpc-fault-calibration`, `reasonable-firmness-objective`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q11.** Evaluate Kant's necessity defense to criminal mischief and unauthorized possession charges stemming from the Green Street Pharmacy incident, under both common-law ("clearly outweigh") and Model Penal Code ("greater than") proportionality standards.

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because federal scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim involving self-acquisition of a controlled substance.

(c) The defense is likely viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break substantially outweighs the property damage of the broken window and the regulatory harm of unauthorized possession, and the fact that an ER bed would not be available for at least 48 hours—offering no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired—supports the no-reasonable-legal-alternative element. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically bars necessity whenever the defendant contributed in any way to creating the crisis, and Kant allowed his prescription to run out.
(e) The defense is available under the Model Penal Code's "greater than" standard but fails under the common-law "clearly outweigh" standard, because "clearly outweigh" requires the averted harm to be a homicide or an equivalent felony against persons.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly applies both proportionality frameworks: the threatened harm (acute psychotic episode) substantially exceeds the property destruction (broken window) and regulatory harms of unauthorized possession. (a) falsely overreads *Oakland Cannabis*: that case held legislative preemption forecloses the defense only where the legislature explicitly weighed the competing harms for the specific conduct. (b) imports the duress "person of ordinary firmness" standard; necessity compares harms, not firmness. (d) misstates Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea (defense fails only if fault in creating the crisis is at least as culpable as the offense's mens rea)—not a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a rule: "clearly outweigh" compares the magnitude of harms, not their categorical type.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

check 1: finding (Fails accuracy) - Option (c) claims the defense is "likely viable under both standards". However, under the traditional common-law 5-element test (noted in the Grounding), a defendant is categorically barred from the necessity defense if they were at fault in creating the emergency. Option (d) establishes that "Kant allowed his prescription to run out." If Kant's negligence created the crisis, a well-prepared student would correctly recognize that the CL defense fails entirely, making (c) incorrect.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q11.** Evaluate Kant's necessity defense to criminal mischief and unauthorized possession charges stemming from the Green Street Pharmacy incident, under both common-law ("clearly outweigh") and Model Penal Code ("greater than") proportionality standards.

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because federal scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim involving self-acquisition of a controlled substance.
(b) The defense fails under the common law because Kant cannot show that no person of ordinary firmness would have tolerated the risk of psychosis, and that objective-firmness test is the touchstone of the "clearly outweigh" inquiry.
(c) The defense is likely viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break substantially outweighs the property damage of the broken window and the regulatory harm of unauthorized possession, and the fact that an ER bed would not be available for at least 48 hours—offering no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired—supports the no-reasonable-legal-alternative element. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically bars necessity whenever the defendant contributed in any way to creating the crisis, and Kant allowed his prescription to run out.
(e) The defense is available under the Model Penal Code's "greater than" standard but fails under the common-law "clearly outweigh" standard, because "clearly outweigh" requires the averted harm to be a homicide or an equivalent felony against persons.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly applies both proportionality frameworks: the threatened harm (acute psychotic episode) substantially exceeds the property destruction (broken window) and regulatory harms of unauthorized possession. The no-reasonable-alternative element is supported by the closure of Dr. Singer's office, insurance denial, and the lack of available ER psychiatric beds for at least 48 hours, which offered no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired. (a) falsely overreads *Oakland Cannabis*: that case held legislative preemption forecloses the defense only where the legislature explicitly weighed the competing harms for the specific conduct. (b) imports the duress "person of ordinary firmness" standard; necessity compares harms, not firmness. (d) misstates Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea (defense fails only if fault in creating the crisis is at least as culpable as the offense's mens rea)—not a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a rule: "clearly outweigh" compares the magnitude of harms, not their categorical type.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- edge-case-audit: CLEAN
1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: pass (the prompt smartly limits the charges to criminal mischief and possession, avoiding a trap where leaving the $240 cash would negate the intent to permanently deprive for a larceny charge)

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q11.** Evaluate Kant's necessity defense to criminal mischief and unauthorized possession charges stemming from the Green Street Pharmacy incident, under both common-law ("clearly outweigh") and Model Penal Code ("greater than") proportionality standards.

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because federal scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim involving self-acquisition of a controlled substance.
(b) The defense fails under the common law because Kant cannot show that no person of ordinary firmness would have tolerated the risk of psychosis, and that objective-firmness test is the touchstone of the "clearly outweigh" inquiry.
(c) The defense is likely viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break substantially outweighs the property damage of the broken window and the regulatory harm of unauthorized possession, and the fact that an ER bed would not be available for at least 48 hours—offering no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired—supports the no-reasonable-legal-alternative element.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically bars necessity whenever the defendant contributed in any way to creating the crisis, and Kant allowed his prescription to run out.
(e) The defense is available under the Model Penal Code's "greater than" standard but fails under the common-law "clearly outweigh" standard, because "clearly outweigh" requires the averted harm to be a homicide or an equivalent felony against persons.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly applies both proportionality frameworks: the threatened harm (acute psychotic episode) substantially exceeds the property destruction (broken window) and regulatory harms of unauthorized possession. The no-reasonable-alternative element is supported by the closure of Dr. Singer's office, insurance denial, and the lack of available ER psychiatric beds for at least 48 hours, which offered no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired. (a) falsely overreads *Oakland Cannabis*: that case held legislative preemption forecloses the defense only where the legislature explicitly weighed the competing harms for the specific conduct. (b) imports the duress "person of ordinary firmness" standard; necessity compares harms, not firmness. (d) misstates Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea (defense fails only if fault in creating the crisis is at least as culpable as the offense's mens rea)—not a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a rule: "clearly outweigh" compares the magnitude of harms, not their categorical type.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

```
<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative established a strict limit on the necessity defense when a legislative scheme has already addressed the balance of harms. By placing prescription drugs within a strictly regulated scheduling system, Congress has signaled that the controlled distribution of these substances is a paramount interest. Under this view, self-help acquisition of a controlled substance is always preempted because the legislature has already determined the exclusive lawful methods of possession. Therefore, no judicial necessity inquiry is permitted.

(b) Argument-for: Under the common law, the necessity defense is strictly objective and intended to apply only when the pressure of circumstances is so overwhelming that the actor’s choice is socially vindicated. One could argue that the "clearly outweigh" standard functions as a proxy for the duress-like requirement that no person of ordinary firmness would have chosen to obey the law in that moment. If a person of ordinary firmness could have waited the 48 hours for an ER bed despite the risk, the defense must fail. This ensures that necessity remains an exceptional justification rather than a subjective excuse.

(c) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies that necessity (choice of evils) hinges on a comparative valuation of harms. A psychotic break is a profound injury to the person, involving potential violence, loss of liberty through civil commitment, and severe mental suffering; this clearly outweighs the minor economic harm of a broken window and the regulatory violation of possession. Furthermore, the defense requires the absence of a reasonable legal alternative, which is satisfied here because the 48-hour ER wait offered no certainty of treatment within the critical 72-hour window.

(d) Argument-for: Model Penal Code § 3.02 is often interpreted as being more restrictive regarding a defendant's prior fault than the common law. A student might argue that the MPC's "choice of evils" policy is unavailable to anyone who creates their own necessity. Since Kant allowed his prescription to run out, he "contributed" to the crisis. If § 3.02 is read to contain an absolute bar for any degree of fault in creating the situation, the defense would be unavailable regardless of the proportionality of the harms.

(e) Argument-for: There is a recognized doctrinal distinction between the MPC’s "greater than" standard and the common law’s "clearly outweigh" standard. A student could argue that "clearly outweigh" is a heightened burden that requires a massive disparity in the nature of the harms. Under this interpretation, property crimes (mischief) can only be justified to prevent crimes of extreme violence or death. Because a psychotic break is a medical/psychological condition rather than a "homicide or equivalent felony against persons," it might satisfy the MPC's lower bar but fail the common law's more rigid categorical hierarchy.

Head-to-head: (c) is the strongest answer because it accurately reflects the "harm-balancing" nature of necessity and the "fault-calibration" rule of the MPC. Option (a) fails because Oakland Cannabis specifically addressed Schedule I drugs (where the legislature found "no medical use"), whereas the medications in a pharmacy are recognized as having medical utility. Option (b) fails because it confuses the necessity standard (harm-balancing) with the duress standard (person of ordinary firmness). Option (d) fails because the MPC § 3.02(2) only bars the defense if the defendant's fault matches the offense's mens rea, not for any contribution. Option (e) fails because the common law "clearly outweigh" standard is a relative comparison, not a categorical limitation to homicide prevention.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "federal scheduling... constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim..." — wrong because Oakland Cannabis only found preemption where the legislature had specifically excluded the "medical necessity" in question (e.g., Schedule I's "no medical use" finding).
- (b): "that objective-firmness test is the touchstone of the 'clearly outweigh' inquiry." — wrong because "ordinary firmness" is the standard for duress, whereas necessity is an objective balancing of the harms of the act vs. the harms averted.
- (d): "§ 3.02 categorically bars necessity whenever the defendant contributed in any way to creating the crisis" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) uses a fault-calibration rule where the defense is only barred if the defendant was reckless or negligent in creating the situation and the underlying crime has a similar mens rea requirement.
- (e): " 'clearly outweigh' requires the averted harm to be a homicide or an equivalent felony against persons." — wrong because the common law proportionality test compares the magnitude of any recognized harms (life vs. property), and is not categorically limited to preventing homicides.

Recommended fix: none
-->
```

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q11.** Evaluate Kant's necessity defense to criminal mischief and unauthorized possession charges stemming from the Green Street Pharmacy incident, under both common-law ("clearly outweigh") and Model Penal Code ("greater than") proportionality standards.

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because federal scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim involving self-acquisition of a controlled substance.
(b) The defense fails under the common law because Kant cannot show that no person of ordinary firmness would have tolerated the risk of psychosis, and that objective-firmness test is the touchstone of the "clearly outweigh" inquiry.
(c) The defense is likely viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break substantially outweighs the property damage of the broken window and the regulatory harm of unauthorized possession, and the fact that an ER bed would not be available for at least 48 hours—offering no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired—supports the no-reasonable-legal-alternative element. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically bars necessity whenever the defendant contributed in any way to creating the crisis, and Kant allowed his prescription to run out.
(e) The defense is available under the Model Penal Code's "greater than" standard but fails under the common-law "clearly outweigh" standard, because "clearly outweigh" requires the averted harm to be a homicide or an equivalent felony against persons.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly applies both proportionality frameworks: the threatened harm (acute psychotic episode) substantially exceeds the property destruction (broken window) and regulatory harms of unauthorized possession. The no-reasonable-alternative element is supported by the closure of Dr. Singer's office, insurance denial, and the lack of available ER psychiatric beds for at least 48 hours, which offered no guarantee of treatment before the 72-hour risk window expired. (a) falsely overreads *Oakland Cannabis*: that case held legislative preemption forecloses the defense only where the legislature explicitly weighed the competing harms for the specific conduct. (b) imports the duress "person of ordinary firmness" standard; necessity compares harms, not firmness. (d) misstates Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea (defense fails only if fault in creating the crisis is at least as culpable as the offense's mens rea)—not a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a rule: "clearly outweigh" compares the magnitude of harms, not their categorical type.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might select this option by reasoning that *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* established that the Controlled Substances Act precludes medical necessity defenses for scheduled drugs. Since prescription drug distribution is strictly regulated, a student could conclude that Congress has already balanced the harms, thereby preempting any ad hoc necessity claim for self-help acquisition of a scheduled substance.
(b) Argument-for: A student could plausibly believe that the objective "person of ordinary firmness" standard from duress also applies to the common-law necessity defense. Because necessity requires an objective balancing of harms ("clearly outweigh"), a student might argue that the touchstone of this inquiry is whether a reasonable person of ordinary firmness would have tolerated the threatened harm without resorting to a crime.
(c) Argument-for: This is the keyed answer. A student would recognize that avoiding an acute psychotic break is a significant harm that substantially outweighs the minor property damage of a broken window and the regulatory violation of unauthorized possession. Furthermore, the unavailability of an ER bed for at least 48 hours establishes the "no adequate legal alternative" element, rendering the defense viable under both common-law and MPC standards.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that under some strict common-law formulations, the defendant must be totally without fault in bringing about the situation. The student could incorrectly assume the Model Penal Code codified this strict approach in § 3.02, interpreting Kant's failure to refill his prescription as a disqualifying contribution that categorically bars the defense.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that the common-law "clearly outweigh" standard is extremely strict compared to the MPC's "greater than" standard. Believing that common-law necessity historically existed primarily to save lives in absolute emergencies, a student might conclude that it is only available when the averted harm is of the highest magnitude, such as a homicide or equivalent serious felony against a person.

Head-to-head: Option (c) is the only legally accurate answer, properly applying the balancing of harms and the absence of a reasonable legal alternative under both the common-law and MPC necessity frameworks. The distractors are securely locked with explicit, falsifiable legal errors. Option (a) falsely expands the *Oakland Cannabis* legislative preemption rule (which applied specifically to a Schedule I drug with a congressional finding of no medical use) into a categorical bar for all scheduled prescription drugs. Option (b) explicitly but incorrectly imports the "ordinary firmness" test from duress into the necessity proportionality analysis. Option (d) provides a verifiably false statement of MPC doctrine, as § 3.02(2) does not categorically bar the defense for any fault, but rather limits it based on the defendant's mens rea in creating the situation. Option (e) fabricates a rigid categorical rule for the common law, falsely claiming the averted harm must be a homicide or equivalent felony.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "federal scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim" — wrong because *Oakland Cannabis* relied on the specific classification of marijuana as a Schedule I drug (which mandates a finding of no accepted medical use), rather than creating a categorical preemption for all scheduled prescription drugs or all necessity claims.
- (b): "objective-firmness test is the touchstone of the 'clearly outweigh' inquiry" — wrong because the "person of ordinary firmness" is the standard for duress, whereas necessity objectively evaluates the proportion of the competing harms regardless of psychological firmness.
- (d): "§ 3.02 categorically bars necessity whenever the defendant contributed in any way to creating the crisis" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) does not categorically bar the defense for any contribution; it only strips the defense for crimes requiring recklessness or negligence if the actor was reckless or negligent in bringing about the situation.
- (e): "'clearly outweigh' requires the averted harm to be a homicide or an equivalent felony against persons" — wrong because the common law imposes no such categorical limitation on the type of harm averted, so long as the harm averted clearly outweighs the harm caused.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q12

### Grounding

**Q12.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Evaluate Kant's duress defense to the armed robbery charge under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law (given Bentham's documented pattern of ordering hospitalizations with surgical repair) and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies both element rules. The common law requires "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating through Jarvis—documented to involve broken limbs and surgical repair—qualifies as grievous bodily harm, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a close relation. The Model Penal Code sets a lower threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), plainly satisfied here. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope: it expressly extends to "close relations," including a parent. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the Model Penal Code; § 2.09 drops strict imminence, though it remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) falsely elevates a fact-bound reasonableness inquiry into a matter of law—whether reporting would be reasonable is a jury question.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duress, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-threat-threshold` and `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

**Q12.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. ```
<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies that Bentham’s threats meet the threshold for duress under both legal frameworks. Under the common law, "serious bodily injury" is the standard, and Bentham's history of inflicting injuries requiring "surgical repair" (like broken limbs) clearly satisfies this. The Model Penal Code (MPC) utilizes a broader "unlawful force" standard, which Bentham's beating certainly entails. Furthermore, both standards recognize threats to third parties: the common law includes "close relations" like a mother, while the MPC includes "any person," making (a) the most legally accurate application of the facts.

(b) Argument-for: This option rests on a traditionalist, "strict" interpretation of common-law duress. Historically, many jurisdictions required the threat to be directed at the defendant’s own person to justify the commission of a crime. By arguing that the common law "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally," a student could suggest that expanding the defense to include family members is a modern statutory or judicial innovation rather than the "true" common-law rule. If the court adheres to this narrowest possible construction, the threat to Kant's mother would be legally irrelevant.

(c) Argument-for: In many jurisdictions, even those influenced by the Model Penal Code, the lack of imminence is fatal to a duress defense. This argument posits that because Bentham’s threat was for future retaliation rather than an immediate, present application of force, it fails the threshold. While MPC § 2.09 does not explicitly use the word "imminence," many courts interpret the "person of reasonable firmness" standard as implicitly requiring imminence, because an ordinary person would seek police protection if the threat were not immediate. Thus, the "threshold" is not met because the threat lacks the temporal urgency required to overbear a reasonable person's will.

(d) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the high "death or serious bodily injury" bar of the common law. It characterizes the common law as requiring an extreme "gun-to-the-head" scenario of lethal force to excuse an armed robbery. By contrasting this with "mere verbal threats of a beating," the argument suggests that unless the actor is facing an immediate life-or-death situation, the common law does not provide an excuse. Since the MPC only requires "unlawful force," it creates a disparity where the MPC would allow the defense but the common law would not.

(e) Argument-for: This argument appeals to the "no reasonable alternative" component inherent in the duress defense. If Bentham made threats prior to March 15, Kant arguably had an opportunity to contact law enforcement or flee. A person of "reasonable firmness" is generally expected to exhaust legal avenues before resorting to crime. If a court determines that the opportunity to report was so clear that no reasonable jury could find otherwise, it can rule the defense insufficient "as a matter of law," preventing the issue from reaching the jury entirely.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is the strongest because it correctly identifies the legal thresholds (SBI vs. unlawful force) and the target scope (close relations vs. any person) as they are actually applied in modern criminal law pedagogy. Option (b) is incorrect because the common law has long recognized "close relations" as valid targets. Option (c) is incorrect because the MPC’s hallmark change from the common law was the removal of the strict imminence requirement. Option (d) is incorrect because "serious bodily injury" (broken limbs requiring surgery) has always satisfied the common law; it does not require a "gun-to-the-head." Option (e) is incorrect because the "reasonable firmness" and "opportunity to escape" inquiries are almost always questions of fact for the jury, not determinations made "as a matter of law" when there is a credible threat of surgical-level violence.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because common law target scope extends to close relations.
- (c): "Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 intentionally omits the word "imminent" to broaden the defense.
- (d): "mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury" — wrong because a beating that results in broken limbs and surgical repair is a textbook definition of serious bodily injury.
- (e): "rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law" — wrong because the reasonableness of failing to report a high-level threat is a factual question for the jury, not a legal bar.

Recommended fix: none
-->
```

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q12.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Evaluate Kant's duress defense to the armed robbery charge under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law (given Bentham's documented pattern of ordering hospitalizations with surgical repair) and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies both element rules. The common law requires "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating through Jarvis—documented to involve broken limbs and surgical repair—qualifies as grievous bodily harm, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a close relation. The Model Penal Code sets a lower threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), plainly satisfied here. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope: it expressly extends to "close relations," including a parent. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the Model Penal Code; § 2.09 drops strict imminence, though it remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) falsely elevates a fact-bound reasonableness inquiry into a matter of law—whether reporting would be reasonable is a jury question.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) accurately applies the black-letter rules for duress under both the common law and the Model Penal Code. The common law requires a threat of death or serious bodily injury; a documented history of beatings requiring surgical repair clearly meets this threshold. Additionally, the common law extends the target scope to close relatives, such as a mother. The Model Penal Code further broadens both elements, requiring only a threat of "unlawful force" against "any person," which is straightforwardly satisfied here.
(b) Argument-for: A student might choose (b) by relying on the strictest historical formulation of the common-law duress defense, which originally demanded that the threat be directed solely at the defendant. While modern common law has expanded the target scope, a student might argue that the foundational common-law rule strictly limits cognizable threats to the defendant's own person, making threats against third parties, including a mother, categorically insufficient.
(c) Argument-for: A student could defend (c) by arguing that imminence is practically inherent in the concept of a legally recognizable threat. They might reason that the Model Penal Code, while relaxing certain common-law strictures, still fundamentally requires a threat to be imminent to trigger the "person of reasonable firmness" standard. Under this view, the MPC functionally retains the common-law imminence requirement because future retaliation cannot create immediate compulsion.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) can be defended by pointing to the typical factual scenarios that successfully raise common-law duress, which almost universally involve immediate, deadly peril (the classic "gun to the head"). A student might argue that the common-law threshold is interpreted so strictly that a mere verbal threat of a beating falls short of the immediate, lethal force required to excuse a serious felony, whereas the MPC's "unlawful force" threshold is lower.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue for (e) based on the principle that the duress defense is unavailable if the defendant had a reasonable opportunity to seek official protection. Since the facts imply time passed before March 15, a student could conclude that the failure to notify the police defeats the defense pre-trial. They might argue that under the MPC's "reasonable firmness" test, a failure to report when an ordinary person would have done so destroys the defense as a matter of law.

Head-to-head: Option (a) correctly synthesizes the doctrine, recognizing that both standards' thresholds and scope elements are met. The distractors all utilize explicit, falsifiable misstatements of law. Option (b) falsely claims the common law limits threats to the defendant personally, ignoring the universal "close relation" exception. Option (c) explicitly and falsely states the MPC retains the common-law imminence requirement, contradicting MPC § 2.09. Option (d) manufactures a strict "gun-to-the-head" requirement for the common law, whereas any threat of serious bodily injury suffices. Option (e) falsely claims that a failure to report to the police renders the threat insufficient "as a matter of law" under the MPC's reasonable firmness test, whereas the MPC delegates this reasonableness determination to the jury.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly extends the target scope to include close relations like a mother.
- (c): "the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 deliberately abandons the strict imminence requirement.
- (d): "the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" — wrong because a threat of serious bodily injury (not just immediate deadly force) is sufficient under the common law.
- (e): "rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law" under the "reasonable firmness" test — wrong because under the MPC, whether a person of reasonable firmness would have reported to police is a question of fact for the jury, not a categorical bar as a matter of law.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q13

### Grounding

**Q13.** Assume for this question that Kant satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, and that it is established that Bentham (not Kant) fired the shot that killed Bayes (Fact 7). The State charges Kant with felony murder for Bayes's death, with the armed robbery as the predicate. Evaluate duress as a defense to the felony-murder charge.

(a) Duress is available for felony murder under both the common law and the Model Penal Code, because *United States v. Contento-Pachon* recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat of death.

(b) Duress is unavailable for felony murder under both the common law and the Model Penal Code, because both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any killing that occurs during the commission of a predicate felony.

(c) Although duress is unavailable, Kant may instead assert necessity under Model Penal Code § 3.02's greater-evils balancing, because avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death.

(d) Duress is available for felony murder under the Reichard/McMillan exception only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm; because the armed-car robbery inherently involves firearms, the exception is categorically foreclosed for any defendant who participated in such a robbery.

(e) Duress is unavailable for felony murder at common law under the traditional rule that extends the murder bar to killings committed during a predicate felony; however, the Michigan/Maryland Reichard/McMillan exception makes duress available for felony murder where the defense is available for the predicate felony, and Kant — a non-killer participant whose only link to Bayes's death was driving the van — fits that exception cleanly. Under Model Penal Code § 2.09, duress is available for felony murder because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder. However, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception permits duress for felony murder if the defense excuses the predicate felony and the defendant was a non-killer participant. Given the prompt's stipulation that duress is met for the robbery, Kant fits this exception cleanly. Under MPC § 2.09, duress is available because it lacks a homicide exception. (a) is incorrect; *Contento-Pachon* involved drug trafficking, not homicide, and did not disturb the murder bar. (b) incorrectly applies the common-law murder bar to the MPC, which has no such bar. (c) incorrectly invokes necessity; avoiding injury is not a greater evil than a human death, and the common law bars necessity for homicide. (d) invents an ignorance-of-armament requirement; *Reichard* allowed the defense even though the defendant knew her co-felon was armed.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; *People v. Reichard*, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); *McMillan v. State*, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress murder bar and felony-murder exception, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar` and `felony-murder-duress-exception`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

check 1: pass
check 2: finding. A well-prepared student could object to (e)'s premise that one can be charged with "felony murder" under the Model Penal Code. The MPC famously rejected strict-liability felony murder, replacing it with a rebuttable presumption of extreme recklessness/indifference (MPC § 210.2(1)(b)). Calling it "felony murder" under the MPC in the correct answer could cause high-performing students to eliminate (e) as a trick.

In Option (e), revise the final sentence to say "Under Model Penal Code § 2.09, duress is available for murder..." rather than "felony murder" to avoid friction with the MPC's abolition of traditional felony murder.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q13.** Assume for this question that Kant satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, and that it is established that Bentham (not Kant) fired the shot that killed Bayes (Fact 7). The State charges Kant with felony murder for Bayes's death, with the armed robbery as the predicate. Evaluate duress as a defense to the felony-murder charge.

(a) Duress is available for felony murder under both the common law and the Model Penal Code, because *United States v. Contento-Pachon* recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat of death.

(b) Duress is unavailable for felony murder under both the common law and the Model Penal Code, because both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any killing that occurs during the commission of a predicate felony.

(c) Although duress is unavailable, Kant may instead assert necessity under Model Penal Code § 3.02's greater-evils balancing, because avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death.

(d) Duress is available for felony murder under the Reichard/McMillan exception only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm; because the armed-car robbery inherently involves firearms, the exception is categorically foreclosed for any defendant who participated in such a robbery.

(e) Duress is unavailable for felony murder at common law under the traditional rule that extends the murder bar to killings committed during a predicate felony; however, the Michigan/Maryland Reichard/McMillan exception makes duress available for felony murder where the defense is available for the predicate felony, and Kant — a non-killer participant whose only link to Bayes's death was driving the van — fits that exception cleanly. Under Model Penal Code § 2.09, duress is available for felony murder because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder. However, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception permits duress for felony murder if the defense excuses the predicate felony and the defendant was a non-killer participant. Given the prompt's stipulation that duress is met for the robbery, Kant fits this exception cleanly. Under MPC § 2.09, duress is available because it lacks a homicide exception. (a) is incorrect; *Contento-Pachon* involved drug trafficking, not homicide, and did not disturb the murder bar. (b) incorrectly applies the common-law murder bar to the MPC, which has no such bar. (c) incorrectly invokes necessity; avoiding injury is not a greater evil than a human death, and the common law bars necessity for homicide. (d) invents an ignorance-of-armament requirement; *Reichard* allowed the defense even though the defendant knew her co-felon was armed.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; *People v. Reichard*, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); *McMillan v. State*, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that *United States v. Contento-Pachon* signals a modernization of the common law, moving away from rigid categorical bars toward a functional assessment of whether a defendant's will was overborne. By focusing on the "triad" of immediacy, fear, and lack of escape, the case suggests that the nature of the crime is less important than the psychological reality of the threat. Under this expansive reading, the common law effectively recognizes a "universal" exception for any crime committed under a threat of death, as no person can be expected to act as a hero when their own life is at stake.

(b) Argument-for: This option relies on the "Reasonable Firmness" standard found in MPC § 2.09. A student might argue that even though the MPC does not contain an explicit homicide bar, the standard is objective: the threat must be such that a "person of reasonable firmness" would be unable to resist. One could argue that as a matter of law, no person of reasonable firmness would participate in a dangerous felony that results in the loss of an innocent life, regardless of the threat to themselves. Consequently, both the common law and the MPC frameworks "categorically" bar the defense for felony murder by defining the standard in a way that excludes homicide.

(c) Argument-for: Under MPC § 3.02, the necessity defense is governed by a pure balancing of harms rather than the "overborne will" of duress. A student could argue that the MPC does not explicitly bar homicide from this balancing test. If Kant believed that Bentham’s threat to hospitalize him or harm his mother constituted a significant harm that, when combined with the threat to his own safety, outweighed the unintended death of a stranger (Bayes) during a robbery, he could theoretically argue that his choice was the "lesser" evil. This shifts the focus from the murder bar to a subjective-objective evaluation of the relative value of the lives and safety involved.

(d) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the "voluntary placement" limitation found in both common law and modern statutes. Even if the *Reichard/McMillan* exception exists, a student could argue it is strictly limited to defendants who did not foresee the potential for lethal violence. By agreeing to participate in an "armed-car robbery," Kant arguably assumed the risk that firearms would be used. Therefore, one could defend the claim that the exception is "categorically foreclosed" for any defendant who enters into a conspiracy involving weapons, as they have recklessly placed themselves in a situation where homicide was a predictable outcome.

(e) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the jurisdictional split and the nuances of the "murder bar." At traditional common law, duress cannot excuse murder, and this extends to felony murder because the intent to commit the felony provides the "malice" for the death. However, modern cases like *Reichard* (Michigan) and *McMillan* (Maryland) allow the defense if the defendant was not the actual killer and the duress excuses the predicate felony. Furthermore, the MPC § 2.09 is distinct because it lacks the traditional common law homicide exception, making the defense available for any crime provided the "reasonable firmness" test is met.

Head-to-head: (e) is the strongest choice because it accurately identifies that the MPC § 2.09 lacks a homicide bar, whereas (b) falsely claims the MPC categorically bars the defense for killings. (a) is weak because *Contento-Pachon* is a drug-trafficking case that did not establish a "universal" exception to the centuries-old murder bar. (c) fails because, under any reasonable MPC § 3.02 balancing, the death of an innocent (Bayes) is a greater evil than the "hospitalization" of the defendant. (d) is incorrect because the *Reichard/McMillan* exception does not require the defendant to be "unaware" of firearms; in *Reichard*, the defendant knew of the weapon but was still permitted the defense because she was not the one who used it to kill.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "United States v. Contento-Pachon recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat of death." — wrong because *Contento-Pachon* addressed drug trafficking and did not create a universal exception that overrides the common-law homicide bar.
- (b): "both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any killing" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 contains no categorical bar for homicide.
- (c): "avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death." — wrong because under the MPC § 3.02 "choice of evils," the death of an innocent human being is valued above avoiding non-lethal physical injury.
- (d): "only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm" — wrong because the *Reichard/McMillan* exception for non-killers does not require ignorance of the presence of weapons.

Recommended fix: none
-->
```

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q13.** Assume for this question that Kant satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, and that it is established that Bentham (not Kant) fired the shot that killed Bayes (Fact 7). The State charges Kant with felony murder for Bayes's death, with the armed robbery as the predicate. Evaluate duress as a defense to the felony-murder charge.

(a) Duress is available for felony murder under both the common law and the Model Penal Code, because *United States v. Contento-Pachon* recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat of death.

(b) Duress is unavailable for felony murder under both the common law and the Model Penal Code, because both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any killing that occurs during the commission of a predicate felony.

(c) Although duress is unavailable, Kant may instead assert necessity under Model Penal Code § 3.02's greater-evils balancing, because avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death.

(d) Duress is available for felony murder under the Reichard/McMillan exception only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm; because the armed-car robbery inherently involves firearms, the exception is categorically foreclosed for any defendant who participated in such a robbery.

(e) Duress is unavailable for felony murder at common law under the traditional rule that extends the murder bar to killings committed during a predicate felony; however, the Michigan/Maryland Reichard/McMillan exception makes duress available for felony murder where the defense is available for the predicate felony, and Kant — a non-killer participant whose only link to Bayes's death was driving the van — fits that exception cleanly. Under Model Penal Code § 2.09, duress is available for felony murder because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder. However, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception permits duress for felony murder if the defense excuses the predicate felony and the defendant was a non-killer participant. Given the prompt's stipulation that duress is met for the robbery, Kant fits this exception cleanly. Under MPC § 2.09, duress is available because it lacks a homicide exception. (a) is incorrect; *Contento-Pachon* involved drug trafficking, not homicide, and did not disturb the murder bar. (b) incorrectly applies the common-law murder bar to the MPC, which has no such bar. (c) incorrectly invokes necessity; avoiding injury is not a greater evil than a human death, and the common law bars necessity for homicide. (d) invents an ignorance-of-armament requirement; *Reichard* allowed the defense even though the defendant knew her co-felon was armed.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; *People v. Reichard*, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); *McMillan v. State*, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might defend this option by viewing *Contento-Pachon* as the seminal, expansive case on common-law duress that broadly excuses conduct under immediate threat of death. They could mistakenly assume that because the MPC also broadly allows duress, the two frameworks merge into a universal standard that supersedes the historical murder bar, obliterating the traditional strictures against homicidal duress.
(b) Argument-for: A student could defend this option by relying on the historic sanctity of human life in criminal law, reasoning that felony murder is still murder. Because traditional common law categorically bars duress for murder, a student might infer that the MPC implicitly retains this fundamental moral bar against excusing killings, leading them to conclude both regimes categorically forbid duress for any death resulting from a felony.
(c) Argument-for: A student might favor this option by correctly recognizing that the Model Penal Code, unlike the common law, does not categorically exclude homicides from justification defenses. They could argue that the MPC’s § 3.02 greater-evils defense is a pure balancing test designed for the jury to weigh all circumstances, meaning Kant could assert the defense by arguing that saving two people from severe injury arguably outweighs one death in a utilitarian calculus.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that allowing duress in an "armed" robbery inherently contradicts the underlying premise of the defense—that the defendant did not recklessly place themselves in a situation where they would be forced to commit a crime. They might reason that the *Reichard/McMillan* exception logically must be limited to unarmed or unaware participants, because knowing a co-felon is armed makes the resulting death a foreseeable, accepted risk.
(e) Argument-for: The traditional common-law rule strictly barred duress as a defense to murder, including felony murder. However, modern cases like *Reichard* and *McMillan* created a targeted exception: a non-killer participant coerced into the predicate felony can assert duress against the felony-murder charge. Furthermore, under the Model Penal Code, § 2.09 contains no homicide exception whatsoever, rendering duress broadly available for felony murder. Kant fits these standards perfectly.

Head-to-head: Option (e) flawlessly reflects the nuanced reality of the doctrine, capturing the traditional common-law bar, the specific modern exception for non-killer accomplices (*Reichard/McMillan*), and the MPC's explicit omission of a homicide bar. The distractors all rely on clear, falsifiable errors of law. Option (a) overextends a drug-trafficking case into a universal homicide exception. Option (b) falsely applies the common-law murder bar to the MPC. Option (c) falsely claims duress is unavailable under the MPC and misapplies the objective greater-evils balancing test by asserting non-lethal injury outweighs human life. Option (d) invents a strict "unaware of firearms" requirement that contradicts the very cases it cites.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Contento-Pachon* recognized a universal common-law duress exception" — wrong because *Contento-Pachon* was a drug-smuggling case that did not alter the common-law bar on duress for homicide, nor did it establish a "universal" exception.
- (b): "both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any killing" — wrong because Model Penal Code § 2.09 famously omits the homicide exception and does not categorically bar duress for killings.
- (c): "Although duress is unavailable [under the MPC]" — wrong because duress *is* available for homicide under MPC § 2.09. Additionally, avoiding hospitalization is objectively not a "greater evil" than death under MPC § 3.02.
- (d): "only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm" — wrong because the *Reichard* court allowed the exception even though the defendant was aware her co-felon was armed.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q14

### Grounding

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — objective reasonableness, Ch 22, Refinement `objective-reasonableness-standard` and `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->
