### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at his chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Although the Court of Appeals in *Goetz* eventually rejected the Appellate Division's purely subjective test, the core of the defense's argument—and the initial lower court ruling—was that a defendant's actions must be judged from their own perspective to be fair. An advocate could argue that by incorporating the defendant's "situation" and "prior experiences," *Goetz* effectively allows a jury to decide if the act was "reasonable to him." This ensures that the jury accounts for the specific physical and mental state of the defendant at the time of the shooting.

(b) Argument-for: Under *People v. Goetz*, the New York standard for reasonableness is an objective one that nonetheless accounts for the defendant's specific "circumstances" and "situation." This includes any prior knowledge the defendant had of the attacker, such as Bentham's specific threat to hospitalize Kant. Given the immediate physical threat of a leveled firearm and the lack of accessible cover or a nearby exit, a reasonable person possessing Kant's knowledge would logically conclude that deadly force was necessary to prevent his own death or serious injury.

(c) Argument-for: One could argue that to remain truly "objective," the *Goetz* standard must look at the immediate physical facts of the encounter—the six-foot distance and the revolver—rather than the history between the parties. Allowing a jury to consider prior threats or reputations for violence risks turning the trial into a character assassination of the victim rather than a clinical evaluation of the immediate threat. From this perspective, the "reasonable person" is an abstract construct that reacts to the barrel of a gun, not to personal grievances or prior non-lethal threats like a "hospitalization" comment.

(d) Argument-for: An advocate might argue that the inclusion of the word "reasonably" in the New York statute is a mere formal hurdle, and that in practice, New York aligns with the Model Penal Code's emphasis on the defendant's internal belief. Since the law requires the jury to step into the defendant's shoes and look through his eyes, the inquiry is fundamentally subjective. If Kant truly believed he was in danger of death, his belief should satisfy the standard regardless of how an external "reasonable person" might feel.

(e) Argument-for: In many areas of criminal law, such as duress, the "person of reasonable firmness" is the standard for evaluating how much pressure a defendant is expected to withstand. A student might argue that self-defense is essentially a subset of this logic: would a person of reasonable firmness, facing a loaded gun and a prior threat of violence, feel compelled to fire? This provides a clear, high-threshold benchmark that matches the intensity of a lethal encounter like the one between Bentham and Kant.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the strongest because it perfectly captures the "hybrid" nature of the *Goetz* standard—retaining the objective "reasonable person" while populating that person with the defendant's specific situational knowledge. Options (a), (c), and (d) each take one side of the subjective/objective divide that *Goetz* explicitly attempted to bridge, making them legally inaccurate. Option (e) is a "category error" distractor, importing the standard for duress into a self-defense analysis.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because *Goetz* expressly rejected this formulation in favor of an objective standard.
- (c): "*Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the "situation" include the defendant's relevant prior experiences.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because *Goetz* specifically noted that the NY legislature rejected the MPC approach by requiring that the defendant "reasonably" believe force is necessary.
- (e): "controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist firing" — wrong because "reasonable firmness" is the standard for duress under MPC § 2.09, not self-defense under NYPL § 35.15.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that *Goetz* emphasized evaluating the circumstances from the defendant's perspective. The Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation captured the reality that the factfinder must step into the defendant's shoes. Because the Court of Appeals required the jury to consider the defendant's specific background, physical attributes, and prior experiences, a student might conclude that the highest court essentially adopted the "reasonable to him" standard in substance, even if it refined the nomenclature.
(b) Argument-for: A student could point out that *Goetz* famously established a hybrid objective-subjective standard for self-defense in New York. The Court held that while the belief must be objectively reasonable, the "reasonable person" must be placed in the defendant's exact "situation." This situation explicitly includes the physical realities of the encounter (a leveled gun, no cover) as well as the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker's prior threats. Thus, a reasonable person in Kant's exact situation could find deadly force immediately necessary.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that *Goetz* explicitly rejected the Model Penal Code's subjective approach in favor of retaining an objective standard. To maintain a true objective standard, the inquiry must be cleansed of the defendant's subjective biases, fears, and idiosyncratic prior experiences. If the jury considers the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker, it risks converting the test back into the rejected subjective "reasonable to him" standard. Therefore, true objective reasonableness must exclude these subjective historical factors.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that the New York penal code revisions in the 1960s were heavily influenced by the Model Penal Code. The MPC deliberately abandoned the strict objective standard for self-defense, requiring only that the actor genuinely believe the force was necessary. A student could mistakenly believe that New York adopted this MPC provision wholesale, meaning Kant's honest, subjective belief is enough to satisfy the statute without an objective reasonableness requirement.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that when an actor is facing an immediate, terrifying threat like a leveled revolver, the situation triggers the "person of reasonable firmness" standard. This standard, widely used in necessity and duress contexts to evaluate objectively reasonable responses to overwhelming pressure, perfectly captures the terror of a gun pointed at one's chest. Thus, the controlling objective test is whether a reasonably firm person would succumb to the pressure and fire.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear correct answer, as it accurately applies the *Goetz* standard, which incorporates the defendant's "situation" (including prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker) into the objective reasonable person test. Option (a) fails because it explicitly states *Goetz* "adopted" the Appellate Division's standard, a historically and legally false claim since the Court of Appeals reversed the Appellate Division specifically to reject that formulation. Option (c) fails because it categorically asserts the standard "excludes any reference" to prior experiences, directly contradicting *Goetz*'s holding that the defendant's situation encompasses such experiences. Option (d) fails because it falsely claims New York adopted the "purely subjective formulation" of the MPC without requiring objective reasonableness, ignoring the legislature's addition of the word "reasonably." Option (e) fails because it incorrectly identifies the "person of reasonable firmness" test—which belongs to duress—as the controlling inquiry for self-defense. All distractors feature clear, falsifiable legal errors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because the Court of Appeals expressly rejected the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" standard to preserve an objective requirement.
- (c): "excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the objective standard must incorporate the physical circumstances and the defendant's prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because the New York legislature deliberately added the word "reasonably" to the MPC's language, requiring objective reasonableness.
- (e): "the controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist" — wrong because this formulation states the objective standard for the excuse of duress (MPC § 2.09), not the justification of self-defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q15

### Grounding

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume a jury finds Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. Evaluate how the duty-to-retreat analysis would proceed across three jurisdictions: New York, Florida, and California.

(a) Kant is entitled to Stand Your Ground immunity in Florida regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer, because the statute's "engaged in criminal activity" limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation; Bentham's aggression severs any connection to the predicate robbery.

(b) The New York duty to retreat is not satisfied unless a reasonably prudent person would have retreated using any available escape route, regardless of safety; the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes an available route as a matter of law.

(c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant cannot invoke stand-your-ground immunity because the statute bars immunity where the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity"; in California, there is no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->

(e) The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions because Kant was a business invitee, and all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly traces each jurisdiction. NY requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; a fifteen-foot traverse of open floor against a leveled revolver from six feet likely fails that standard. Florida's stand-your-ground statute excludes immunity when the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity" (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)), which the prompt stipulates applies here. CA imposes no duty to retreat by case law. (a) fabricates a "severance" doctrine; FL's statutory limitation is categorical. (b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duty-to-retreat/stand-your-ground, Ch 22, Refinement `duty-to-retreat-general`, `stand-your-ground-statute`, and `castle-doctrine-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

(b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume a jury finds Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. Evaluate how the duty-to-retreat analysis would proceed across three jurisdictions: New York, Florida, and California.

(a) Kant is entitled to Stand Your Ground immunity in Florida regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer, because the statute's "engaged in criminal activity" limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation; Bentham's aggression severs any connection to the predicate robbery.

(b) The New York duty to retreat is not satisfied unless a reasonably prudent person would have retreated using any available escape route, regardless of safety; the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes an available route as a matter of law.

(c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant cannot invoke stand-your-ground immunity because the statute bars immunity where the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity"; in California, there is no duty to retreat.

(e) The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions because Kant was a business invitee, and all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly traces each jurisdiction. NY requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; a fifteen-foot traverse of open floor against a leveled revolver from six feet likely fails that standard. Florida's stand-your-ground statute excludes immunity when the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity" (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)), which the prompt stipulates applies here. CA imposes no duty to retreat by case law. (a) fabricates a "severance" doctrine; FL's statutory limitation is categorical. (b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that Florida’s “engaged in criminal activity” bar requires a nexus between the crime and the threat to prevent absurd results. If Bentham’s threat is an independent act of aggression unrelated to the logistics of the proceeds transfer, the "severance" of the criminal enterprise from the self-defense event allows the fundamental right to stand one's ground to reappear. Under this view, the statute only punishes criminal activity that proximately causes the need for force.

(b) Argument-for: In New York, the duty to retreat is a rigorous objective standard designed to favor the preservation of life over the use of deadly force. A fifteen-foot distance to an exit represents a clear, physically achievable path that any reasonably prudent person would attempt before taking a life. By prioritizing the objective availability of a route over the subjective fear of the actor, the New York rule can be interpreted as creating a duty to retreat whenever a physical exit is within a reasonable distance, such as fifteen feet.

(c) Argument-for: While California is often cited as a "stand your ground" state, this rule is primarily a product of jury instructions (CALCRIM 505) and common law rather than a categorical statute. An advocate could argue that in commercial settings—especially those involving illegal transactions—California courts would revert to the traditional common law rule requiring retreat. Because the warehouse is not a dwelling, the common law preference for avoiding homicide would impose a duty to retreat on anyone not currently in their home.

(d) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the "complete personal safety" caveat in New York Penal Law § 35.15(2)(a), noting that running fifteen feet while staring down a leveled barrel is inherently unsafe. It accurately applies the Florida statutory bar (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)) which removes immunity for those "engaged in criminal activity" at the time of the force. Finally, it recognizes that California case law has long established a no-duty-to-retreat rule that applies both inside and outside the home.

(e) Argument-for: The castle doctrine frequently extends to an individual’s place of business or employment. Since Kant was present at the warehouse for a business transaction (albeit an illegal one), he could be characterized as a business invitee or an occupant of the workplace. If all three jurisdictions recognize that a person should not be forced to flee their place of work, the castle doctrine would apply to the warehouse regardless of the legality of the specific transaction occurring.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is the strongest because it accurately reflects the statutory and case law nuances of all three jurisdictions, particularly the "complete personal safety" qualifier in NY and the "criminal activity" bar in FL. Option (a) fails because Florida's statutory bar is generally interpreted as categorical; the statute does not provide for a "severance" based on the source of the aggression. Option (b) is legally incorrect because it claims the NY duty applies "regardless of safety," which directly contradicts the text of NY PL § 35.15. Option (c) is false because California case law (e.g., People v. Zurica) explicitly rejects a duty to retreat in non-dwelling settings. Option (e) is incorrect because the castle doctrine is generally inapplicable to defendants who are themselves engaged in criminal activity or who lack a legal/proprietary interest in the premises.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the statute's 'engaged in criminal activity' limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation" — wrong because Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) contains no such "unrelated" qualifier; the bar is triggered by the status of being engaged in the activity.
- (b): "regardless of safety" — wrong because New York law explicitly limits the duty to situations where retreat can be accomplished with "complete personal safety."
- (c): "California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations" — wrong because California case law (and CALCRIM 505) establishes that there is no duty to retreat in any place the person has a right to be.
- (e): "The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions" — wrong because none of these jurisdictions automatically extend the castle doctrine to a warehouse where the claimant is an invitee engaged in criminal activity.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume a jury finds Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. Evaluate how the duty-to-retreat analysis would proceed across three jurisdictions: New York, Florida, and California.

(a) Kant is entitled to Stand Your Ground immunity in Florida regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer, because the statute's "engaged in criminal activity" limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation; Bentham's aggression severs any connection to the predicate robbery.

(b) The New York duty to retreat is not satisfied unless a reasonably prudent person would have retreated using any available escape route, regardless of safety; the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes an available route as a matter of law.

(c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant cannot invoke stand-your-ground immunity because the statute bars immunity where the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity"; in California, there is no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->

(e) The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions because Kant was a business invitee, and all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly traces each jurisdiction. NY requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; a fifteen-foot traverse of open floor against a leveled revolver from six feet likely fails that standard. Florida's stand-your-ground statute excludes immunity when the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity" (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)), which the prompt stipulates applies here. CA imposes no duty to retreat by case law. (a) fabricates a "severance" doctrine; FL's statutory limitation is categorical. (b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that Florida courts have occasionally read a nexus requirement into the "engaged in criminal activity" exception to avoid absurd results. Under this view, a defendant does not lose their right to self-defense entirely just because they are committing an unrelated crime, and the aggression by Bentham acts as an intervening cause. Therefore, the "criminal activity" limitation shouldn't strictly bar SYG immunity when the confrontation is completely separate from the predicate robbery proceeds transfer.
(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that under the traditional retreat rule, physical availability of an exit creates a duty to attempt retreat. Because fifteen feet is a relatively short distance, a matter-of-law determination is appropriate. Furthermore, strict retreat requirements conceptually mean any physical avenue of escape must be taken before resorting to deadly force, minimizing the subjective assessment of "safety."
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that California historically required retreat in certain contexts before specific jury instructions solidified the "no duty to retreat" rule. They might confuse California with traditional Eastern jurisdictions that restrict the right to stand your ground exclusively to the home (castle doctrine). Thus, in a commercial warehouse, the traditional duty to retreat would apply.
(d) Argument-for: This option perfectly maps onto the laws of the three jurisdictions. New York explicitly requires retreat only if the defender knows they can do so with "complete personal safety," which is likely unachievable against a leveled gun from six feet away. Florida's Stand Your Ground law explicitly removes immunity if the person is "engaged in a criminal activity." California firmly rejects the duty to retreat entirely through established case law.
(e) Argument-for: A student might contend that the castle doctrine has been broadly expanded to include a person's place of business across the majority of jurisdictions. If Kant was a business invitee at the warehouse, he had a lawful right to be there, and thus the space functions as an extension of his "castle." Under this logic, no duty to retreat applies in any of the three states due to the workplace exception.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is clearly the best answer, correctly applying the exact retreat and stand-your-ground standards in NY, FL, and CA. Option (a) relies on a fabricated "severance" exception to Florida's statutory criminal-activity bar. Option (b) makes the explicit and easily falsifiable claim that NY requires retreat "regardless of safety," completely ignoring the "complete personal safety" statutory standard. Option (c) contains the explicitly false claim that "California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations," which contradicts CA's well-established stand-your-ground case law. Option (e) falsely claims that "all three recognize a workplace extension" of the castle doctrine, which fails because NY explicitly limits its castle exception to dwellings.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the statute's 'engaged in criminal activity' limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation" — wrong because Florida's SYG statute categorically removes the immunity when the defendant is engaged in criminal activity, without any such severance or relation test.
- (b): "regardless of safety" — wrong because NY Penal Law explicitly conditions the duty to retreat on whether the actor can do so with "complete personal safety."
- (c): "California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations" — wrong because California is a stand-your-ground state by case law and imposes no duty to retreat in non-dwelling confrontations.
- (e): "all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine" — wrong because New York strictly limits its castle doctrine exception to the actor's "dwelling."

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q16

### Grounding

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty. <!-- correct -->

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home.

**Answer:** (a)
**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.
**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — castle doctrine, Ch 22, Refinement `castle-doctrine-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty. <!-- correct -->

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home.

**Answer:** (a)
**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.
**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: The castle doctrine is an exception to the duty to retreat, not an expansion of the definition of justifiable force. Under *People v. Tomlins*, while one is not required to flee their own home, the use of force is still predicated on the presence of an unlawful threat. Because Officer Hand entered lawfully pursuant to a valid warrant and identified himself, there is no "unlawful force" to defend against, and the doctrine provides no shield for Kant's actions.

(b) Argument-for: *People v. Tomlins* famously declares that "a man's house is his castle," describing the home as a sanctuary. A student could argue that the logic of this doctrine is to provide absolute protection within the home, making the occupant's right to defensive force superior to the state's power of entry. This interpretation views the dwelling as a jurisdictional limit on all external force, suggesting that the sanctuary protection is categorical and does not fluctuate based on the official identity or legal status of the entrant.

(c) Argument-for: In jurisdictions like Florida, the castle doctrine is codified to include a statutory presumption of reasonable fear of death or great bodily harm for anyone who uses force against a person who has entered their dwelling. A student might argue that this presumption dispenses with the need to prove the actual nature of the threat because the law assumes the threat from the fact of entry alone. This reading focuses on the procedural advantage of the presumption as a substitute for the traditional "unlawful threat" analysis.

(d) Argument-for: The initial-aggressor doctrine bars an individual who provoked a confrontation from later claiming self-defense in that same chain of events. If the attempt to arrest Kant is viewed as a continuation of the warehouse encounter, Kant's initial aggression there would follow him into the apartment. This argument posits that a defendant cannot cleanse their status as an aggressor by simply moving to a new location while being actively pursued for the initial crime, making the castle doctrine irrelevant.

(e) Argument-for: One could argue that the sanctuary of the home is a universal principle of American law that supersedes specific statutory exceptions regarding police entry. This argument suggests that the "castle" status is absolute and that the legal system lacks any "lawful entrant" exception because such an exception would effectively abolish the doctrine's core purpose of protecting the home from state overreach. It positions the home as a space where even the execution of a warrant does not strip the occupant of the right to resist.

Head-to-head: (a) is the strongest and correct option because it identifies the necessary intersection of the retreat rule (castle doctrine) and the predicate requirement of "unlawful force." The castle doctrine only modifies the *where* (the home), not the *what* (unlawful force vs. lawful entry). (b) and (e) are legally false because they claim the castle doctrine is absolute; in reality, it never protects against lawful entrants like police with warrants. (c) is legally false because the Florida presumption specifically requires the entry to be "unlawful and forcible," and a warrant makes entry lawful. (d) is legally false because the initial-aggressor bar is restricted to the discrete confrontation in which it arose and does not follow a defendant indefinitely into subsequent encounters.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder" — wrong because the castle doctrine only applies when the intruder's force is "unlawful," and it does not apply to lawful entrants like police.
- (c): "dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat" — wrong because even statutory presumptions (like Florida’s) require an "unlawful and forcible" entry to trigger the presumption in the first place.
- (d): "initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations" — wrong because initial-aggressor status is encounter-specific and does not permanently disable the right to self-defense in future, unrelated confrontations.
- (e): "no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception" — wrong because every jurisdiction recognizes that the castle doctrine does not permit force against officers executing a valid warrant.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. (a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty. <!-- correct -->

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home.

**Answer:** (a)
**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.
**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: The castle doctrine functions strictly as a geographic exception to the duty to retreat, not as an independent grant of authority to use violence. To validly claim self-defense, a defendant must always establish that they were facing an imminent threat of unlawful force. Since Officer Hand was executing a valid arrest warrant after proper loudspeaker identification, his entry was legally authorized and thus constituted lawful force. Consequently, the foundational predicate for self-defense is absent, rendering the castle doctrine's retreat exception entirely irrelevant to Kant's actions. This option perfectly captures the structural limits of self-defense doctrine.

(b) Argument-for: In *People v. Tomlins*, Judge Cardozo emphasized the absolute sanctity of the home, stating that a man assailed in his dwelling "may stand his ground, and resist the attack." A student might argue that this foundational formulation of the castle doctrine centers entirely on the location of the attack rather than the legal status of the intruder. Because the home is an inviolable sanctuary, any forced entry—even by law enforcement—can be perceived by the occupant as a life-threatening intrusion justifying defensive force. Under this reading, the castle doctrine provides a blanket authorization for defending the dwelling without exception for the intruder's identity.

(c) Argument-for: Modern Stand Your Ground laws, typified by Florida's statutory framework, significantly expand the common-law castle doctrine by creating a legal presumption of reasonable fear of imminent death or great bodily harm. A student could argue that this presumption is designed to be universally applicable to any forceful entry into a dwelling, specifically to relieve the homeowner of the burden of proving the intruder's intent or lawful status in the heat of the moment. By dispensing with the need to affirmatively prove an actual imminent unlawful threat, this interpretation suggests the castle doctrine automatically protects Kant's defensive force upon any forced entry into his home.

(d) Argument-for: The initial-aggressor rule operates as a strict bar to claiming self-defense unless the aggressor clearly communicates a withdrawal. A student could argue that Kant's continuous flight from the initial warehouse altercation to his apartment constituted a single, unbroken chain of events. Because Kant never successfully withdrew or communicated a withdrawal to the pursuing authorities, his status as the initial aggressor remained active throughout the chase. Under this theory, the initial-aggressor taint follows him across all subsequent confrontations, universally precluding him from invoking the castle doctrine against Officer Hand.

(e) Argument-for: The fundamental principle that "a man's home is his castle" is deeply entrenched in American legal tradition. A student might argue that the castle doctrine is recognized in every U.S. jurisdiction as an absolute protection of the home that supersedes even law enforcement authority. Because forced entry inherently creates a chaotic and dangerous situation for the occupant, the argument would assert that no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception when the home's threshold is violently breached. Therefore, the doctrine universally applies to permit defensive force against anyone, including an officer executing a warrant.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is the only legally accurate statement, correctly identifying that the castle doctrine removes the duty to retreat but cannot cure the lack of the "unlawful force" predicate required for self-defense. Options (b), (c), (d), and (e) are effectively falsified by their absolute claims. Option (b) falsely claims *Tomlins* created a rule "without exception as to the identity of the intruder," ignoring the foundational unlawful force requirement. Option (c) falsely claims Florida's statutory presumption applies to "any in-home force claim," when Florida statutes expressly exempt entry by law enforcement officers executing their official duties. Option (d) falsely asserts the initial-aggressor bar "follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations," when it actually applies only within the discrete confrontation with the original adversary or their immediate defenders. Option (e) falsely claims "every U.S. jurisdiction" allows force against warrant execution and that "no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception," which blatantly contradicts universal self-defense predicate requirements.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder" — wrong because self-defense fundamentally requires the threat to be unlawful; a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant does not qualify as an unlawful threat.
- (c): "applies to any in-home force claim" — wrong because statutory presumptions like Florida's explicitly exempt known law enforcement officers who are legally entering a dwelling to perform official duties.
- (d): "follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations" — wrong because the initial-aggressor bar does not attach to a person as a permanent, universal disability; it applies narrowly per discrete encounter.
- (e): "every U.S. jurisdiction... because no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception" — wrong because all jurisdictions require the predicate of unlawful force to claim self-defense, meaning every state effectively recognizes a lawful entrant exception.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q17

### Grounding

**Q17.** Assume that, whether or not Kant is found insane, he stabbed Officer Hand while genuinely believing, due entirely to a psychotic delusion, that Officer Hand was a demon dispatched to execute him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense in California under *People v. Schuller* and *People v. Elmore*, and in a jurisdiction (e.g., New York) that rejects imperfect self-defense.

(a) Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief, though purely delusional, suffices under *Schuller* to negate malice; it is unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine.

(b) Imperfect self-defense is unavailable in California because an honest defensive belief based entirely on a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in insanity rather than self-defense; it is likewise unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine altogether. <!-- correct -->

(c) Imperfect self-defense is available in both California and New York, because both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief—even a purely delusional one—as negating malice and reducing the charge to attempted voluntary manslaughter.

(d) Imperfect self-defense reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California because it substitutes a subjective-reasonableness standard; in New York, it serves as a complete justification.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense for delusional beliefs, preempting California's and New York's state-law limitations.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) is correct because, under California law (*People v. Elmore*, reaffirmed in *People v. Schuller*), a belief in the need for self-defense that is based *entirely* on a psychotic delusion cannot support imperfect self-defense; such purely delusional claims sound exclusively in the insanity defense. New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine. (a) misstates California law; *Schuller* did not adopt a pure "genuineness-only" rule for pure delusions, which remain barred under *Elmore*. (c) misstates both California and New York law, as neither permits imperfect self-defense for a pure delusion. (d) misidentifies the doctrine's effect in California (it reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misstates New York law, which rejects the doctrine rather than treating it as a complete justification. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses, contrary to the claim that it mandates imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, Elmore-delusion, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Elmore*, 59 Cal.4th 121 (2014) & *People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): purely delusional beliefs barred from imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Imperfect self-defense (delusional beliefs), Ch 22, Refinement `imperfect-self-defense` and `schuller-genuineness-only`; Kahler due-process floor, Ch 23, Refinement `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q17.** Assume that, whether or not Kant is found insane, he stabbed Officer Hand while genuinely believing, due entirely to a psychotic delusion, that Officer Hand was a demon dispatched to execute him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense in California under *People v. Schuller* and *People v. Elmore*, and in a jurisdiction (e.g., New York) that rejects imperfect self-defense.

(a) Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief, though purely delusional, suffices under *Schuller* to negate malice; it is unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine.

(b) Imperfect self-defense is unavailable in California because an honest defensive belief based entirely on a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in insanity rather than self-defense; it is likewise unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine altogether. <!-- correct -->

(c) Imperfect self-defense is available in both California and New York, because both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief—even a purely delusional one—as negating malice and reducing the charge to attempted voluntary manslaughter.

(d) Imperfect self-defense reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California because it substitutes a subjective-reasonableness standard; in New York, it serves as a complete justification.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense for delusional beliefs, preempting California's and New York's state-law limitations.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) is correct because, under California law (*People v. Elmore*, reaffirmed in *People v. Schuller*), a belief in the need for self-defense that is based *entirely* on a psychotic delusion cannot support imperfect self-defense; such purely delusional claims sound exclusively in the insanity defense. New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine. (a) misstates California law; *Schuller* did not adopt a pure "genuineness-only" rule for pure delusions, which remain barred under *Elmore*. (c) misstates both California and New York law, as neither permits imperfect self-defense for a pure delusion. (d) misidentifies the doctrine's effect in California (it reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misstates New York law, which rejects the doctrine rather than treating it as a complete justification. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses, contrary to the claim that it mandates imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, Elmore-delusion, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Elmore*, 59 Cal.4th 121 (2014) & *People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): purely delusional beliefs barred from imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under People v. Schuller (2023), the California Supreme Court clarified that imperfect self-defense is not a true affirmative defense but a doctrine that negates the "malice" element of murder. Because malice requires the absence of a belief in the need for self-defense, a defendant like Kant, who genuinely (even if delusionally) believes he is under threat, lacks the subjective state of mind necessary for murder. One could argue that because Schuller elevated the failure to instruct on this doctrine to a constitutional error, the "genuineness" of the belief is the only constitutionally relevant factor, potentially superseding the earlier limitation in Elmore regarding delusions. Thus, California would allow the defense while New York, which strictly adheres to a reasonable-belief standard for justification, would not.

(b) Argument-for: This option correctly applies the rule from People v. Elmore, which remains good law in California even after Schuller. Elmore held that while imperfect self-defense negates malice, it cannot be predicated on a "purely delusional" belief because such a belief has no basis in objective reality and is therefore the exclusive province of the insanity defense (M'Naghten) rather than the self-defense doctrine. Since Kant’s belief was based "entirely" on a psychotic delusion, he is ineligible for the defense in California. In New York, the doctrine is rejected categorically, as New York does not recognize an "unreasonable belief" as a mitigator for murder outside of the specific "Extreme Emotional Disturbance" (EED) defense.

(c) Argument-for: An advocate might argue that the fundamental principle of mens rea requires that a person cannot be convicted of murder if they lack the subjective intent to act unlawfully. If Kant genuinely believed he was defending his life against a demon, he lacked the "malignant heart" or intent to kill a human being unlawfully. In this view, both California and New York must provide a mechanism—whether called imperfect self-defense or interpreted through the lens of voluntary manslaughter—to ensure that a defendant’s actual subjective state of mind is accounted for, preventing a murder conviction where the defendant believed their actions were justified.

(d) Argument-for: This argument posits that imperfect self-defense functions as a bridge between intentional murder and accidental killing. In California, by proving a subjective (but unreasonable) belief in the need for force, the defendant demonstrates a level of culpability closer to criminal negligence than to malice. In New York, one could argue that the state's justification statutes, when read broadly in light of due process, should function as a complete justification if the defendant can prove they acted without any criminal intent, effectively treating a "genuine" belief as a total shield to liability.

(e) Argument-for: In Kahler v. Kansas (2020), the Supreme Court held that states have the power to define the insanity defense, but it also suggested that states must allow defendants to introduce evidence of mental illness to negate the mens rea of a crime. A student could argue that Kahler implies a constitutional mandate: if a delusion negates the specific intent or malice required for murder, the state cannot categorically bar that evidence. Under this reading, Kahler would preempt state-law restrictions like California’s Elmore rule or New York’s rejection of the doctrine, forcing every state to recognize a "delusional belief" as a mitigator or defense.

Head-to-head: (b) is the strongest answer because it identifies the precise legal boundary established in People v. Elmore—that purely delusional beliefs are relegated to the insanity phase and cannot support a claim of imperfect self-defense during the guilt phase. (a) is weaker because it interprets People v. Schuller as expanding the doctrine to delusions, when Schuller actually focused on the procedural standard of review for instructional error. (c) is factually incorrect regarding New York law, which has explicitly rejected the imperfect self-defense doctrine for decades. (d) misstates the legal effect of the doctrine in California (which reduces murder to manslaughter, not negligent homicide) and mischaracterizes New York's justification law. (e) is a significant misreading of Kahler v. Kansas, which actually affirmed state sovereignty in defining the insanity defense rather than imposing a federal mandate for specific mitigations like imperfect self-defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief... suffices under Schuller to negate malice" — wrong because Elmore (reaffirmed by Schuller) holds that purely delusional beliefs cannot support imperfect self-defense.
- (c): "available in both California and New York" — wrong because New York does not recognize the doctrine and California excludes purely delusional beliefs.
- (d): "reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California" — wrong because the doctrine reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter.
- (e): "Kahler v. Kansas requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense" — wrong because Kahler held that the Constitution does not require any specific version of the insanity defense, let alone a specific mitigation doctrine for delusions.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q17.** Assume that, whether or not Kant is found insane, he stabbed Officer Hand while genuinely believing, due entirely to a psychotic delusion, that Officer Hand was a demon dispatched to execute him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense in California under *People v. Schuller* and *People v. Elmore*, and in a jurisdiction (e.g., New York) that rejects imperfect self-defense.

(a) Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief, though purely delusional, suffices under *Schuller* to negate malice; it is unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine.

(b) Imperfect self-defense is unavailable in California because an honest defensive belief based entirely on a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in insanity rather than self-defense; it is likewise unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine altogether. <!-- correct -->

(c) Imperfect self-defense is available in both California and New York, because both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief—even a purely delusional one—as negating malice and reducing the charge to attempted voluntary manslaughter.

(d) Imperfect self-defense reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California because it substitutes a subjective-reasonableness standard; in New York, it serves as a complete justification.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense for delusional beliefs, preempting California's and New York's state-law limitations.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) is correct because, under California law (*People v. Elmore*, reaffirmed in *People v. Schuller*), a belief in the need for self-defense that is based *entirely* on a psychotic delusion cannot support imperfect self-defense; such purely delusional claims sound exclusively in the insanity defense. New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine. (d) misidentifies the doctrine's effect in California (it reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misstates New York law, which rejects the doctrine rather than treating it as a complete justification. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses, contrary to the claim that it mandates imperfect self-defense.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that because imperfect self-defense is fundamentally about the subjective negation of malice, *Schuller*'s emphasis on a defendant's actual belief logically encompasses delusions. If the belief is genuine, regardless of its psychotic origin, it negates the malice aforethought required for murder, making imperfect self-defense available in California while correctly noting New York's rejection of the doctrine.
(b) Argument-for: This is the structurally correct application of California and New York law. Under *People v. Elmore*, a defensive belief arising entirely from a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in the insanity defense, not imperfect self-defense, a rule that remains intact under *Schuller*. Furthermore, New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine, making the defense unavailable in both jurisdictions for this scenario.
(c) Argument-for: A student might reason that modern mens rea principles strictly require malice for murder, meaning any genuine belief in the need for self-defense—even if entirely delusional—must negate malice. This would lead to the conclusion that both California and New York treat such a subjective mental state as incompatible with murder, universally reducing the charge to manslaughter or attempted manslaughter.
(d) Argument-for: A student could believe that because an imperfect self-defense claim is inherently based on an objectively unreasonable (negligent) belief, California law explicitly maps the offense to criminally negligent homicide rather than voluntary manslaughter. Additionally, they might mistakenly conflate New York law with a completely subjective justification standard under the Model Penal Code.
(e) Argument-for: A student could interpret the Supreme Court's ruling in *Kahler v. Head-to-head: (b) accurately synthesizes California's limitation on imperfect self-defense (barring pure delusions under *Elmore*) and New York's complete rejection of the doctrine. The question is mathematically sound and conceptually rigorous.

- (a): "suffices under Schuller to negate malice" — wrong because California law (*Elmore*) explicitly bars imperfect self-defense based entirely on a psychotic delusion.
- (c): "both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief... as negating malice" — wrong because New York categorically rejects imperfect self-defense and California bars it for pure delusions.

## Q18

### Grounding

**Q18.** Assume that Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia, in active psychotic episode) at the time of the Hand stabbing. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited, and Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer demonstrates knowledge that the stabbing was illegal.

(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because he believed Officer Hand was a demon rather than a human officer; that mistaken belief about the act's object satisfies the test regardless of whether Kant understood the act's wrongfulness.

(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because 18 U.S.C. § 17 requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that dangerousness showing, is insufficient to meet the federal standard.

(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to an acquittal because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a floor for insanity defenses.

(e) Kant likely satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because Dr. Singer's testimony established that Kant's delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong — he believed killing the possessed human host was morally required to defeat the demonic attack; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because Dr. Singer's testimony also established that Kant understood the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied only a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted.

**Answer:** (e)
**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong. Jurisdictions split between legal and moral formulations of wrongfulness, and the two formulations generate different results here. Under a moral formulation, Dr. Singer's testimony that Kant believed killing the possessed host was morally required to defeat a demonic attack (Fact 13) goes directly to the prong: Kant could not appreciate the act's moral wrongness. Under a legal formulation, Fact 13 establishes the opposite result — Kant understood that the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted. The doctrinal pivot is whether the delusion replaces the object of the act (killing a pure demon, to which no homicide statute applies) or merely rewrites the moral calculus (still killing a human, still legally prohibited, but subjectively justified by a demonic-possession narrative). Facts 12–13 place Kant in the latter posture: the officers remain human beings whose bodies are possessed, and Kant knows the law forbids killing them. (a) falsely treats M'Naghten wrongness as uniformly legal; the split between moral and legal formulations is recognized in *Clark v. Arizona* and acknowledged in *Kahler*. (b) misapplies the "nature and quality" prong. That prong addresses the physical nature of the act (e.g., strangling vs. squeezing a grapefruit), not the target's moral identity; Kant knew he was physically stabbing a living human being and therefore understood the act's nature and quality. (c) imports the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act into a state-law analysis (Kant is charged in state court, not federal), fabricates a "dangerousness to the public" element that the Insanity Defense Reform Act does not contain, and incorrectly describes the Act's operation. (d) inverts *Kahler*, which held that due process does *not* require any particular insanity formulation.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); *Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — M'Naghten wrongness (moral vs. legal), Ch 23, Refinement `mnaghten-wrongness`. Rule regarding the jurisdictional split on moral vs. legal wrongness and the holding in Kahler v. Kansas (Refinement `kahler-constitutional-floor`) paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q18.** Assume that Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia, in active psychotic episode) at the time of the Hand stabbing. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited, and Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer demonstrates knowledge that the stabbing was illegal.

(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because he believed Officer Hand was a demon rather than a human officer; that mistaken belief about the act's object satisfies the test regardless of whether Kant understood the act's wrongfulness.

(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because 18 U.S.C. § 17 requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that dangerousness showing, is insufficient to meet the federal standard.

(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to an acquittal because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a floor for insanity defenses.

(e) Kant likely satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because Dr. Singer's testimony established that Kant's delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong — he believed killing the possessed human host was morally required to defeat the demonic attack; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because Dr. Singer's testimony also established that Kant understood the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied only a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)
**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong. Jurisdictions split between legal and moral formulations of wrongfulness, and the two formulations generate different results here. Under a moral formulation, Dr. Singer's testimony that Kant believed killing the possessed host was morally required to defeat a demonic attack (Fact 13) goes directly to the prong: Kant could not appreciate the act's moral wrongness. Under a legal formulation, Fact 13 establishes the opposite result — Kant understood that the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted. The doctrinal pivot is whether the delusion replaces the object of the act (killing a pure demon, to which no homicide statute applies) or merely rewrites the moral calculus (still killing a human, still legally prohibited, but subjectively justified by a demonic-possession narrative). Facts 12–13 place Kant in the latter posture: the officers remain human beings whose bodies are possessed, and Kant knows the law forbids killing them. (a) falsely treats M'Naghten wrongness as uniformly legal; the split between moral and legal formulations is recognized in *Clark v. Arizona* and acknowledged in *Kahler*. (b) misapplies the "nature and quality" prong. That prong addresses the physical nature of the act (e.g., strangling vs. squeezing a grapefruit), not the target's moral identity; Kant knew he was physically stabbing a living human being and therefore understood the act's nature and quality. (c) imports the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act into a state-law analysis (Kant is charged in state court, not federal), fabricates a "dangerousness to the public" element that the Insanity Defense Reform Act does not contain, and incorrectly describes the Act's operation. (d) inverts *Kahler*, which held that due process does *not* require any particular insanity formulation.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); *Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).
