check 1: pass
check 2: pass (assuming the missing master facts clarify the delusion; otherwise, (b) is a credible distractor if Kant believed the officer was a pure non-human demon)
check 3: pass
check 4: fail. The stem references "the Hand stabbing," "Dr. Singer's testimony," "Facts 12-13," and the explanation relies on Kant being charged in state court. These facts are missing from the question text itself. As a standalone question, it lacks the necessary facts to eliminate (b).

### Edge Case Audit

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q18.** Assume that Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia, in active psychotic episode) at the time of the Hand stabbing. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited, and Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer demonstrates knowledge that the stabbing was illegal.

(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because he believed Officer Hand was a demon rather than a human officer; that mistaken belief about the act's object satisfies the test regardless of whether Kant understood the act's wrongfulness.

(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because 18 U.S.C. § 17 requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that dangerousness showing, is insufficient to meet the federal standard.

(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to an acquittal because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a floor for insanity defenses.

(e) Kant likely satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because Dr. Singer's testimony established that Kant's delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong — he believed killing the possessed human host was morally required to defeat the demonic attack; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because Dr. Singer's testimony also established that Kant understood the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied only a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted.

**Answer:** (e)
**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong. Jurisdictions split between legal and moral formulations of wrongfulness, and the two formulations generate different results here. Under a moral formulation, Dr. Singer's testimony that Kant believed killing the possessed host was morally required to defeat a demonic attack (Fact 13) goes directly to the prong: Kant could not appreciate the act's moral wrongness. Under a legal formulation, Fact 13 establishes the opposite result — Kant understood that the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted. The doctrinal pivot is whether the delusion replaces the object of the act (killing a pure demon, to which no homicide statute applies) or merely rewrites the moral calculus (still killing a human, still legally prohibited, but subjectively justified by a demonic-possession narrative). Facts 12–13 place Kant in the latter posture: the officers remain human beings whose bodies are possessed, and Kant knows the law forbids killing them. (a) falsely treats M'Naghten wrongness as uniformly legal; the split between moral and legal formulations is recognized in *Clark v. Arizona* and acknowledged in *Kahler*. (b) misapplies the "nature and quality" prong. That prong addresses the physical nature of the act (e.g., strangling vs. squeezing a grapefruit), not the target's moral identity; Kant knew he was physically stabbing a living human being and therefore understood the act's nature and quality. (c) imports the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act into a state-law analysis (Kant is charged in state court, not federal), fabricates a "dangerousness to the public" element that the Insanity Defense Reform Act does not contain, and incorrectly describes the Act's operation. (d) inverts *Kahler*, which held that due process does *not* require any particular insanity formulation.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); *Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the legal interpretation of M’Naghten’s second prong, "wrong" means "contrary to the law of the land." In many jurisdictions, evidence that a defendant hid from the police or resisted arrest is conclusive proof that the defendant appreciated the illegality of their actions. Because Kant violently resisted a uniformed officer, he demonstrated a behavioral realization that his conduct was subject to legal sanction. Therefore, if a jurisdiction adopts the legal-wrongfulness standard, Kant categorically fails to meet the insanity threshold regardless of his subjective moral delusions.

(b) Argument-for: The "nature and quality" prong of M'Naghten is a cognitive test regarding the physical act. If Kant's delusion was so profound that he believed he was stabbing a "demon" (a supernatural entity) rather than a "human officer," he did not understand the physical nature and quality of his act as a homicide. Under this view, the mistake of fact regarding the victim’s identity is so fundamental that it satisfies the first prong of M'Naghten, rendering an inquiry into the "wrongness" prong unnecessary for acquittal.

(c) Argument-for: Following the Hinckley acquittal, the federal government enacted 18 U.S.C. § 17 to significantly narrow the insanity defense. This statute requires that the defendant suffer from a "severe" mental disease. An advocate could argue that under this rigorous federal standard, the defense must go beyond a clinical diagnosis and prove that the disease manifested in a way that creates a specific, identifiable danger to the public to justify the high "clear and convincing" burden of proof. Because the facts do not explicitly link Kant's schizophrenia to a documented history of public dangerousness, he fails the federal standard.

(d) Argument-for: The Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* (2020) engaged in a deep historical analysis of the insanity defense. A proponent of this option would argue that the "moral wrongness" prong of M'Naghten is so deeply rooted in the Anglo-American legal tradition that it forms a "fundamental principle of justice" protected by the Due Process Clause. Under this reading, while states have some flexibility in phrasing, they are constitutionally prohibited from abolishing the core M'Naghten requirement that a defendant must be able to distinguish right from wrong.

(e) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the jurisdictional split regarding "wrongness." In jurisdictions following the "moral" formulation (like the original M’Naghten judges intended), Kant is insane because his delusion compelled him to believe the act was a moral necessity. However, in jurisdictions following the "legal" formulation (like California or Arizona), Kant is sane because he remained cognitively aware that society and the law forbade the act of killing, even if he felt subjectively justified by a divine or demonic command.

Head-to-head: (e) is the most legally accurate because it accounts for the nuanced split in M'Naghten jurisdictions between legal and moral wrongfulness, which was a central point of discussion in both *Clark v. Arizona* and *Kahler v. Kansas*. (a) is incorrect because it uses the word "uniformly," ignoring the significant number of jurisdictions that use a moral wrongfulness standard. (b) conflates the two prongs; believing a human is a demon is typically treated under the "wrongness" prong (deific decree/moral delusion) rather than the "nature and quality" prong, which usually requires a failure to understand the physical act (e.g., thinking a throat is a lemon). (c) is incorrect because 18 U.S.C. § 17 contains no "danger to the public" element and would only apply in federal court. (d) is a direct inversion of the holding in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that the Due Process Clause does not require any specific insanity test.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited" — wrong because many jurisdictions (and the original M'Naghten Case) allow for a moral wrongness standard.
- (b): "satisfies M'Naghten's 'nature and quality' prong because he believed Officer Hand was a demon" — wrong because nature/quality refers to the physical nature of the act (stabbing vs. something else), not the victim's moral identity.
- (c): "18 U.S.C. § 17 requires... clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public" — wrong because the statute has no "public danger" requirement for the insanity defense itself.
- (d): "Kahler v. Kansas held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong" — wrong because Kahler held the exact opposite, that states can constitutionally abolish the "moral wrongness" prong.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q18.** Assume that Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia, in active psychotic episode) at the time of the Hand stabbing. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited, and Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer demonstrates knowledge that the stabbing was illegal.

(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because he believed Officer Hand was a demon rather than a human officer; that mistaken belief about the act's object satisfies the test regardless of whether Kant understood the act's wrongfulness.

(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because 18 U.S.C. § 17 requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that dangerousness showing, is insufficient to meet the federal standard.

(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to an acquittal because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a floor for insanity defenses.

(e) Kant likely satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because Dr. Singer's testimony established that Kant's delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong — he believed killing the possessed human host was morally required to defeat the demonic attack; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because Dr. Singer's testimony also established that Kant understood the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied only a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)
**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong. Jurisdictions split between legal and moral formulations of wrongfulness, and the two formulations generate different results here. Under a moral formulation, Dr. Singer's testimony that Kant believed killing the possessed host was morally required to defeat a demonic attack (Fact 13) goes directly to the prong: Kant could not appreciate the act's moral wrongness. Under a legal formulation, Fact 13 establishes the opposite result — Kant understood that the penal code prohibited killing a human being, and his delusion supplied a moral-override belief rather than a belief that the conduct was legally permitted. The doctrinal pivot is whether the delusion replaces the object of the act (killing a pure demon, to which no homicide statute applies) or merely rewrites the moral calculus (still killing a human, still legally prohibited, but subjectively justified by a demonic-possession narrative). Facts 12–13 place Kant in the latter posture: the officers remain human beings whose bodies are possessed, and Kant knows the law forbids killing them. (a) falsely treats M'Naghten wrongness as uniformly legal; the split between moral and legal formulations is recognized in *Clark v. Arizona* and acknowledged in *Kahler*. (b) misapplies the "nature and quality" prong. (c) imports the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act into a state-law analysis (Kant is charged in state court, not federal), fabricates a "dangerousness to the public" element that the Insanity Defense Reform Act does not contain, and incorrectly describes the Act's operation. (d) inverts *Kahler*, which held that due process does *not* require any particular insanity formulation.

## Q19

### Grounding

**Q19.** Assume that Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect" and that Kant is tried in state court in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Model Penal Code's § 4.01 insanity test. The defense memorandum asks you to compare how Kant's defense will fare under Model Penal Code § 4.01 in this state prosecution with how the same evidence would fare under the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) if Kant had instead been charged federally on parallel facts. Based on Dr. Laplace's evaluation, which of the following is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would prevail because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984, and Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" tracks that statutory language.

(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs — he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of the stabbing and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law — and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable.

(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct, and Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is insufficient because substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires.

(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence, and Dr. Laplace's testimony satisfies that standard uniformly across jurisdictions.

(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea; the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)
**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack *substantial capacity* (not total inability) either to appreciate criminality/wrongfulness or to conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs per Dr. Laplace. The Insanity Defense Reform Act, enacted in 1984 after Hinckley, tightened the federal standard: it requires "severe" mental disease, dropped the volitional prong entirely, and shifted the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states that the Insanity Defense Reform Act adopted the Model Penal Code volitional prong; the Insanity Defense Reform Act *eliminated* the volitional prong after Hinckley. (c) falsely elevates the Model Penal Code degree requirement to total incapacity; Model Penal Code § 4.01 requires only "substantial capacity" — a key softening from M'Naghten. (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the Insanity Defense Reform Act requires clear and convincing evidence, a higher standard than preponderance. (e) inverts *Kahler*, which permitted states to abolish the insanity defense but did not require or constitutionalize that choice, and did not invalidate any Model Penal Code prong.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC § 4.01 vs. Federal IDRA, Ch 23, Refinement `mpc-appreciate-criminality`, `mpc-conform-conduct`, `federal-idra-requirements`, and `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q19.** Assume that Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect" and that Kant is tried in state court in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Model Penal Code's § 4.01 insanity test. The defense memorandum asks you to compare how Kant's defense will fare under Model Penal Code § 4.01 in this state prosecution with how the same evidence would fare under the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) if Kant had instead been charged federally on parallel facts. Based on Dr. Laplace's evaluation, which of the following is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would prevail because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984, and Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" tracks that statutory language.

(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs — he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of the stabbing and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law — and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->

(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct, and Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is insufficient because substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires.

(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence, and Dr. Laplace's testimony satisfies that standard uniformly across jurisdictions.

(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea; the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)
**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack *substantial capacity* (not total inability) either to appreciate criminality/wrongfulness or to conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs per Dr. Laplace. The Insanity Defense Reform Act, enacted in 1984 after Hinckley, tightened the federal standard: it requires "severe" mental disease, dropped the volitional prong entirely, and shifted the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states that the Insanity Defense Reform Act adopted the Model Penal Code volitional prong; the Insanity Defense Reform Act *eliminated* the volitional prong after Hinckley. (c) falsely elevates the Model Penal Code degree requirement to total incapacity; Model Penal Code § 4.01 requires only "substantial capacity" — a key softening from M'Naghten. (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the Insanity Defense Reform Act requires clear and convincing evidence, a higher standard than preponderance. (e) inverts *Kahler*, which permitted states to abolish the insanity defense but did not require or constitutionalize that choice, and did not invalidate any Model Penal Code prong.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q19.** Assume that Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect" and that Kant is tried in state court in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Model Penal Code's § 4.01 insanity test. The defense memorandum asks you to compare how Kant's defense will fare under Model Penal Code § 4.01 in this state prosecution with how the same evidence would fare under the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) if Kant had instead been charged federally on parallel facts. Based on Dr. Laplace's evaluation, which of the following is most accurate?

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q19.** Assume that Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect" and that Kant is tried in state court in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Model Penal Code's § 4.01 insanity test. The defense memorandum asks you to compare how Kant's defense will fare under Model Penal Code § 4.01 in this state prosecution with how the same evidence would fare under the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) if Kant had instead been charged federally on parallel facts. Based on Dr. Laplace's evaluation, which of the following is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would prevail because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984, and Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" tracks that statutory language.

(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs — he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of the stabbing and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law — and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable.

(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct, and Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is insufficient because substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires.

(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence, and Dr. Laplace's testimony satisfies that standard uniformly across jurisdictions.

(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea; the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)
**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack *substantial capacity* (not total inability) either to appreciate criminality/wrongfulness or to conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs per Dr. Laplace. The Insanity Defense Reform Act, enacted in 1984 after Hinckley, tightened the federal standard: it requires "severe" mental disease, dropped the volitional prong entirely, and shifted the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states that the Insanity Defense Reform Act adopted the Model Penal Code volitional prong; the Insanity Defense Reform Act *eliminated* the volitional prong after Hinckley. (c) falsely elevates the Model Penal Code degree requirement to total incapacity; Model Penal Code § 4.01 requires only "substantial capacity" — a key softening from M'Naghten. (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the Insanity Defense Reform Act requires clear and convincing evidence, a higher standard than preponderance. (e) inverts *Kahler*, which permitted states to abolish the insanity defense but did not require or constitutionalize that choice, and did not invalidate any Model Penal Code prong.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) of 1984 was a comprehensive overhaul intended to bring federal law into the modern era of psychological understanding. Because the Model Penal Code was the prevailing modern standard at the time, it is logical to assume the federal government adopted its most innovative feature—the volitional prong—to account for defendants who understand their actions but cannot control them. Under this view, Dr. Laplace’s specific finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct" would be the exact type of evidence the 1984 statute was designed to encompass.

(b) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the structural differences between the MPC and the federal IDRA. The MPC § 4.01 uses a "substantial capacity" standard, which is more lenient than the "total" incapacity found in older tests, and it includes both a cognitive prong (appreciation of wrongfulness) and a volitional prong (conformity of conduct). In contrast, the IDRA, passed in the wake of the Hinckley acquittal, deliberately narrowed the federal defense by requiring "severity," removing the volitional prong entirely, and raising the burden of proof to clear and convincing evidence.

(c) Argument-for: One could argue that the term "substantial" in the Model Penal Code creates a high threshold that is functionally equivalent to total incapacity in practice. If a defendant merely has "substantial impairment," a skeptical court might rule that they still possess enough residual capacity to fail the "substantial capacity" test. Under this interpretation, the MPC requires a near-total breakdown of the mental faculties, and Dr. Laplace’s use of the word "impairment" suggests a level of functioning that does not meet the Code's rigorous demand for a lack of "substantial capacity."

(d) Argument-for: In the American legal system, the preponderance of the evidence is the default standard for affirmative defenses unless otherwise specified. A student might argue that for the sake of judicial economy and constitutional fairness, a uniform standard of proof exists across jurisdictions. Since Dr. Laplace’s testimony provides a clinical basis for the insanity claim, it would meet this preponderance threshold in both state (MPC) and federal (IDRA) courts, as requiring "clear and convincing" evidence would place an unconstitutional burden on a mentally ill defendant.

(e) Argument-for: In *Kahler v. Kansas*, the Supreme Court addressed whether a state must provide an insanity defense based on moral wrongfulness. One could interpret the Court's holding as a broad grant of power to states to restrict the insanity defense to the narrowest possible grounds—the negation of *mens rea*. Under this reading, any defense that allows for acquittal despite the presence of *mens rea* (such as the MPC's volitional prong) is constitutionally suspect. Therefore, following *Kahler*, the volitional prong is effectively dead in every jurisdiction as it exceeds the constitutional minimum.

Head-to-head: (b) is the only option that accurately reflects the statutory text and historical context of both the MPC and the federal IDRA. (a) is factually incorrect regarding the 1984 federal change, which was a restrictive move, not an expansion. (c) misinterprets "substantial capacity" as "total incapacity," whereas the MPC specifically chose "substantial" to avoid the "all-or-nothing" pitfall of M'Naghten. (d) fails because the IDRA explicitly raised the federal burden to clear and convincing evidence, creating a lack of uniformity. (e) misreads *Kahler*, which held that states *may* limit the insanity defense, not that they *must* or that the MPC prongs are unconstitutional. (b) remains the strongest because it correctly identifies the multi-layered tightening of the federal standard (severity, cognitive-only, higher burden).

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because the IDRA explicitly abolished the volitional prong in federal courts.
- (c): "substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires" — wrong because the MPC § 4.01 explicitly rejected the total incapacity standard in favor of "substantial capacity."
- (d): "the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence... uniformly across jurisdictions" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17(b) explicitly requires "clear and convincing evidence" for federal cases.
- (e): "The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by Kahler v. Kansas... no longer available in any jurisdiction" — wrong because Kahler held that the Due Process Clause does *not* require any specific insanity test, leaving jurisdictions free to keep or adopt the volitional prong.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q19.** Assume that Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect" and that Kant is tried in state court in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Model Penal Code's § 4.01 insanity test. The defense memorandum asks you to compare how Kant's defense will fare under Model Penal Code § 4.01 in this state prosecution with how the same evidence would fare under the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) if Kant had instead been charged federally on parallel facts. Based on Dr. Laplace's evaluation, which of the following is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would prevail because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984, and Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" tracks that statutory language.

(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs — he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of the stabbing and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law — and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->

(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct, and Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is insufficient because substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires.

(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence, and Dr. Laplace's testimony satisfies that standard uniformly across jurisdictions.

(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea; the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)
**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack *substantial capacity* (not total inability) either to appreciate criminality/wrongfulness or to conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs per Dr. Laplace. The Insanity Defense Reform Act, enacted in 1984 after Hinckley, tightened the federal standard: it requires "severe" mental disease, dropped the volitional prong entirely, and shifted the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states that the Insanity Defense Reform Act adopted the Model Penal Code volitional prong; the Insanity Defense Reform Act *eliminated* the volitional prong after Hinckley. (c) falsely elevates the Model Penal Code degree requirement to total incapacity; Model Penal Code § 4.01 requires only "substantial capacity" — a key softening from M'Naghten. (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the Insanity Defense Reform Act requires clear and convincing evidence, a higher standard than preponderance. (e) inverts *Kahler*, which permitted states to abolish the insanity defense but did not require or constitutionalize that choice, and did not invalidate any Model Penal Code prong.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under federal common law pre-1984, many federal circuits had adopted the MPC test, which included the volitional prong. A student could argue that if the Insanity Defense Reform Act merely codified the prevailing federal standards of the time, it might have retained the "unable to conform" language. Thus, Dr. Laplace's finding of Kant's inability to conform would squarely satisfy this prong and ensure Kant prevails.
(b) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the exact statutory elements of both tests. The MPC § 4.01 requires only "substantial capacity" to appreciate criminality or conform conduct to law, and is satisfied in the disjunctive. The IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease, an absolute inability ("unable") to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, abolishes the volitional prong entirely, and places the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that insanity is historically an all-or-nothing defense demanding total incapacity, as established by M'Naghten. If one believes the MPC merely modernized the phrasing without lowering the threshold, "substantial impairment" would be treated as a diminished capacity concept. Therefore, establishing insanity would require demonstrating the defendant was wholly "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct.
(d) Argument-for: A student could point out that insanity is universally an affirmative defense for which the defendant bears the burden of proof. Because the standard of proof for defendants in criminal cases is almost always a preponderance of the evidence (to avoid violating Due Process), the student could deduce that both the MPC and IDRA must apply the preponderance standard, rendering Laplace's testimony uniformly sufficient.
(e) Argument-for: A student could interpret *Kahler v. Kansas* as fundamentally altering the constitutional landscape of the insanity defense. Because *Kahler* held that the Constitution does not mandate an insanity defense extending beyond the negation of mens rea, one could extrapolate that the Court implicitly found freestanding volitional prongs to be constitutionally suspect or invalid, rendering the MPC's volitional prong no longer available.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the only legally defensible answer, correctly mapping the specific statutory and textual differences between the MPC and the IDRA. Option (a) fails because the IDRA explicitly eliminated, rather than adopted, the volitional prong following the Hinckley acquittal. Option (c) is incorrect because the MPC's hallmark innovation was replacing the strict "total incapacity" standard of M'Naghten with the softer "substantial capacity" threshold. Option (d) fails because the IDRA explicitly sets the federal burden of proof at clear and convincing evidence, not a preponderance. Option (e) completely misstates the holding of *Kahler*, which ruled that states are permitted to abolish the moral-incapacity insanity defense, not that they are required to do so. Every distractor contains a stark, easily identifiable false claim of black-letter law.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17 explicitly eliminated the volitional prong.
- (c): "falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires" — wrong because MPC § 4.01 expressly uses the relaxed "lacks substantial capacity" standard, not total incapacity.
- (d): "Under both... the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance" — wrong because the IDRA requires proof by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by Kahler v. Kansas... the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction" — wrong because *Kahler* merely held that states are not constitutionally required to adopt a volitional or moral-incapacity test, leaving jurisdictions completely free to retain the MPC volitional prong.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q20

### Grounding

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — NGRI civil commitment and Mutina instruction, Ch 23, Refinement `ngri-civil-commitment` and `mutina-instruction`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that an acquittal, regardless of the underlying reason, triggers the full protection of the Double Jeopardy Clause. Under this view, once a jury returns a "not guilty" verdict, the state's criminal jurisdiction over the defendant ends immediately. Any further detention must rely on the state's parens patriae or police powers through standard civil commitment procedures, which, under Addington v. Texas, require clear and convincing evidence. Therefore, the defendant cannot be held under the umbrella of the criminal case and must be released unless separate civil proceedings are already complete.

(b) Argument-for: This option appeals to principles of proportionality and due process. A student might argue that it is fundamentally unfair to detain a person in a psychiatric facility for longer than they could have been imprisoned for the same conduct. While the Supreme Court in Jones v. United States allowed for longer commitment, a student might argue that state constitutions or modern interpretations of the Due Process Clause require a "ceiling" on commitment duration. Once the maximum penal sentence has elapsed, the state’s interest in "quasi-punitive" detention ends, necessitating release regardless of clinical status unless the state meets the higher burden of general civil commitment.

(c) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the shift toward clinical expertise in modern mental health law. A student could posit that because the determination of "dangerousness" and "recovery" is a medical rather than a legal judgment, the Constitution requires that specialized bodies (like Psychiatric Security Review Boards) manage the release process. By removing these decisions from the potentially biased or politically pressured judiciary and granting "exclusive authority" to boards of professionals, the system ensures that the acquittee’s liberty is determined by clinical reality, making any judicial override a violation of the medical-standard requirement.

(d) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the holding of Commonwealth v. Mutina, which recognized that jurors often labor under the misconception that NGRI results in immediate release. This misconception can lead to "fear-based" convictions. Moreover, it accurately reflects the holding in Jones v. United States, which permits automatic and potentially indefinite commitment of NGRI acquittees because the verdict itself establishes both mental illness and dangerousness (through the commission of the act), often leading to stays that exceed the maximum criminal sentence.

(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that the Supreme Court's decision in Kahler v. Kansas emphasized the broad discretion states have in shaping their insanity defenses, while reinforcing the traditional rule that juries should not be informed of the consequences of their verdicts. Under this interpretation, a Mutina-style instruction "improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences," which is generally prohibited in federal and many state courts (Shannon v. United States). If Kahler is read as strictly separating the jury's role as fact-finder from the dispositional consequences, then providing such instructions would constitute reversible error.

Head-to-head: (d) is the strongest answer because it accurately synthesizes the empirical reality of NGRI (long-term commitment) with the specific legal doctrine of the Mutina instruction. (a) and (b) are directly contradicted by SCOTUS in Jones v. United States, which held that NGRI acquittees are not entitled to immediate release and can be held longer than their maximum hypothetical sentence. (c) fails because the PSRB model is used in only a handful of states; most jurisdictions maintain judicial control over release. (e) is incorrect because Kahler v. Kansas dealt with the substantive elements of the insanity defense (mens rea approach vs. moral incapacity), not jury instructions regarding commitment consequences.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal... with the defendant released pending their outcome." — wrong because Jones v. United States permits automatic commitment and distinguishes NGRI from regular acquittals.
- (b): "Commitment duration... is capped at the maximum sentence... must be released regardless of clinical status." — wrong because under Jones, duration is tied to clinical status (illness and dangerousness) and can exceed the maximum sentence.
- (c): "In every jurisdiction... a psychiatric security review board... has exclusive authority... and courts are categorically barred from overriding..." — wrong because PSRBs are a minority model (e.g., Oregon) and most jurisdictions vest final authority in judges.
- (e): "The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in Kahler v. Kansas... its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error." — wrong because Kahler did not address jury instructions on consequences, and many jurisdictions continue to use or require such instructions.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: An argument for (a) relies on the basic constitutional premise that an acquittal terminates criminal jurisdiction. Because the Double Jeopardy Clause prohibits punishing someone who has been acquitted, the state loses its penal authority over an NGRI acquittee. Under this view, the state must treat the acquittee exactly like any other civilian facing civil commitment under *O'Connor v. Donaldson*, meaning they cannot be automatically confined under criminal statutes and must be released pending a separate civil hearing.
(b) Argument-for: An argument for (b) relies on principles of proportionality and due process. Because an NGRI acquittee is technically not legally culpable, it would violate equal protection to hold them for a period longer than a fully culpable convicted criminal would serve for the same act. Therefore, the maximum hypothetical criminal sentence serves as a hard constitutional cap on confinement, requiring release or a transition to standard civil commitment once that term expires.
(c) Argument-for: An argument for (c) focuses on the medicalized nature of the insanity defense. Because the NGRI verdict is predicated on a mental disease or defect, the determination of when sanity is restored is a purely clinical question. Consequently, specialized Psychiatric Security Review Boards (PSRBs) composed of mental health professionals are universally recognized as the sole competent authorities to manage release, barring lay judges from making specialized medical determinations.
(d) Argument-for: An argument for (d) is grounded in both Supreme Court precedent (*Jones v. United States*) and trial practice. *Jones* established that automatic, indefinite commitment of an NGRI acquittee is constitutional and need not be capped by the hypothetical prison sentence. Because juries often operate under the misconception that NGRI means immediate release—prompting them to convict out of fear for public safety—instructions like the Mutina instruction are given to apprise the jury of the reality of secure commitment, allowing them to make a verdict on the merits.
(e) Argument-for: An argument for (e) relies on the traditional rule that juries are fact-finders who should not be influenced by the post-verdict consequences of their decisions (as seen in *Shannon v. United States*). Introducing instructions about civil commitment inappropriately biases the jury by injecting sentencing-like outcomes into their deliberations. One could argue the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* addressed the due process limits of the insanity defense and implicitly prohibited mechanisms that distract the jury from the core issue of moral culpability.

Head-to-head: (d) correctly states the legal holding of *Jones v. United States* regarding indefinite commitment and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. The distractors fail because they rely on explicit, falsifiable falsehoods: (a) directly contradicts *Jones* by mandating immediate release; (b) fabricates a constitutional sentence cap that *Jones* expressly rejected; (c) relies on an absolute "every jurisdiction" claim for a PSRB model that only a minority of states actually use; and (e) fabricates a holding for *Kahler v. Kansas*, which dealt with substantive due process and the abolition of the insanity defense, not jury instructions.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "results in the defendant's immediate release... must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome" — wrong because *Jones v. United States* held that automatic commitment of NGRI acquittees without a separate civil hearing is constitutional.
- (b): "capped at the maximum sentence... must be released regardless of clinical status" — wrong because the Supreme Court explicitly ruled that NGRI commitment is tied to clinical status (dangerousness and mental illness) and can exceed the maximum hypothetical sentence.
- (c): "In every jurisdiction... exclusive authority" — wrong because only a small minority of states use the PSRB model; most jurisdictions leave release decisions to judges.
- (e): "held unconstitutional in Kahler v. Kansas... its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error" — wrong because *Kahler* evaluated whether a state could abolish the moral-wrongness prong of the insanity defense, not the constitutionality of jury instructions regarding commitment.

Recommended fix: none
-->
